A tincture brighter, than they thence receive.
VIII.
Till the Day come, my Love, the Bridal-Day,
For which thou less than I do long;
Till the Nights shadows swiftly flee away,
A while I Will leave Thee, a little while be gone:
To the Hills of Myrrh, and Frankincense I Will go,
And fetch the Morning, as it 'gins to blow.
IX.
Yet (ah!) I cannot leave Thee, Love, thus soon,
My Love thus soon I cannot leave;
Our Flocks may gaze, for it is yet scarce High-noon,
And we, till Folding time, the Hours deceive.
Thou art all fair, my Love, all fair Thou art,
And first I Will die, e're from Thee thus depart.
X.
Rather a while, my Love, let 'us hand in hand,
To Liban walk, and Amana;
Shenir, and Hermon, which large views command,
And thence see how our Lambs do feed and play,
Whether unscar'd they from the Lions rest,
Or Leopards paws, and where the' next Folding's best.
XI.
They 'are safe, my SPOUSE, they' are safe, but (Oh) my heart,
SISTER, my Heart (Oh) where is it gone?
For gone it is, I know, by the' pleasing smart;
Welfare poor Heart, that is from its Master flown!
But this one glance, one Eyes bright glance has done:
Who Is proof for both, if thus Thou wound'st with One?
XII.
Yet as Thou' have taken my Heart, so keep it fast!
A single Chain is strong enough,
One link of the Chain, with which thy Neck is graced,
To hold, fast hold a never starting Love;
My SPOUSE, my SISTER, I Am Thy Captive made,
Bind him, nor of Thy Priso'ner be afraid!
XIII.
A willing Captive, Prisoner of Thy love,
Which guess how strong, and fair it is;
So strong 't has drawn me from my Throne above,
And truly hides a God in this disguise:
So fair, that Thee no less to Heaven 't shall raise,
To Crown Thy Maker with Immortal Bays.
XIV.
But this, till to' Heaven we come, I Will let alone,
Nor can Thou understand it yet;
Wherefore Thy Love by things to Thee well known,
As thou cans^ bear, I will before Thee set;
To Wine compare 't, tho better it is than Wine,
And all Thy Odours are of race Divine.
XV.
Thy Lips, my SPOUSE, drop as the Honeycomb,
Honey and Milk are in Thy Tongue;
Some drops for Susten'ance, and for Medicine some,
The Weak to nourish, and confirm the Strong:
Like Libanus, Thy perfumed Garments smell,
Liban, that does all Mounts, for Sweets excel.
XVI.
All, but that Garden, in the holy Mount,
Where I design three Nights to lie,
In Spices wrapped, as Prophets shall recount,
And notice give the World, when the' time draws nigh:
Yet such a Garden, and so sweet art Thou,
My SPOUSE, my SISTER, when Thy Spices blow.
XVII.
A Garden fenced, and all enclosed around,
Locked up, and double is the Key,
With a quick Spring, that waters the' holy Ground,
And all its parching Fervors does allay;
But so sealed up, the curious passer by
With Foot never soild, never saw it with his Eye.
XVIII.
To none but its own sacred Plants it slows,
Making the place a Paradise,
Where nothing noxious, or forbidden grows,
Nothing, but what will make one truly wise:
Or make, or please, or truly keep one so;
For all there planted, as its Trees do grow.
XIX.
The Pomgranate is there, and there the Date,
The Spikenard, and the Cypress Tree,
Spikenard and Saffron, and hi'ther brought of late,
The sweet-Cane, Cinamom, and all sorts that be
Of Frankincense, Myrrh, Aloes, and a Train
Of Princely Spices, wont in the East to Reign.
XX.
There Reign they, but here serve my Love, and me,
Whose Fountain all their drought supplies,
And slows so largely, constant, and so free,
The whole World may have for its necessities:
Jordan that sacred, and perpetual Flood,
Had not more streams, when like the Sea on heaps it stood.

SPONSA. Surge Aquilo and veni Auster.
I.
FATHER Winds, Sponsa.
that gently ride,
On downy Feathers, through the Sky,
You, that in the cold North abide,
And Southern Gales, which cold defy!
II.
North Wind awake, and Thou, O South,
And gently on my Garden blow;
Blow, gentle Winds, with different Mouth,
That all its various scents may slow!
III.
Then on your Wings the Perfumes take,
And bear them with you gently Home;
More grateful your return they will make,
And the young Winds wonder whence you come.
IV.
But why, O Winds, first call I you,
Let my BELOVED rather come!
More than your Gales his Breath can do,
Not show, but make my best Perfume.
V.
Why comes he not? Ah! where is the Let?
Now that his Garden's in its prime,
Now that his Fruits are fit to eat,
And may be worse another time?

V.
CATASTASIS.
SPONSUS. SPONSA. CHORUS. CAP. 5.
SPONSUS. Veni in Hortum meum Soror.
I.
I HEARD Thee call, Sponsus.
my Sister, my SPOUSE,
I heard Thee call, and I came away;
To my Garden I came, and as Lovers use,
Have gathered each Flower in my Walk that lay;
My Myrrh, and my Spices, and all that is rare,
And thus to my Garden I Will often repair.
II.
So pleased I was with each choice delight,
And delights such as Thine I but rarely meet;
That, scarce knowing how, I forgot my self quite,
Nor took time enough to choose only the sweet;
My Honey and Honeycomb at once devoured,
And with my Wine, Milk into the Bowl poured.
III.
Cheer up, my Friends, and eat your fill,
Cheer up, and eat, here is store enough,
Thanks to my bounteous Love,
And drink, drink plenteously, what each one will!
"Who fears excess, or fondly thinks to spare,
"Disparages that Heaven, whose Guests we are.

SPONSA. Ego dormio, and cor meum vigilat.
ALL as I slept, Sponsa.
but with my waking mind,
(My Heart slept not, tho sleep my Eyes did bind,
And on my Temples his soft Fillet ti'd,
But as I slept and) dead to all beside,
Lived to my LOVE, a Voice I seemed to hear,
A shrilling Voice, and pitteous, pierced my ear;
A Voice well known, 'twas my BELOVED's Voice,
And at the Door he made the appointed Noise,
Knocking, and calling, Why comes not my Love?
"Open my SISTER, my 'Undefil'd, my Dove,
"Open, I pray Thee, nor be so unkind,
"Thus to treat him, who is come thy Love to find;
"A long, and weary Journey come in the Night,
"Weary and wet, by the Moon's dusky Light.
"Lo! how my Head with the moist Dew is filled,
"And the Nights drops through all my Curls distilled!
"And art Thou come, I flatteringly replied,
"But why, my Dear, so late, without a Guide?
"I fear it much, but (ah!) I Am now in Bed;
"And many a sad distrust nights horrors breed,
"He're in the dark, in the dark here all alone;
"My Vest's put off, How shall I put it on?
"My Feet are washed too from the Days foul soil,
"Nor with new sullies would I them defile.
"O, how I tremble, and would gladly rise,
"But dare not, (Oh!) I dare not, LOVE how dark it is!
I spoke, but 'twas excuse: To be denied,
Unwilling he, still for admission cried.
Drawing the Latch, and lifting with his Hand
The easy Door; grieving he thus should stand,
My Heart was in me moved, and I began
To pity him, and my' unjust self disdain;
At his so meek attendance did relent,
And of my own unlovelike words repent.
So up I rose in haste, and let him in,
Who was my LOVE, and had so patient been.
I rose, and to the Door like Lightning flew,
To the very place, where he the Latch first drew.
My Hands dropped Myrrh, which on the Door he left,
Pure Myrrh, in eve'ry chink of th'Door, and cleft,
Myrrh on the Lock, in every ward was Myrrh,
And Myrrh, where every busy Finger I could stir.
But opening, -- Woe's me! he was gone;
Himself, my dearest LOVE, had thence withdrawn.
My Soul was gone too, roused as I lay,
And heard him speak, but with him fled away.
So pensive, all about I for him sought,
Sought curiously about, but found him not:
Called him by Name, begged his return to have,
I called, and sought, but he no answer gave.
Nor hear, nor find him could I, but they found me,
They, who the Warders of the City be:
Unlike in Court'sie, tho alike in Name
Those Just ones, to 'whom in my first search I came,
Who let me pass unwrong'd; ^ ese barba'rously
Struck, and did more than strike, have wounded me:
And though by place, and office bound to look
No' assaults be made, my torn Vail rudely took.
But you, fair Daughters of Jerusalem,
From whom much better things to hope I seem,
Worthy your selves, and worthy your bright stem.
If you chance my Love to find,
Virgins, be to' a Virgin kind!
If you chance my LOVE to meet,
Virgins, be to a Virgin sweet!
Sweet, and kind so Love to y'ou be,
As you are kind, and sweet to me!
I charge you, I charge you by all that is dear,
My LOVE to inform, that his Wounds I do bear,
Sick to death with his Love, without his care.

CHORUS Filiarum Hierosolymitanarum. Qualis est Dilectus tuus.
FAIREST of Women, Chorus.
if we find
Thy LOVE, we'll to Thy suit be kind.
Fairest of Women, if we meet
Thy LOVE, we'll be as Thou art, sweet.
(Sweet, and kind, so Love to us be
As we are kind and sweet to Thee!)
Tell him all thou have charged us,
In terms, like Thine, obsequious.
But, if unseasonable it is not,
Nor we be too over curious thought,
Be pleased to advise us, what above
All other Lovers, is Thy LOVE,
In Air, and rich Endowments large,
That us Thou giv'st so strict a charge?

SPONSA. Dilectus meus candidus and rubicundus.
FAIR Ones, Sponsa.
tho that, which you of me require,
And which my self should in your place desire,
Be far above all Art, or Skill of mine
As it deserves in Colors to design,
Yet that I may my just Compliance show,
The best that is in my power I Will gladly do.
Help, help me LOVE, to give each part its grace,
Nor from my humble Verse, or Heart, their Lines efface!
WHITE, and ruddy is my LOVE,
As when the Rose and Lilly strove.
White and pure, as Midday Light;
Ruddy as Clouds that fly the Night,
And even or'e-taken blushing run,
Blushing to o're-take the setting Sun,
And wish him in the Sea headlong plunge down*
The Banner worthy ^alone to bear,
Under which Myriads listed are.
Around his Head a Ray divine,
Bright as the purest Gold does shine.
His Locks as any Raven black,
Hang in loose Curls a-down his Back.
His Eyes like Doves by' a Rivers side,
Well set, and with a decence wide,
As washed in Milk, are lovely white,
But sparkling Fire from the killing sight.
His Cheeks with spicy mixtures flow,
Flourets in the downy Borders grow.
His Lips the choicest Myrrh distill,
Lillies the parting Valley fill.
His Hands with Rings of Berril set,
Of Jacynct, and of Chrysolet.
His Chest's like polisht Ivory,
Whose Veins of liquid Saphir lie.
Two Pedestals of solid Gold
His Marble-pillar'd Thighs uphold.
Libanus only is his Peer,
In The fairest Cedar growing there.
Strait as a Cedar and as tall,
(By Trees to express the Natural)
But when he speaks, so 'himself he is,
With every Grace, and every Bliss,
Whole Liba'nus is unfit to be,
With all its stores, the Pourtraict of his Deity.
Such, fair Daughters of Jerusalem,
Such is my LOVE, and such my Friend;
If such an One you chance to find,
Self him for me, when you have by the Marks discovered him.

VI.
AMBITIO SPONSAE.
CHORUS Filiarum Hieros^lymitanarum. CAP. 7.
SPONSA, SPONSVS, CHORUS alter Mulierum e Gentibus. CHORUS. Fill. Hier. Quo
abiit Dilectus tuus O Pul^^erri^^a?
WHITHER is thy BELOVED gone, Chorus.

Fairest of Women, whither turned aside?
Tell us, if Thou have his retirements known,
Or dei^n Thy self to be our Guide!
In every place, we'll with Thee seek Him out,
And find, (...)
or else be found of Him they sought.

SPONSA. Dilectus meus descend^ in Hor^um.
DOWN to his Garden my BELOVED went.
To' his Garden, as I thought, with full intent,
The Beds of Spices, in his Walk to see,
How fresh and verdant in his Livery.
There to divert a while, and feed his Flock,
And new-blown Lillies gather from his Stock.
-- But (Lo!) I see him, this way the steps incline.
I my BELOVED's AM, and HE IS MINE.
His, none but his, who amongst the Lillies feeds,
And with them to be Crowned, first for them bleeds.

SPONSVS. Pulchra es Amica mea, etc.
I.
BELOVED, Sponsus.
Thou 'art fair, my Love, behold Thou 'art fair,
As Tirza, which for Empire strove
With the holy City', and with it would compare;
Fair as Jeru'salem, coming from above:
So fair art Thou, but dreadful too withal,
As a ranged Phalanx, at the Trumpets call.
II.
Ah! from me turn those killing Eyes away,
Too piercing is their beauteous sight;
I yield, my Love, I yield, Thou have gained the Day,
And all in vain I see it is to fight:
Thou need'st but only look to overcome;
And, bound in Chains, thy Captives are led home.
III.
Chains of that Hair, that does like Gilead shine,
When on the Golden Fleeces there,
The jealous Sun, just ready to decline,
In The Mid-Heav'n stops, his Tresses to compare;
But brighter far, and longer much are they,
Than Sunbeam in the fairest Summer-day.
IV.
Such are Thy Locks, Thy Teeth like couples thence,
All washed in Aenons Streams, and shorn,
In Aenons Silver Streams, and rising whence,
To the joyful Shearing-house, new washed they'are born:
Each has it is make, and Twins they bear all round,
Nor is a Barren one in the number found.
V.
Thy Cheeks, which underneath Thy Temples lie,
And there their modest blushes hide,
In such a Vail, as does all Art defy,
(Nature for Virgins does that Vail provide)
Thy Cheeks, all vaild within Thy Curls, appear
Like shaded Pomegranats, but shine more clear.
VI.
Tho threescore Queens, and fourscore Concubines,
And Virgins numberless there be;
Ten thousand Virgins, Concubines, and Queens,
Nor are, nor ever were so fair as She:
She, who is my Dove, my Undefiled, the One,
Mine and her Morthers, more than all, alone.
VII.
The Virgins saw her, and their blessings sent,
Heavens tendrest blessings on her shour'd;
As if the officious drops they on her spent,
Should on themselves again, in streams be poured:
The Queens, and Concubines by different ways,
Whom yet they envied, could not choose but praise.

CHORUS Mulierum e Gentibus. Quae est ista quae progreditur quasi Aurora?
WHAT fair One's that, Chorus.
who like the Day,
From its dark Prison broke away,
Comes gently on, all crowned with Light,
The Rosie-fingred Morn in white;
And as She still does draw more near,
Still more radiant does appear?
How fair She is, as Midnight Moon,
Radiant too as Midday Sun;
Clear, and bright, but terrible withal,
As a ranged Phalanx at the Trumpets call.

SPONSA Sola. Descendi in Hortum Nucum.
DOWN to my Garden am I come; Sponsa.
and here,
How fresh, how verdant all my Fruits appear!
To see I Am come, how my Nuts clusters grow,
How my Vines shoot, and the Pomgranats blow,
But hold! -- What sudden change is it I feel?
What it is I know not, how I cannot tell;
But now, methinks, I mount, and now I fly:
Lo! how the Earth, and Clouds beneath me lie;
And now to the Empyrean I approach,
And starry is my Way, a Cherub is my Coach.

CHORUS Mulierum e Gentibus. Revertere, Revertere Sunamitis.
WHither, Chorus.
O Sulamite, whither so fast,
And why thus soon, to leave us, all this haste?
Return, O Sulamite, return, that we
On equal ground may thy chaste Beauties see!
Return, that we a while may on Thee gaze;
Return, or us together with Thee raise.

SPONSA. Quid videbis in Sunamite?
AH! Sponsa.
too over curious, and too over-bold,
What is it you would in the Sulamite behold?
What is it, when all that you can in her see,
Are but the Lines of a tremendous Majesty.
As when two Armies ready are to join,
And with stern Looks, to the onset give the Sign.

VII.
AMORES MUTUI.
SPONSVS SPONSA. CAP. 7.
SPONSVS. Quam pulchri sunt gressus tui in calceamentis?
FAIR One, Sponsus.
who do from Loins of Kings descend,
The King, whose Empire does over all extend,
Beyond those Worlds, which undiscovered lie,
In The boundless circuit of the All-embracing Sky,
Let others, as they please, recount Thy praise,
I from the ground its Monument will raise.
Those parts of Thee which have the lowest place,
But beauteous in their kind --
Thy Feet, which on their well fixed bases, bear
The fairest Pile, that Nature e're did rear.
How glorious are they, when Thou leadst Thy Flock,
Or on Thy buskins raised, or in thy humble Sock?
The juncture of Thy Thighs like Jewels are,
The Work of the most famed Artificer.
Thy Navil (nor let Scoffers here blaspheme
The Mysteries of the New Jerusalem,
Or to vile Lust debase the noble Theme.)
But like a Goblet round Thy Navil is,
Brim full, and flowing with the richest Juice.
Thy Belly pregnant with a numerous Train,
Which Heavens void space, shall People once again.
Like heaps of Wheat, with Lillies set around,
Thy Children show, and how their Mother's Crowned.
Thy Brest's like two Twin-Roses, and Twins they are,
In all the Herd there is not a lovelier pair.
Thy Neck is like a Tower of Ivory,
Hung with the Trophies of Loves Victory.
Thy Eyes surpass the Silver Streams, that run
By Bathrabbim, to the Pools of Essebon.
Thy Nose, of perfect shape, like a Fort Royal stands,
Off from Thy Cheeks, and the under Plains commands.
Or like Loves Labyrinth, by the Forest side,
Pointing, and to Damascus opening wide.
Thy Head's like Carmel, and Thy purple Hair,
The brightest Dye the Assyrian Fleece can bear.
Fettered in its soft Chains the King does lie,
Enamored of the Bands of his Captivity.
How fair art Thou! how made for all delight!
Slender, clean Limbed, and, as a Palm, upright?
Grow, happy Tree, the Queen of all the Wood,
Grow, as Thou do, less great than Thou art good!
In whose warm Bosom, the Vines clusters rest,
And show, and seem themselves, the swellings of Thy Breast.
Up to the Palm Tree I Will ascend, I said,
Palm in my Hand; with Palm Ill crown my Head;
The blushing clusters with my Lips I Will seize,
And all their winy juice, rich Wines express:
With which enflamed, I Will cooling Apples take,
Apples, the best repast that Lovers make;
Apples, whose Cordial they the best can tell,
Apples, which like Thy perfumed Breath do smell.
Apples, Thy Breath: Thy Speech is generous Wine,
That sparkles in the Cup, and gives its shine;
Moves it self right, to my Beloved goes,
Goes as it can, and all its Wonders shows:
Making the Lips of those, who sleep to speak,
And at one touch their Chains and silence break.

SPONSA. Fgo Dilecto me, and ad me conversio ejus.
I My BELOVED's am alone, Sponsa.

And beside me has he none;
None beside me does he love,
Equal none, and none above.
Mine he is and mine alone,
And beside him have I none;
None beside him do I love,
Equal none, and none above.
Come then my LIFE, ah! come away!
In The City let us not ever stay;
But look how our Fields do smile,
And in the Villas lodge a while.
There, with the Sun, We'll early rise,
And visiting our Nurseries,
See how the Vines their Branches spread,
And the young Grape shoots forth his head:
And how the Pomgranates do blow.
But then my LOVE less ruddy show.
My Loves there will I to Thee give.
Loves in the Country happie'st live.
The Mandrag's there, that Love excites,
The Mandrag, chief in Loves delights;
Which forward now, begins to 'appear,
And fumes our Gates with it is Rosy Hair.
With many a Fruit, and many a Flower,
Which I will in Thy Bosom pour.
Store of Pleasures, new, and old,
More than can of Tongue be told.
More, if possible, than enough,
And all reserved for Thee, my LOVE.

VIII.
CATASTROPHE.
SPONSA. CHORUS Gentium. CAP. 8.
SPONSVS. SPONSA. Quis mihi det te Fratrem meum?
I LOVE, Sponsa.
but cannot yet my LOVE enjoy,
Ah! Love that Thou should have so much alloy!
Still some nice thing to interrupt Our bliss,
Something to fault, which yet we would not miss!
This as I think, I wish Thou were my BROTHER,
Thou my Soul loves, the Son of my own Mother:
How would I 'embrace Thee then, and kiss thine Eyes,
Where e're I meet Thee, safe from base surmize!
Home would I lead Thee to my Mothers House,
And, by her taught, the happy Moment use;
Talk over our Loves, fill, fill the Wine,
The spicy blood of the Pomgranate and Vine.
Satiate with which, Thou should thy Left Hand place
Under my Head, whole me with the' right embrace.
It Is done, and see he sleeps --
Fair Daughters of Jerusalem,
Who are, and to your selves more glorious seem,
In our attendance, than your own bright Stem,
By the Hills, and by the Launs,
By the Roes, and by the Fauns,
By the Fields, and by the Woods,
By the Springs, and by the Floods,
All that I or you count dear,
All he loves, and all I fear:
I charge you, I charge you not to awake
My LOVE, from the rest, which now he does take,
Till himself pleases the signal to make!

CHORUS Gentium. Quae est ista, quae ascendit de deserto?
WHAT fair One's that (but words can never express Chorus.

How fair she is) who from the Wilderness,
Leaning on her BELOVED's Arm ascends,
And hitherward their amo'rous Journey tends.
They 'are come, and now approach't so near,
That were not rudeness, we their privacies may hear.

SPONSVS. Sub arbore malo suscitavi te.
-- THERE 'twas, under the Apple Tree, Sponsus.

Where first I found, and first raised Thee.
Thy Mother there her Fruit disclosed,
And there thou first did lie exposed,
And had lain still, but that I came,
I ^d to Thy rescue, by my first Loves purest flame.

SPONSA. Pone me ut signaculum super Cor. Sponsa.

BLESSED, ever blessed be that Day.
When t^us Thou cam'est, and thus I lay!
O let us never, never part,
But make me' a Scal upon Thy Heart!
A Badg Thou on Thy Arm may wear,
That where Thou art, I may be there,
And never, never from Thee part,
But always on, or next Thy Heart!
" For love, (And who loves more than I?)
" Stronger than Death, does Death defy.
" His Conquests like the Graves, extend,
" And further, up to Heaven his flames ascend.
" But never was there such a Flame,
" As that, in which to me he came;
" So dreadful, scorching, and so bright,
" I blessed, but trembled at the sight.
" A Fire; Love was himself that Fire,
" Which in no Waters will expire:
" But, eve'n in Floods, securely lives,
" And all their watery threats survives.
" Nor quench, nor drown it can the Flood,
" Nor buy it, all the seeming good
" This World, that mighty Chapman, boasts
" In his vast Traffic through all Coasts.
" For so invaluable is its price,
" And its exchange so monstrous high does rise,
" The Universe too little is for 'its Merchandise.

CHORUS Gentium, etc. Soror nostra parva and ubera non habet.
A LITTLE Sister yet we have, Chorus.

For whom we'd Loves assistance crave.
Young, and little, though she 'is yet,
And her Breast not fully set,
Tho as yet no Breasts she has,
Grow our Sister does apace.
But if thus high Loves price does go,
What shall we for our little Sister do,
When the time comes, that we should her bestow?

SPONSVS. Si murus est aedificemus super.
IF, when grown up, your Sister prove a Wall, Sponsus.

We'll on her build a Silver Arcenal;
Defense, and Ornament we'll to her give,
And all her Stones shall, as in the Quarry, live.
If, when grown up, your Sister prove a Door,
With Cedar mouldings we will case her o*re;
With Freeze, and Cornice all her Pillars crown,
And to her sacred Glories add our own.

SPONSA. Ego Murus and ubera mea sicut Turris.
BUT I Am a Wall, and my soft Breasts full grown, Sponsa.

Like Towers, upon the subject Camp look down.
My constancy to guard the Fort does stand,
And dares the rudest Shock, of the roughest Hand.
This as my WELL-BELOVED heard me say,
The Holy Boast he did approve;
And in his Eyes I found such grace that Day,
I dare no longer doubt his Love.

SPONSVS. Vinea fuit Pacifico in ea, etc.
I HAVE a Vineyard, so has Solomon; Sponsus.

His at a distance lies, in Baal-hannon,
To Keepers, and to Under-Keepers set,
And Yearly, for a thousand pieces let.
But mine, the Vineyard that is my own, does lie
In my own care, and ever in my Eye.
Accountable for 'its Fruits to me alone,
And sharers in them, with me, have I none.
Compare we now our Vineyards, peaceful King!
Which does to 'its Lord the greatest Profit bring?
Thine for a thousand Pieces yields its store;
Admit it do; it is worth two hundred more,
But these the Keepers have: Mine is my own,
Accountable for' its Fruits to me alone,
(As for its care) and sharers have I none.
Thou art that Vineyard, Love, my Vineyard Thou,
Who in the Flowery Gardens dwell low;
Low, but with all the Stars of the Garden crowned,
And Thy Companions sitting Thee around,
Attent, and listening to Thy tuneful Voice;
O, make me hear it, and fulfill my Joys!

SPONSA. Fuge Dilecte mi and assimilare, etc.
AND so Thou shall -- Sponsa.

LOVE, bring the Day,
Haste it, LOVE* and come away!
On the Spicy Mountain tops appear,
Swift as the fleet-wing footed Deer!
And driving thence the baleful Night,
On the Spicy Mountains shed Thy Light,
Fleet as their heels, and than their Horns more bright!
Veni Domine, JESV, Veni cito!
SOLI DEO GLORIA.
Cujus Amore inflammato cord haec occini,
Inscius licet, and indignus: Suscipe lauds
Famuli tui. AMEN.

Apud Hartley Maudet. Com. Southton, in Vigilia Ascensionis. MDCLXXVII.
(...). THE LEGEND OF LOVE,
CANTO. I.
I.
SONG, which the God of Love did first inspire,
Not he the blind, and sottish World calls so,
No God, nor ought save a wild raging Fire,
Which with incessant Bellows Lust does blow,
But HE, to whom all things their being owe,
In whom alone they live, in whom they move,
And, by his Love made, to perfection grow,
All who below adore Him, or above,
The Mighty Lord of Hosts, the very GOD of LOVE,
II.
Song, by the very God of Love inspired,
And of a thousand more the sacred Chief,
But wherein Solomon, from himself retired,
Not what his Love, but what was his belief,
(Songs ill became the cause of all his Grief,
If love we to our Mortal Standart bring,
Whose spoils are Robbe'ries, and whose God's a thief)
Under Love's borrowed name to the World did sing,
LOVE is the borrowed Name, Holy Churches FAITH the thing*
III.
Song, which the borrowed Name do understand,
And all the Mysteries which in it lie,
For it is the Dialect of the Heavenly Land,
Nor as to us it sounds does signify,
(Blessed Souls in Love discourse with the most High)
Thou, who know this, and more than this, whence came
The profanation of the Deity,
And how this lustful Earth took the empty Name,
Embracing sulphu'rous clouds, for its Gods purest flame.
IV.
Tell the World, Song, whence first its madness grew!
Who knows not the World is mad? Thou best of all,
Which, if 'twere possible Inno'cence to renew,
To its first Inno'cence would the World recall,
And more than e're it lost in the wretched Fall:
By love Thou callst it, and the Voice is known,
Well known 's the Voice, but we too sensual,
So brutish^ in our Lusts obscene are grown,
That called by it is name, we think it an Idol of our own.
V.
Yet none of ours is what we' have stolen the name,
That Idol, which we thus with Mirtles crown,
But with our form from Heaven at once it came,
From Heaven was with a Silver Cord let down,
And into the Souls mass divinely thrown,
To be its Salt, miraculously contrived;
And first in Heaven the name of Love was known,
From whence at length it hither was derived,
And here a while such, and so called in pleasure lived.
VI.
In pleasure 'it lived, and with its changed abode,
Strangely surprizd, was strangely pleased awhile,
At first perceived not the unusual load,
But in her Face, who should betray 'it, did smile,
As fearless, as unconscious yet of guile:
And to a Body tho it were designed,
A Body, as the Land of its exile,
All parts so' agreeable, and soft did find,
Its Tour seemed thence enlarged more, than confined.
VII.
For all with Spi^rits it did converse before,
It self a Spirit, but when the Eternal Mind
To new displays of his creating Power,
In beings of a lower Rank inclined,
Which Angels yet should not come far behind;
Soon as the great Resolve was past above,
And Man of all those Works the Lord designed,
Love the first mover, and the last, did move,
That He, whom all obeyed, over all should rule by Love.
VIII.
It moved thus, and the motion was embraced,
Als' Love, that made it downwards took its way;
And viewing how the Bodies parts were cast,
Seized, with the Spi^rit, the scarce yet molded Clay,
Nor for partition, or nice choice did stay,
But blending both together, seized the whole;
Both in the whole, whole both in each part lay,
And both together making the humane Soul,
The unactive Matter did both quicken, and control.
IX.
The Spi'rit gave Life, with what to Life pertain,
Sense, Motion, and the several Faculties,
A cheerful, goodly, and a pompous Train,
When ruled, as they' ought, and managed by the Wise;
The wise is Love, that the whole unites, and ties,
And which, where e're it undisturbed does reign,
Makes the earthly frame, with the Heavenly sympathize,
Nor to rebel dare any passion strain,
When over them reason, over that love does hold the rein.
X.
Thus in the first happy Pair, a while Love reigned,
And but a while, with regal Sovereignty,
Who to its Lore so early both were trained,
That Love it self, as rapt in ecstasy,
Wondered what in dull Flesh the' effects might be.
It wondered, but so chaste, and innocent,
So Love-conspiring every part did see,
And so obsequious to its great intent,
That Love it self enamored was of its descent.
XI.
Forthy the happy pair, with severed flame,
Which yet in both was one, to love inclined;
Two downwards only, but rise'n whence it came,
Again collected in one point, and joynd,
And still the higher rise'n, the more refind;
They loved, and so in Heaven to love are seen
Bright Seraphs, nor could Differe'nce be assignd,
But that the Body here did crowd between,
There by pure Spi'rits was dressed out the mighty Scene.
XII.
The new formed Body here did crowd between,
But all to love was so disposed, and made,
In either Sex, and with so Beauteous mien,
That either seemed with other well appay'd,
And Love himself the Hymenaeal said:
(For Love 's an He, who proud of what was done,
And of Man most, as fairest there Pourtraid,
The form assumed, which likest was to' his own,
And will but as a Male, when sung in Verse be known.)
XIII.
Love said it, and Heaven all he said approved,
By signs authentic, which none durst deny;
The new Creation in soft measures moved,
And the whole World of beings, waiting by,
Bowed all a-down, and blessed the Augury,
They bowed, and blessed it, as they waiting stood,
They bowed, and blessed it each, tho near so high,
From the Angeli^ Natures, to the Brood
Of Earth-born Vegetables, and God saw 'twas Good.
XIV.
'Twas good He saw for Man to have his Make,
Man saw no less 'twas good for him to love,
And in his Arms the Virgin Bride did take,
With all the' endearments that affection move,
Nor Hell that looked askaunce could the Rites reprove.
In 'his Arms he took her, as Superior,
She, as one yet, who would be Conquered, strove,
Till safe arriving at the Genial Bower,
And conquered both, Love each saluted Conqueror.
XV.
There gentle purpose did they enterchange,
And all the innocent delights, that make
Blessed Wedlock happy, nor did think it strange,
Connubial pleasures to or leave, or take;
For, nor him Inn'ocence did, nor her forsake,
And they who think it did, or that the place,
Or state forbad such use 'ith sordice rake
Of their own lusty-head, foully miss the case,
And on God's noblest Masterpiece retort disgrace.
XVI.
Think so, who will for me, and bear the shame
Of such sad thought; those just ones thought not so
Nor God, nor Love, who kindled first the flame
Wherewith they in desire alike did glow,
Desire unhappy never till 't would know
Secrets of knowledge unpermitted it,
Unhappy knowledge, source of all our Woe,
Unhappy Tree, in the midst oath, Garden set,
Unhappy seen, but tasted, more unhappy yet!
XVII.
Forbidden therefore 'twas, but that alone,
What else beside Man could, or wish, or crave,
Enclosure, Limit and Restraint had none,
Nor other Law than what Right Reason gave;
Right Reason, unforced yet to be a Slave,
To 'its under Ministers, a Lawless suit
Of stubborn Lusts, when they the Mastery have,
Less of the Man partaking than the Brute,
And are the dire effects of the forbidden Fruit.
XVIII.
Too dire Effects, if God had seen it fit,
Which Natures Course disturbed and altered so,
That all the Bands, which held it were unknit,
And quite reversed the Pyramid did show,
Above a Front unshapely, 'a point below:
Thus while 'twas turning, but once set upon
It Is no Foundation, for so grim a Brow,
Or which was but a point, as good as none,
Down fell the Heap, Hell trembled, and the Earth did groan.
XIX.
Love was that Pyramid, which whilst it stood,
As fixed by Heaven, on its own proper Base,
(Matter, in Mankind, cemented by Blood
Spiritous, where Sense had lowest place,
Reason supreme) whole Nature did embrace;
Which over her Works such Robes of Beauty threw,
And with such Rays did the Universe enchase,
That Instinct some, the' rest Inclination drew,
Only to Love, and what was loved, by Love pursue.
XX.
Love, and Fruition of their Kind, was the' whole,
Whither to 'arrive, most Creatures did aspire,
(By Sense led,) and arrived; but Man, whose Soul
Had particles of an Ethereal Fire,
Aimed at Fruition too, but of what high'er,
And more sublime, was laid up, than his Kind,
Or that part of 'it his Flesh, whence to retire,
In Innocence he found himself inclined,
The beauties of his own, and of the Eternal Mind.
XXI.
His Kind he loved, as all things did beside,
All things beside his Kind he also loved
In their due Rank, but how to be applied
To use, the Hypothesis was to be proved,
And thousand Stones to move, and be removed,
E're Love he to so just an height could raise,
As in his Intellect it was approved,
That he from Earth to Heaven by steps of Praise,
Might hourly mount, and boldly tread God's secrer Ways.
XXII.
Deep in himself was the Foundation laid,
And in those Acts, wherein he did partake
With meaner Beings, for his Empire made,
And of which some for Propagation make,
By their first Institution, called Love by mistake,
Whose name they have engrost; but Love,
Or cause, or sign of Love no more are, take
But the Disguise off, as bare Acts they prove,
Nor higher look, than it is Love naturally to move.
XXIII.
These therefore were laid deep, and of the Pile,
If ought, Substruction, consecrate to Night,
The sense of Touch, too subject to beguile,
Where Reason does not hold the Balance right;
Reason, wherein true Love, and pure Delight
Only consists, and is the Fabrics Scale,
Uninterruptedly to' ascend Heavens height,
To him, who has the skill Heaven thus to assail,
And over the Omnipotent by almighty Love prevail.
XXIV.
The first Man had that Skill, and of his Love,
BEAUTY and GOOD the Object, he it sought
In that bright Sphere, where it on High does move,
And thither by Reduction all things brought,
Which, in their Season, beautiful are wrought;
All that in others, or himself he spied,
Which of the Heavenly Goodness Trace had ought;
And by this Chain did an Ascent provide,
To the Footstool of that Throne, where its first Link was tied.
XXV.
By view hereof He upward did ascend,
Still upward, as he cast his curious view,
The Higher any thing did upward tend,
The better 'twas, and much more Beauteous grew,
And with it from the Earth, Love upward drew:
From the Earth, of which once having lost the sight,
Love more refined became, by Objects new,
And all presented by a clearer Light,
That did exhaust the Flesh, and ravished the Sp'rit.
XXVI.
For having past at length the Atmosphere,
By many a Stage, the Flesh was left behind;
And now another Face of things appear,
And other Beauties, that surprise the Mind,
On which to gaze he did strange Pleasure find:
And tho their Light excessive he scarce bear,
Seeing above his Journey unconfined,
Upward he presses still, and void of Fear,
As perfect grown, above the Skies his Head does rear.
XXVII.
Above the Skies he to those Heavens does mount,
Step after step ascending, where there be
For happy Souls, as sacred Writ does count,
Mansions prepared, for their sublimity,
The third Heaven called, and 'its Furniture does see:
With which he could delighted ever stay,
But that advanced to the last Degree,
That mortal Foot e're touched, a farther way,
By Wing to be dispatched he' espies, Loves last Essay.
XXVIII.
So on he claps his best, and largest Wings,
And swift as thought, but more direct upright
Than Eagle soars, into a Welkin springs,
(If so it may be called) of Flame more bright,
And pure, than what by this Worlds Sun, and Light,
Can be compared; and in blessed Ecstasy,
With sacred Wonder, but without affright,
Those fair Idaeas contemplates, whereby
Himself, and the World was made, and there laid up do lie.
XXIX.
Ascended hither, Love the place well knew,
As whence himself into our World came down,
Eve'n Man as nearer to his God he drew,
New motions felt, and Powers before unknown;
And swallowed up in Speculation,
Forgot what ever he admired below,
Himself, and Her, whom he had left alone,
To wait' his return, and rapt, he scarce knew how,
To the Land of Love, himself a Love transformed did grow.
XXX.
For why that Tract the Realm of Love was styled,
And all, who thither in deducted mind,
Leaving this lower Earth arrive, are filled,
As the bright Region is, with thoughts refined,
And Quintessence of Love; but of what kind,
Impossible it is to be expressed,
And in a Chime of soundful Words designed,
Which but Conceits are, to our Fashion dressed,
And if Immortal, with the mortal Load oppressed.
XXXI.
Suffice it, Angels there Inhabited,
Ranged all in their Trinal Triplicities,
Into a Body, under Love their Head,
And ready all for some Renowned Emprise,
Soon as the mighty Signal given is:
He saw it given, and all before a Throne,
Of Heavenly Diamond, vailing each his Eyes,
Each Angel prostrate on his Face falls down,
And at its Footstool casts his Consecrated Crown.
XXXII.
From simple ANGELS, as the Inferior Band,
To the ARCHANGELS, led by Michael,
Then to DOMINIONS, that the third Rank stand,
To VIRTUES then, whose numerous Banners swell,
And PRINCIPALITIES, of whom none fell
With Rebel Lucifer, 'or abbetted him,
To POWERS then, and to THRONES, whom who can tell?
And so up to the eighth Order, CHERUBIM,
But by the ninth complete, Love-flaming SERAPHIM.
XXXIII.
All these our Father saw, by Love retired
In, and above Himself, but over them all,
One fairer yet, to whom his Mind aspired,
The Incomprehensible Original,
In whom no Blemish, and no Spot can fall:
By Love he saw Him, and in Him the end,
And utmost point of Love, enough to' appall
The Holie'st of his Sons, who should descend
From' his after sinful Loins, and thither after tend.
XXXIV.
But him the sight appalled not, rather filled
With Images more fair his enlarged Breast;
And from the Principles his Love instil'd,
On naught less high could he affix his rest,
Or be content tho of the Earth possessed:
The' whole Earth, that in this Res'very viewed round,
Too scanty seemed an Heaven-born Soul to' invest,
Too scant a Limit, and too scant a Bound,
For what an Infinite was only equal found.
XXXV.
Excursions therefore into that he made,
Often Excursions into Infinite,
Infinite Love, and Beauties seen displayed
In The Prototype, by Intellectual Light,
And Reasons yet undim'd discursive sight:
Tho when that failed, or when it was weary grown,
Through 'excess of conceived Vision, as it might,
The' Almighties self would come in Person down,
And often prevent Man's journey to Him, with His own.
XXXVI.
Or come, or send Him frequent Embassies
Of Angels, who still ready on the Wing,
With hasty flight, from summit of the Skies,
Would seem to vie, who should the Message bring,
And first approach the Favorite of their King:
Tho all the Message, which they bore was Love,
And all their Service was but Ministering
To one below them made, yet who above,
Was Heavens great Care, and greatest Ornament should prove.
XXXVII.
Not much below them, while his Innocence,
On its Foundation did unshockt abide,
And perfect Love, seen in obedience,
The nether World so fast to the upper ti'd,
That Hell between them could not once divide:
One Heart, one Business was in both approved,
One God alike, in both was magnified,
That mighty Axis, round which both were moved,
And whom alike both praised, and whom alike both loved.
XXXVIII.
Alike they loved, and as they loved alike,
All Virtues in that Act did comprehend,
Which did in both an awful Reverence strike,
And careful made them, lest they should offend,
But chiefly Man did thence his Mark intend,
With greater vigor, as on whom was laid
Fear for a Rein, if Hell its Wiles should send;
For Death was threatened, if he disobeyed,
And He not to be' high-minded, chose to be afraid.
XXXIX.
Love made him choose so kind, and wholesome Fear,
From which all Torment yet secluded was,
And but the brighter made his Love appear,
Like Beams reflected upon burnisht ^rass,
That their own Native Luster thence surpass:
So shon his Love, and thence his Temperance,
That the forfended Limit would not pass,
Nor to behold it care'd with wanton Glance,
What ever fond Conceit its Glories might enhance.
XL.
Hence Justice, hence true Magnanimity,
Prudence, and Wisdom, over his Soul did flow,
And sacred Truth, and peaceful Loyalty,
And all the Graces, that to Love do owe
Their Rise, and into' Eternal Rivers grow:
And all upon the Supreme Beauty placed,
That Good, which still the more we come to know,
The more we' admire, till in its Arms embraced,
An end of all our Love we find, the First, and Last.
XLI.
Such was his Love, and like it was his Praise,
A Service, wherein with him Nature joined,
Whole Nature, and each part, by several ways,
As with him in one love they were combined;
And various was the Anthems stops, and kind,
Articulate by Man, their High Priest, made,
And in one sacred Hallelu-jah twined,
Which he, as First Fruits, on Earths Altar laid,
And Heaven with grateful Odours early did invade.
XLII.
Their Orisons, which he each Morning paid,
With his fair Partner, or by Angels taught,
Or whereof capable their Form was made,
By Images from Heaven first thither brought,
And which they into perfect Figure wrought,
By Plastic Art of Words harmonious;
Which to the great Occasion came unsought:
And whether sung in Verse, or said in Prose,
In Prose most powerful were, in Verse most numerous.
XLIII.
For Verse, and all that does to Verse belong
(In this lowe'r World) had its first happy rise
In Innocence, which first invented Song,
And how so e're one may its Charms despise,
Or which is worse, abuse, in Paradise,
By Pause, and Fugue's, adapted to fit strains,
Was first performed; to whose Discoveries
The Sons of Verse now elocate their pains,
And (save from Heaven) expect not their Immortal gains
XLIV.
In Paradise, discovered first was Verse,
And Voice, and Number with it given to Song,
And glorious Subjects, which both did rehearse,
Alternate, and in Chorus, as or long,
Or short returns did to the Ground belong;
But than of Love they sounded nothing more,
Or ofter, or with Breast more large, and strong,
The Love Divine, whom thus they did implore
In raptur'ous Odes, and (as they of it sang) adore.
XLV.
They sang how All things their beginning had,
From Loves diffusive, and Life-giving heat;
How Immaterial Spirits, with Glory clad,
Its Firstborn were, with Love as high, and great,
As from this lower World's removed their Seat:
Then how this lower World from Nothing rose,
And Nowhere, in that beauteous order met,
That Place, and Matter, which the whole compose,
And circumscribe, unconceiv'd Luster did disclose.
XLVI.
Not at an instant made, but by degrees,
As confused Chaos could the impression take,
In Six Days space, and thoroughly purge its Lees,
Hell newly formed more horrible to make,
And its own new made Enmities off-shake,
For of such jarring Parts it was contrived,
And of such contrarieties did partake,
That it by Discord, and Confusion lived,
(A life such as it was) with Death to be survived.
XLVII.
So strove they, thus would they have striven ever;
Till pitying their debate, the Spirit of Love
Calmed the discordant Mole, and did dissever
The Eternal Combatants, placed some above,
Others did to the deep Abyss remove
Fast to be held, in Adamantine Chain;
Whilst those few Parts, that did more ductile prove,
Into Four Principles, which all contain,
Themselves in all contained, were solely left to reign.
XLVIII.
FIRE, which as lightest took the highest place,
And upward raised its towering Head; then AIR,
That follow^d it, but with unequal pace,
And tho it vy'd to be and look as fair,
Forced in the midst to hang, self-ballanc'd there:
Next WATER, which the Surface covered over,
That pregnant Mother of the EARTH, less rare,
In its vast Womb conceived, but which, before
It could emerge, lackt mighty Love to force the Door.
XLIX.
And so it did, but LIGHT was first to shine,
And an whole Day, for that which makes the Day,
But little enough was thought in the Mind Divine,
Through Darkness palpable to clear its way,
And all its various Beauties to display:
Darkness, which tho but counted Privative,
Such claims to th heap, whence 'twas called out, did lay,
That Love like equal shares to both did give,
Alternately, each Day, in Day, and Night to live.
L.
But harder were the teeming Waters Throes,
When on the second Day, Earth nearer came
To its great Birth, like weight, that heavier grows,
Long born, and to break through disjoynts the frame;
The Waters pangs compared thus, were the same,
When they divided burst, but never to close,
Stopped by the solid FIRMAMENT, whose Name
Immovable Partition does suppose,
By' whose Shore disjoined, upper and nether Ocean flows.
LI.
And now the third Day of her Monstrous Child
Half way delivered the Great Mother was;
Monstrous, unsightly yet, with Horror filled,
Which in its Oasy Arms it did embrace,
And, half suppressed, to the' Birth would never let pass,
But, with it joined, one Monstrous Body made,
Above DRY GROUND, below a confused Mass,
Part Earth, with Briny Hatchments overlaid,
Part unmixt Water, upon empty Nothing staid,
LII.
And called SEA; as what appeared was LAND;
Rough, bare, mishape't, tho dry, unbeautifi'd,
It self unbeautiful, vast plains of Sand,
More horribly deformed, with terrors Pride,
Mountains, that to' Heaven aspired, and gaping wide,
With raised up Jaws, threatened to swallow down,
In gorge unsatiate, glories there envied,
And wrinkled Forehead, which scarce made did frown,
And Omens give of Future War, from cause unknown.
LIII.
Nor could there cause be gi'ven, for a new Face
From the' Love Divine it took, and Nakedness
Was clothed upon with all the Charming Grace
Of Fruit, and Flower; and the grim Gyantess,
(Its own Eternal Goodness to express)
Kind Heaven illumined with a double Light,
The fourth Day made, the greater, and the less;
By Day the SUN with vital heat, and bright,
To warm, the MOON with starry Robe to' invest at night.
LIVE.
Nor was this all, but at the fifth Days dawn,
Earth, and her Mother Sea replenished
With new Inhab'itants were, and every Laune,
And every Hill (scared Solitude thence fled)
Legions of FOWL produced, and kindly bred,
Which on large Wings, above the Ground did fly,
But perching on some Tree, made that their Bed;
Whilst Lakes, and Streams, and the huge Sea fast by,
With mighty WHALES were filled, and with the lesser FRY.
LV.
Filled were the Floods with these, but still the Earth,
(As whose Wing'd-People most partook of the Air,
Their haunt) impregnate with a second Birth,
For which disclosed 't had room enough to spare,
Did on the sixth Day to disclose prepare;
And out all BEASTS, and REPTILS, in their kind,
Sprang from the fertile Womb, proportioned fair,
Each to its Nature, but with Look declined,
To the Earth whence taken, to the Earth whereto confined.
LVI.
Of these, and other Works of God they sang,
In Lays harmonious, as Love utterance gave;
Yet these, and others, which they lowdly rang,
Were but as Praeludes, which, with Mast'ry brave,
Their Voice showed, and what compass Verse might have:
Verse which then triumphed in Recitative,
When they all other Grounds resolved to wave,
Sang of themselves, and Him, who at once did give
One power to Sing thus to Him, and like Him to live.
LVII.
A wondrous Work it was, from Nothing thus
All things in weight, and measure up to raise,
And perfect Order formed most beauteous,
Subordinate, as different were the Ways,
Whereby their Maker would direct his Praise:
But none so Wondrous did, and strange appear,
Of Power and Beauty with so rich displays,
As Man's Formation, made the Rule to bear,
And sublimed Earth equal to highest Heaven rear.
LVIII.
That Dust could Live, in what was done before
Was plainly told, but that it too could love,
As Love all life in it contains, and more,
All that or Reason knows, or can improve,
The Eternal Treasures only were enough
To drein, and to be drein'd: For God but spoke,
And all below, and all the Hosts above,
Being, and Life from the great Word did take;
But Hands Divine, Man's model were employed to make.
LIX.
By Hands Divine, his Body first was wrought,
The full Abridgment of this World to be;
With curious Art, to 'its last perfection brought,
But infinitely base, in its degree,
To the Soul the Pourtraict of the Deity,
Into his Nostrils breathed; that in his Brain
Might be infixt the Heavenly Ima'gry,
And Life, with Vital Blood in every Vein,
To the Parts extreme conveyed, the Character retain.
LX.
Love was that Character, in Holiness,
And perfect Purity exemplifi'd,
And Innocence, which that first state did bless,
And Reason with them Empire to divide,
And over the Inferior Appetite preside,
Which it restrained, and furnished with true Skill,
It self, in all its Acts to curb, and guide;
At least had power to do so, and fulfill
The Charge Divine, close backed by Freedom of the Will.
LXI.
Blessed Qualities, which made him Lord and King,
Of all this lower World, and Majesty
On his Erected Countenance stamped, did bring
Heaven down to Earth, and Earth, that flat did lie,
Advance'd to be for Angels Company;
Nay farther, and what Angels did admire,
For its Aetern Exemplar, the Most High,
Who with his Work delighted, would retire
Frequent from Heaven, as to divert, and view it nighe'r.
LXII.
Himself hereby Man dexterously did guide,
And over himself so absolutely reign,
(The greatest Kingdom in the World beside,
And which all other Kingdoms did contain,
In 'it self in Chief, or Vassalages Chain)
That only Peace, and only what was Good,
And only Love was given for Love again;
With Charms, that by no force could be withstood,
And centred in one Point Indivisible GOD.
LXIII.
Hail happy state of Innocence, thrice Hail!
Hail to Thy Love and Thee! And may my Verse,
From thence inspired, with generous Souls prevail,
As they deserve, Thy Wonders to rehearse,
And through the World thy Sacred Name disperse!
No Theme, like that, so high can Numbers raise,
Or render more Humane Mankinds converse;
For if there any Virtue be, or Praise,
They in Perfection shon, in those first happy Days.
LXIV.
Ah! that those Happy Days should be so few,
Ended, cut off, e're scarce they were begun!
Whose early Happiness, when known to Two,
A Third crept in, by whom all was undone,
And Love unthron'd hard drive'n away to run.
Follow him, Muse, for if he should go wrong,
Thou art in danger too, whose Fates are one
With his; Nor can Thou be without him long,
But do it, if thou'hast Courage, in another Song.
THE LEGEND of LOVE.
CANTO. II.
I.
I OFTEN am thinking whether Love was known
To the World Heathen, and what was their sense
Of Man's, and His Origination;
Or if of this first state of Innocence
They any certain knowledge had, and whence
The Notice was derived, and to them came,
Dark as it was; and turn over Books, but thence
Am little satisfied, find there the Name,
It Is true, but Pillars of black Smoke instead of Flame.
II.
Fiction I there find has so Truth disguised,
That hard it is for one to know what is true;
And, amongst all the Vanities they prized,
How much, or little 'twas they truly knew,
And ancient Faith obscured with Fancies new;
Which diversely, as diffe'rent was their Art,
They dressed out, and amongst the People threw,
Part plain, with lame or monstrous Figures part,
As in a scarce, or not discovered Countries Chart.
III.
For there as We' Hippogryphs, and Satyrs place,
Or Seas, or Carra'vans, when there is nothing found
Of certainty, to fill the empty space,
That each Man in his own sense may abound:
Just so did they, but more the Truth confound,
With Fable, as themselves it suited best,
Who, so they did but keep the marked out bound,
Conveyed them by Tradition, for the rest,
Left it to others, as 'twas left them to be ghest.
IV.
A Godlike therefore 'twas, and Golden Age,
Tho little known to them, who called it so,
And of it counted, filled with ancient rage,
Not as it was, but did at distance show;
Tho something, it is confessed, they seemed to know;
And what they of it said, by chance was true,
As to the main, if somewhat we allow,
For the false Light, and those, who 'its Figure drew,
In Plane, or Prospective, and were but Copiers too.
V.
By Negatives the Plane was most designed,
And proved the Fairest, and the luckiest Draught;
The Prospective was stiff, and more inclined
To an hard confused roughness, scarcely brought
To decence, done by Images they sought
In their own Breasts, or Age, by Heavenly Light
Unlumin'd, and by false Priests blindly taught:
Love only made over all to take his Flight,
Came nearest to the Truth, if understood aright.
VI.
For by a Child they Love did represent,
As his best Emblem, under which disguise,
(For such it was) they hid with high intent,
Or rather plainly showed those Mysteries,
Which were concealed from base and vulgar Eyes;
Native Simplicity, pure Innocence,
Absolute freedom from all touch of Vice,
An easy mildness Favors to dispense,
And all those Graces, that its Actions influence.
VII.
Not that they would that Ages Faults approve,
In Lovers, but its Virtues thence propound;
For tho a Child they made, and painted Love,
Scarce such another Child in the World was found,
By whose soft Hands were fiercest Lions bound,
Traind to his Yoke; the Course of Thunder staid;
And all the Gods above, and underground,
Which Gentilisms great Religion made,
(As sottish as it was) in heavy Fetters laid.
VIII.
Naked, it is true, they feigned him; but just so,
As we, who yet esteem our selves more Wise,
In Oratories naked Angels show,
Nor count it blame our selves thus to advise,
How open all we do, or think to Heaven lies;
That from it naught we can, or would conceal,
That from us banished is all Artifice;
All which by 'his Nakedness Love does reveal,
And, from his Step-dames Temples, to the Church appeal;
IX.
But by his Fillet they such Lessons taught,
As the Worlds present Learning far out-goes;
Nor did the Master, who designed the Draught,
And for a Dia'dem put it on Loves brows,
E're think it would his Sacred Head expose;
Tho be 'it a Fillet, what can it intend?
What teach, but that, which every Lover knows,
Eve'ry true Lover, that to choose a Friend,
Barely by sight, 's beginning where scarce Fools would end.
X.
His Eyes were therefore hid (if hid they were)
From all commerce in Love to' exclude the Eyes;
Which judging, as the Object does appear,
Too often impose, imposed by Flatteries,
False mirrors of an Heart, which deeper lies:
The Heart, where Love that is true does first begin,
By Reason guided, its own worth to 'apprize;
Then by Discretion, seld in Lovers seen,
Who still the more's the outward glare, see least within.
XI.
Eyes Intellectual he' had, wherewith agree
Those Wings, which they no less unto him gave;
I Will Furniture for Love, if Blind he be,
Who rather then would want a Guide to have,
Himself from dangers unforeseen to save:
But love himself alone was his own Guide,
Nor needed any others Conduct crave,
And with his Wings spread, and extended wide,
A flight to Heaven attempted, none durst dare beside.
XII.
For why no Love it is, whose vile desires,
Incline it downwards to the Miry Ground;
This Earth of ours, which Sottices inspires,
Preying on Carnage there, or made, or found,
And with ignoble thoughts does most abound:
But that is true Love, and does deserve the Name,
Whose noble Acquists, nothing mean can bound;
And mindful of the Region, whence it came,
Thither aspiring terminates with Heaven its Fame.
XIII.
Like Wings, like Quiver: With bright Arrows filled,
Of different sorts, but with like Mystery,
As is well known to them, who in Love are skilled,
Well taught, what Motions in the Mind there be,
And how both Love, and Hatred there agree;
Hatred of all that bears the' impress of ill,
With love of all that is good, in its degree;
The Choques and just fixed bias of the Will,
Which make up Love, and all its various parts fulfill.
XIV.
The Golden pyl'd its Inclination proved,
Leaden Aversion, never to be denied;
Or if thereof there should be Question moved,
The Torch Love bore, his other Arms beside,
Too bright did shine the Mystic Truth to hide,
That Soul of the World, that all things keeps entire,
In Life, and Motion, nobly Typifi'd;
Noblest of Elements, but yet raised hi'gher
Than place'd at first, when made Loves noblest Symbol, Fire.
XV.
Thus by the Ancients Love was first expressed,
And, tho a Child, their God most ancient owned;
Older than Saturn, whom Jove dispossessed
Of Right usurpt, and in his stead was Crowned:
Son of Urania, (as in Books is found)
Venus Urania, whom we Heaven call
With them, who their Mythologie expound,
Distinct from that Pandeme, who was Thrall
To Vulgar Souls, and had from the Earth Original.
XVI.
Two unlike Mothers of two Sons unlike,
Eros and Anteros, whose opposed Name,
(Which different Sentiments in them did strike)
From their own first great Opposition came,
That from their Nature; and tho judged the same,
In one, and the other Sex, where they preside,
The Male, and Female Hymeneal Flame,
Are two so, that once kindled they divide,
Just like those Fatal Brethrens, who Dead, each defied.
XVII.
Eros was Elder, and the stronger too;
Of Constitution likely to endure
Eternal Ages, if the Spi'rit he drew,
Were not empoyson'd by an Air impure;
Of which consulting how to be secure,
'Twas plainly told him from the Oraculous Throne,
Than that of Themis far more Old, and sure,
A Tripode, which never failed, When two are One,
Then Love in danger is by Love to be undone.
XVIII.
Of two made one, Love well enough conceived,
For that himself had seen, himself had done;
But it by Love could hardly be believed,
That he to' himself so contrary should run,
As to provoke, what most he sought to shun:
Against himself that he should so prevail,
As thence in hazard be, to be undone;
This ev'en to Love, obscured in Mortal Vail,
Abstruse did seem, and by another sai'd might fail.
XIX.
But Time, a little time the Mystery cleared,
When by another Love, this Anteros,
He was betrayed; who that which first appeared,
(And 't was the Sun) when from the Earth he rose,
Adored as his chief God, with sacred Vows;
And many Altars to it after made,
By 'his Votaries, on whom he did impose
The Task Idolatrous, and First Fruits pai'd,
Himself the first Idolater, there profanely laid.
XX.
From Earth first rose this feigned, and Idol Love,
By DIS begot; tho not till after known
Who was his Sire, or what himself would prove;
Of a cursed Father the as cursed Son,
Born Armed for others Ruin, and his own;
With all the Ensigns Love was wont to bear,
By the Heathen read, that if together shown,
All but themselves to' impose on they would go near,
And here is the true Love one would say, and one he is there.
XXI.
Like Youth in both, like Face, and Looks were seen
Like naked Limbs, with covered Impudence
In this, but gesture tending to obscene,
In a free gayety 'hid, to' elude the Sense;
Like Chaplet, for their Brows, and Eyes defense;
Like Wings, like Quiver hung their Backs adown;
Like different Arrows to be drawn out thence;
Like burning Torch, which Flames so like did crown,
Either would seem the true, if single, and alone.
XXII.
Ah! that they had like Grace those Arms to use!
But this was loudly by the False decry'd,
Who only took them, that he might abuse,
The World, by whom he should be Deifi'd,
And acts inglorious in that Visor hide:
Never to use them was his full intent,
Or if he did, not as before well tried;
But to a Love, and Learning different,
Where Sense alone should be supreme in Government.
XXIII.
Love saw all this (for what to Love is hid?)
The true Love saw it, and withdrew in haste;
No place was left him on the Earth to' abide,
Wherefore he to the Empyrean past,
And only Conscience his Vicegerent placed:
With Promise yet, that if in time to come,
Man wiser grew (the Counterfeit uncas'd)
Himself his now left Charge would reassume,
And once again to Man return, as his last Home.
XXIV.
For Man, it is known, scarce the wild Phanto'sm saw,
This Pageant Counterfeit of Love Divine,
But his Allegiance back he straight did draw,
And to the Enemies part from Love decline,
By Sense led, which had quickly sunk a Mine,
Reason, or to surprise, or to overthrow;
And the vain Stratagem had dressed so fine,
With umbrage, that it Good, and Ill should know,
The Fort surrendered was, scarce known for what, or how.
XXV.
But up 'twas given, and therewith given was all,
(For Nothing, or what is worse than nothing, lies)
What ever Man his own, by right might call,
Or by Commission, but sure never did prize
Deserv'dly, since he' it could so soon despise;
His Life, his Soul, what most was of behoof,
To' a Being so disposed, and framed, as His;
Of his Obedience the first easy proof,
And what, as Heaven it self was valuable, his Love.
XXVI.
All were give'n up, and now adieu, blessed Love!
Adieu all Happiness, and Innocence!
Honor, and Virtue, which the same Course move,
And Mortals very rarely visit since!
Adieu unto you all, for Love's gone hence,
And only left your empty Names behind,
Upon the Stage to please, or give offense,
As variously Spectators are inclined,
But wherewith most are pleased, as most against Love combind.
XXVII.
Honor and Love adieu! And now my Song,
Since thou have trace'd them to their first abode,
Rest they a while; and tell, as does belong
To the Mock-Love, the World's, and ^coples God!
But make of every Rhyme an Iron Rod,
Wherewith thou may the Profligate chastise!
And tho thou go a way, as yet untrod,
Despair not but thy Work shall beauteous rise,
And with the Sober find acceptance, and the Wise!
XXVIII.
With these acceptance, but with others scorn,
Who to this Anti-Love blind Vassals made,
By our First-Fathers Act, have Fealty sworn
To a fell Tyran, who must be obey^d,
And will, nor in his furious heats gainsaid:
Unhappy Men, if their unhappiness
They could but know, and how they are betrayed;
Enough unhappy, would they but confess
The little that they know, which words can scarce express!
XXIX.
Slaves of Vile Passions, which drive furiously,
And all that is Sacred, with high Hand bear down;
Themselves, their Ancestors, the Deity,
Reason, and Reasons Guide, Religion,
The Worlds consent attested by their own;
Till to the Winds, and Seas their Faith they gave,
And sought, what else they could not flee, to drown
In bottomless Abyss, nor Shipwrackt have
The least security, they shall their Fraightage save.
XXX.
And yet who more secure? But this their Love,
Their New Love, in whose Service they 're retained,
Gives as his own, and their most urging Proof,
That they, true Conquerors, have the Victory gained,
And broke those Iro'ns, wherewith all else are chained:
Ill Education, brutal Thunders dread,
A fear of what is above, to which they 'are trained,
And what is to come, dreams of the long since dead,
That first made Gods, and what their Fears made, worshipped.
XXXI.
Horrors the Anti-diluvian World never knew,
Or if it did, durst not bare-face'd profess;
Tho from one Seed their Love, and Atheism grew,
Both Ills, but which the greater, which the less,
Is hard to say, almost as hard to ghess:
For either both, in both was either had,
In both, and either lost Man's Righteousness;
In The cause, or in the effect, both equal bad,
And both, that sensually, and this profanely mad.
XXXII.
But for Profaneness, Sense was the Harbinger,
And busily by Love prepared its way;
(For so I^ll call awhile the Worlds Troubler)
Who all its Stages did beforehand lay,
And longer here, and longer there would stay:
Till having the whole Earth compassed, and laid waste,
A Deluge came, and swept their Spoils away,
The Spoilers 'scaping with the few, who past,
By Ship to a new world, where Cham their Standard placed.
XXXIII.
Cham pitched it there; Cursed Cham who for the shame
Done to his Father, just Reproach did bear,
In that Addition to his cursed Name,
Servant of Servants, which his Race still wear:
But never did he Slave so much appear,
Of Slaves the verie'st Drudg, as when he Love
Permitted uncontrolled to domineer,
And lustful War against Heaven, and God to move,
And all below seduce, and defy all above.
XXXIV.
With him Love went (while yet the World was one,
One People, and one Language) unperceived,
And so great Victories by his Conduct won,
That He was openly at last received,
The first, that no more Worlds there were, who grieved:
And yet more Worlds he for his Triumphs found,
Or what is as good, new Coasts to be deceived;
When the divided Earth was Peopled round,
With distinct Nations sown, which stockt the new-broke Ground.
XXXV.
Each Nation was a several World, or as
A World distinct; the Islands most of all,
Which, late discovered, for new Worlds did pass
With those, who by that Name did Countries call,
Surrounded by the wide Seas rolling Wall:
And Love in every Land, and every Isle,
Did reign with Majesty Imperial,
And sway unbounded by the farthest Thyle,
Tho that, as the World's farthest bound, did stand erewhile.
XXXVI.
A mighty Prince, and curious Traveller,
Of sense most exquisite, from each to take,
Each Country, lay it farther off, or near,
What for the gust of Loving most could make,
And the o're-labour'd Appetite awake,
Satiate with loose disport, and rockt asleep,
Of not yet tasted Pleasures to partake;
Which in his dark Serraig'ls he close did keep,
And laid, till time should serve, as the Earths Center deep.
XXXVII.
At length it served; Ah! that it served not now!
And Love by his Conquests had such Subjects gained,
(For to his Yoke the Universe did bow,
And Arts, and Arms were to the Service trained)
That out of fear to lose what he' had obtained,
He only Trophies gave himself to rear,
And the Disguise put off he had sustained;
As the Supreme God would Himself appear,
And above all, that is else called God, his Head did bear.
XXXVIII.
Above the Sun, whom Himself worshipped,
But whom disgraded he to the Persian sent,
Next to himself to be propitiated,
In their Horned Mithra, which to Idols bent,
With thousand others, of obscure descent,
By this time Gentilism Gods had made,
New Deities industr'ious to invent;
Whom or in Hills, or thick Groves hallowed shade,
With Beasts, or Humane Blood they atoned, and to them prayed.
XXXIX.
Their Sons, or Daughters Blood for these sufficed,
But what to him they offered was their own;
Freedom, above their Life, to be appriz'd,
Which how they could disvalue well was shown
By this, if only this had been alone,
That when the Usurper did their Homage claim,
And in 'his true Colors made himself be known,
In throngs they to his Feet, and Altars came,
And kept, with Fires from their own Breasts, perpetual Flame.
XL.
Fires, which consumed, e're felt, the noblest Part,
And all that is good in Man, or great, laid waste,
By the Eyes conveyed most treacherously to the Heart,
Whence to the Liver soon the Burning past,
And Virtues Images in both defaced;
Thence to the Bones, nor did the Flesh 'scape long;
Till Soul and Body in a Flame at last,
The present God, to be denied too strong,
Plunged all into a Sea of Sulphur'ous Flame headlong.
XLI.
Like God, like Victim; To a God unclean,
Of Beasts the Uncleanest offered, Man turned Beast;
Himself the Sacrifice, and Priest obscene,
In which to minister he did divest,
All that above the Brute he once possessed,
And lower than the Brute unforce'd sank down;
With such Unmanly' Indignities oppressed,
He' had scorned them, as he might, by 'another done,
Her rude disgusts, whom he solicited, and his own.
XLII.
For why a SHE 's the Quarry, and the Game,
At which this Mock-Love, and his Haggards fly;
The Sex in either Sex, both worthy blame,
And tho distinguished first for ends more high,
Both equally debaucht by 'his Effrontry,
And acts so mad, and foolish (yet called Love,
Thereby engrossing the' whole Monopoly)
That one would think it should Indignation move,
Such Follies to commit, such Madness to reprove.
XLIII.
A Medly, with part Folly, Madness part,
And is the All of Love, Person, or Thing,
Or Act, or Powe'r, or by the Poets Art
However called, as he can Matter bring,
Of Love in Numbers and in Verse to sing;
And tending all, as Love 's in all the same,
By Images of diverse fashioning,
(If all are yet enough to' expose the shame)
One Spoiler to denote, and LEGION is his Name.
XLIV.
The hardest Devil to be dispossessed;
For e're he came the empty Rooms were swept,
By Idleness the' Housekeeper, and new dressed;
Idleness, only for that service kept,
And who, or so employed, or ever slept:
A brawny Carle, that never did work beside,
But here commanded forward briskly stepped;
And rather than be found unoccupi'd,
The Windows stopped, and all the several Lights did hide.
XLV.
But Fancy well enough did that supply,
Or little mist, Loves next forerunner light;
Who having once uprear'd his tender Eye,
And of the Object taken the distant height,
Made up the view all by Internal Sight:
And with new Beauties unperceived before,
But there displayed, himself did first delight;
Then opened, which he'had to him shut, the Door,
To let in loose Desire, and again tell them over.
XLVI.
So in Desire came, vainest of the Three,
And after him in Triumph rode the Fiend;
Whom seven Spirits, full as bad as he,
Did close behind in Mascarade attend,
And Io sang to Love, that Heaven did rend:
So sang they all, but with unequal grace,
As were their looks; for some their Brows did bend,
And grin'd most horrid, with distorted Face;
Others were blithe, and smiled as they along did pass.
XLVII.
These Folly were, and Mirth, and Dalliance,
Who hardly could their Way for laughing hold,
By Genius closed; the Three, who looked askaunce,
And midst their shouts could hardly cease to scold,
Were Lust, that scorned by the best to be controlled;
And Proteus Sin, who diverse Shapes put on,
As diverse kind, and Names he' had manifold;
And Death, the seventh from Love, mere Skeleton
One half, the other Fantastic clad as shall be shown.
XLVIII.
Folly the First, by' her Habit seemed a Maid,
And by her Face, which was excelling fair;
Tho whether such, was difficult to be said,
Nor safe it may be, since few Men there were,
But in her claimed with Women equal share,
And ill had took't, had she pure Woman been:
A pretty thing to gaze on, but whose Air,
And Gate, and Gesture, made it quickly seen,
She none of the Wisest was, and somewhat lackt within.
XLIX.
Light Gesture, Gate ungraceful, composed Air,
Save when she spoke, or laughed; but then betrayed
A thousand Follies, with prodigious glare;
For by a Glass she with a Shadow played,
(Her self, to whom she frequent Honors made)
And every glance, which she designed to cast,
And every look, by that in order laid;
And to such troublesome Impertinence past,
That every little Word she thereby formed at last,
L.
Mirth was a Youth of beautiful regard,
With cheerful Eyes, plump downy Cheeks, and Chin;
And nothing in his looks, or strange, or hard,
That, if one by the Face could ought divine,
All Beauties there amass't did seem to shine:
All that can Man become, or Love excite
In Loves great Critics, the Sex Feminine;
All but in the Timorous, whom his Whip did fright,
And more deterr, than all his other Charms invite.
LI.
For in his Hand a Bloody Discipline,
With many a' pointed Rowel stuck, he bore;
And wherewith, when unmark't, he saw his time,
Backward reflecting, he his Shoulders tore,
And the smooth Channel filled with purple gore:
But when or Folly called, or Dalliance,
The fretting exercise he soon gave over,
And, as recovered from a sullen Trance,
Met with quick Eyes, and amorous Look each smiling Glance.
LII.
Him Dalliance followed next, a Damsel gay,
Of light behavior, as she well could feign;
And wantonly her Breast did open lay,
The Lover who came next, to entertain;
Tho who the He were of her mighty Train,
She was not much solicitous to know,
Nor much to fancy him her self did pain;
For she like Favors did on all bestow,
And bonnour was to all, how high so e're or low.
LIII.
A Nightgown was the Habit which she wore,
Loosely clapped round her, but so airy thin,
That through its light disguise appeared the more,
What she never strove to hide, her beauteous Skin,
And just proportion of each curious Limb:
With Impudence too luscious to be told,
And speech Lascivious, when she did begin,
Which none, but like her self, unchaste, and bold,
Or unreprov'd could hear, or unasham'd behold.
LIVE.
Next after her in order Genius came,
Of Body somewhat gross, but Humor free;
Whom part called Comus, as by his Surname,
Tho both, or either with him well agree,
Without whom Love, nor merry Life can be:
A right good Fellow, as his Belly showed,
Which in a Swath reached almost to his Knee,
And made him passage through the admiring Crowd;
Which shouting to him louted, as to them he bowed.
LV.
No wrinkle in his Counte'nance did appear,
Nor careful thought seemed to come near his Mind,
Of what should be; but things, which present were,
Variously turned him, as did sit the Wind,
And this way now, now that way he inclined:
Tho if 'twere still, (and sometime still it lay)
Diversions to himself he would make, or find;
And sometimes only muse a live-long Day,
Tho asked on what, he or naught knew, or naught could say.
LVI.
These were the fairest Shows Loves entrance had,
And of the Pomp the sightliest Officers,
Who therefore next the Carr Triumphal staid,
But Spirits Incarnate were, and all as fierce,
Provoke'd once, as those Fatal Ministers
Of his, and more than his, of the Wrath Divine,
Which followed next, with look, and mien perverse;
A Grisly, Horrid, and Prodigious Trine,
Which hardly into shape, Love could by 'his Art refine.
LVII.
Lust was the First, but whether Man, or Beast,
Or He, or She, one could by no means know,
For it both Sexes had, and did invest
Mankind above, and Beast mishape'd below,
And slote divided did for ostent show:
With shaggy Hair the' whole Body covered over,
And poisonous stench, which he around did throw,
Undampt by the Perfumes, which the Satyr bore,
(For so they called him) and about him ever wore.
LVIII.
A very Satyr, whom he nearest came,
In Face, and Guise, but in Deformity
Excelled, the first of the' Family, and Name;
And shameless was his Look, and lewd his Eye,
But sharp withal, Beauties which cloistered lie,
First to discover, then to circumvent,
By Clamor, wherein lowed he was and high,
Nor could forbear, as he in Triumph went;
Prime Vizier of the Port, and Loves chief Confident.
LIX.
Sin followed him, who was his Eldest Son,
And only Child, with place and dignity,
His Parents Titles suiting, and his own;
But on his own he mostly did rely,
And all, but what his own was, did decry;
Would, and did loudly against Lust declame,
As Impotent, courageous to defy,
But who to Handy-blows, or never came,
Or not with such effect, as he, to get a Name.
LX.
For tho but one, a thousand Heads he had,
And twice a thousand Hands boldly to fight;
An Army of himself, and which he made
Greater, or less, as the Cause did invite;
(Love, and Loves Good Old Cause was his delight)
Rebellion, whose design to carry on,
Himself he variously, as 'it hapt, would dight,
A Beasts, or Man's form take now, and anon
Angels, or Fiends, a multitude, one be, or none.
LXI.
But Death the third, the same shape always kept,
If Shape it might be called, that shape had none,
Except in that half of him, which foremost stepped
And to the view exposed a side of Bone,
That seemed with Skin to have been clothed upon,
And Musculage, not many Days before;
For scarcely cleansed was the Skeleton,
And here and there appeared fresh stains of Gore,
And gobbets of green flesh, which from the joints he tore.
LXII.
To'ther half was the Universe, and all,
And every thing, that in the World is found,
Which hastens, or is ready at Death's call,
And are the Ingredients, which he does compound,
Or single, or in Mass to give the Wound:
A dreadful Mixture, and of which to tell,
Almost to think, would the greatest Wit confound;
For since the time that Man from Happi'ness fell,
They were collecting, and had at the bottom Hell.
LXIII.
Where e're he came, these were Loves Company,
With Train and Baggage, which did far extend;
And Meny suiting so great Prince as He,
For Prince and God they called him, tho Pure Fiend:
Unquiet Care, which all his time did spend
Himself to' undo, backed by Suspicion,
Then Impudence, which did to Lechery lend
His unchaste Ear, and Fury bad come on,
By Riotice drawn up, and Irreligion:
LXIV.
Without door Danger, and Distrust did wait,
And Fear, that never was himself at rest,
Or others would permit their Watch to 'abate;
And Jealousy, which tho he were possessed
Of what he loved, for rage tore his own Breast;
And Lust unnatural, and Villainy,
And Revellings, in thousand Anticks dressed;
And Poverty, in Rags clad piteously,
Calling aloud for Death, which did the unhappy fly.
LXV.
It fled him, as one, who from Love was fled,
Under the Disc'ipline, if he had the skill
To use it right, of Sorrow, seeming dead,
But which for a blessed Life prepares Our will,
By that Repentance, which Shame does instill;
Repentance the first step to Innocence,
Whose various parts it makes, or does fulfill:
But whereof Lustful Minds have little sense,
Till Shame sum up the Total of the vast expense.
LXVI.
Death such a Bankrupt therefore flies,
Hasting to those, who called, or called him not,
By Loves own Hands crowned for the Sacrifice,
And or pursue'd, or in the pursuit were hot,
E're well aware to the end of all things got,
By Death inglorious, and with Infamy;
(Of most Luxurious Livers the hard lot)
Yet which Love colored with such Maistery,
That the most followed, what the most did seem to fly.
LXVII.
For Love had thousand Deaths at his command,
And every Lover, might his own Fate make,
Which some did, but by'a way so underhand,
That from the praise of Love it much did take,
And many 'a Lover lost he for their sake;
Tho such he pleaded were by him given over,
(If all might be believed, which then Love spoke)
Nor could to his account be reckoned more,
Than if thus, or a Natural Death they died before.
LXVIII.
Be the Point therefore, as it will for me,
Who list not further of it here to tell;
Enough are Lovers Deaths we daily see,
(And so 'twas then) a Songs scant bounds to swell*
Nor yet for Love, or them contrived so well,
But that one midst the Pomp might easily find
The Mighty, by whose cruel Hands they fell;
And Verdict give the Murder was designed,
By the Pains they felt before, Reproach they left behind.
LXIX.
Pains more than can of Mortal Tongue be told,
And sharper than e're Tyran did invent;
Which the whole Man did in strait Fetters hold,
Till tortured Nature, quite worn out, was spent,
Of Love the Guerdon and the Punishment:
Yet Tyrans Racks found out, the Pale, and Wheel,
And Fire, and all that can by Fire torment,
Or be prepared, the ^x, and derr-doing Steel,
But make no wounds all, set with those which Lovers feel.
LXX.
Thrice, and than thrice more wretched state of Love,
When Innocence and Truth to Heaven were gone!
But seve'n times wretcheder it yet did prove,
When this Mock-love wholly usurpt the Throne,
(As he 'after did) and single reigned alone,
With Name and Power alike usurpt, yet was,
Ah! What not was he? -- But it is time to' have done
With him, who can to Verse give little grace,
And in another Canto to the True Love pass.
(...). THE LEGEND OF LOVE.
CANTO III.
I.
TIRE'D with the Way I have already gone
Longer by many a Stage, than setting out
I thought it would have proved, and where there is none
To guide me, in the search I am about,
