How well I shall succeed is my great doubt,
Who almost of the Enterprise repent,
Wishing I better of it first had thought;
At least before me some Espials sent,
Who might have told the hazard of the bold Attempt.
II.
But to repent (alas!) it is now too late,
And in the last Act fail, a wrong to Love,
Which in another I should surely hate,
And cannot in my self unblam'd approve,
What Arguments so e're Distrust may move;
Which many, cogent seem, and weighty all,
But all which by this single^One I reprove,
That well if well, if ill succeed I shall,
It Is not inglorious from the noblest heights to fall.
III.
But help me Love, and I Will not yet despair!
For other Muse I dare not invocate;
None but thy Self, with whom the Treasures are
Of bright Idaeas, tho discovered late
To me, who half my time in Darkness sat,
Ylamped only by a Foolish Fire,
Whose wandering guidance I now deprecate,
Led by it often, and my vain desire,
To whence I could not till Thy Day brake out, retire.
IV.
At length it brake out, and I came to know
How woefully I had mistook my Way;
Shine forth again with double Glory now,
And in my Verse Thy fairest Beams display,
That others with me in it triumph may:
And having their Night Sullys thence refined,
Carol with sacred Hymn's to the Beauteous Day;
Carol to Thee, by Heaven, and God designed,
The Counterfeit to' uncase, and Eyes restore the Blind.
V.
LONG had the Mock-love, by his false disguise,
Upon the Credulous World imposed, but more
Upon himself, if he had been so wise
To think how much 'twould cost him to restore,
And, what by tort he' had snatched, a new pay over;
But this (alas!) came seldom in his thought,
Rather perverse still, as he was before,
The World, which he had into Bondage brought,
Eternally to' enthral, was the only Prize he sought.
VI.
This was his aim, nor to rebate it found,
Save Prophecies of a Supplauters race;
Which well he knew not, and could worse expound,
Wherein the happy Country was the place,
Whence was foretold should come his great disgrace;
But when, or how, tho himself Oracles gave,
Too hard to be resolved was the dark case;
Nor could he any certain knowledge have,
Who should the Mighty Conqueror be, his Thralls to' unslave.
VII.
The happy Country well enough he knew,
Part of his Syria to be 'hight Palestine;
Wherefore he thither his Chief Forces drew,
And seized it first by 'a more than double Trine
Of cursed Nations, from the Great Chams Line,
Cham, who first gave him credit by his Arms,
And then his Empire to him did resign;
Cham, whom he thus rewarded for the harms
He had sustained, to be Camp-Master of his Charms.
VIII.
Seven cursed Nations, of his cursed Seed,
To be its constant Guard Love thither sent;
Who ^^ll'd the Land so with their cursed Breed,
That scarce was left him room for his own Tent,
Less for new Col'onies, if such thither went:
A stup*d, bestial, and unmanly Rout,
That all their Age in Lusts unnatural spent;
Till the time came their Land should spue them out,
Too long oppressed, and bring the dread Foresight about.
IX.
Lov^ saw it coming, and began to fear,
When Jacob's numerous Host, from Bondage led,
Unto the Cananitish bounds drew near,
Seizing the Nations with unusual Dread,
While Seas to make them way rose up and fled;
But never did he more confounded stand,
Than when he Jordan saw recoil to 'his Head,
And to new Armies shared by Lot his Land,
Supplanted e're he did the Supplanters understand.
X.
Before he doubted those would prove the Men,
And therefore when they were upon the Way,
From Madian dressed a Female Stratagem,
By which above two Myriads slaughtered lay,
Tho Madian for it after dear did pay,
And he who gave the Counsel with them fell.
Balam their Priest and his the Sword did slay,
To expiate for the Whoredoms he did sell,
And more advise, than by Prophetic rage foretell:
XI.
But then he knew it, and in every Age,
As Israel did in wealth and power increase,
New Wars would with the Holy People wage,
Wars Amorous, the sad result of Peace;
Nor his Assaults defeated often surcease;
Till David was exalted to the Throne,
With Testimony that he God did please,
As Enoch had, and then Love gave for gone
All he before had gained, and by his Conquests won.
XII.
Ah! that it had been gone! and that his sway
Had here expired! But Jesses Son soon fell,
A victim at his Altars, and a Prey;
Wherewith, and with his Pride he so did swell,
That tho he after had not much to tell,
Nor much to boast more, during his whole Reign,
Eluded was he judged the Oracle,
Nor could there for him ought behind remain,
But what He thus had got, as happ'ly to maintain.
XIII.
This Salomons youth did easily persuade,
( David's next Successor) who tho most wise,
(Love of the Wisest never was yet afraid)
Had other Grandures, which might chance to rise
Higher in Loves account, well placed, than his:
Infinite Riches, Peaceful Government,
The Necks, and Pleasures of his Enemies;
And, what then these was more, a mind intent,
Of Folly, and of Love to make the Experiment.
XIV.
He made it, and in making it was caught,
To his Lands baleful Ruin, and his own;
And both, by him that, he by Love ill taught,
As from a Precipice together thrown,
Never stopped till come to the bottom Headlong down*
Unhappy Prince, who could not single fall!
Whose smiles appeared more fatal than his frown;
The Kingdoms Laws this, for the Criminal,
But bad example those, which further reached to all!
XV.
And now, if ever, Love to the' top was got
Of his unmeasurable Soveraigntie;
So far above his Hope and deepest Plot,
That scarce could he believe what he did see,
And almost trembled in those heights to be,
Compared with which the Honors he had gained,
In the World Heathen, were a low degree;
For there he over the brutish only reigned,
The Noblest, and the Wisest here to 'his Lore were trained.
XVI.
Even God himself most High to serve was made;
And his first Institution, Marriage,
Cement of holy Love, so base allayed,
That it degen'rated to Bestial rage,
And more divided, than it did engage
To mutual Amors and joined Hearts consent:
Madness before begun, but which this Age,
With ancient Rites, indulged long, not content,
Perfected by a new, and wild Establishment,
XVII.
Of sharers infinite in the Marriage Bed,
By this false Loves prime Ministers brought in;
With Artifice, at first, close palliated,
The New-made World, which was but peopled thin,
To stock amain and plausibly begin:
Hence Bigamy, and then Polygamy,
Divorce next, to them near of kin,
On every small dislike, did multiply,
And Children reck'ned were of Loves huge Family.
XVIII.
And so it stood, even in the Holy Race,
(From the Beginning tho it were not so)
For many Ages of permissive Grace,
But more of Hardness that in the Heart did grow
Of those, who no delight in One would know,
And whom Variety would only please:
Yet God at first but One designed, to show
His, and the power of Love, and if Disease
Abused 't should after be, its Remedy and Ease.
XIX.
(For more, if more are sought the drought to allay,
But like strange waters to the Hydropic prove,
And frequent draughts, which in the Bowels stay,
And nor the Thirst, nor the Disease remove;
And such is Change to him, who Change does love,
(The more, the worse) He drinks, and thirsts again,
And still the more he drinks, does more improve
His Thirst, the very remedy is pain,
Yet neither can shake off, of neither but complain.)
XX.
And that Disease 'twould prove, in his Foresight,
Abused once, the Almighty knew, and thence
To 'a single Circle bound the subtle Sp'rit,
Who, one transgrest, was Proof against all fence,
And easi'ly could with Just and Fit dispense;
One Partner in Chaste Nuptials, as enough
Their Ornament to be, and their Defense,
By Sin prevented, all that was above,
Or two, or more, or many, came from this False Love.
XXI.
From him they came All, but successively,
And even the Many yet were but a Few,
Compared with that excess, which grown most high,
In Salomon's Reign, no limits had, or knew,
And into the Band, not One, but Hundreds drew:
Seven hundred Wives, Three hundred Concubins,
Whom the Uxorious King did close pursue,
A Female Army, under Love's Ensigns,
With Treasure infi'nit drein'd, and with exausted Loins.
XXII.
Prodigious Astoreth, be 'it an He, or She,
Or both, or neither, an opprobrious Stock;
For Sex in Idols is a Nicetie
Unknown to the Makers, whom it never did choque
To style as they should please the senseless Block:
Tho if they called it by a Female Name,
More Folly, and less Power, they if ere did yoke;
And, if need served, a Male the God became,
Their own to 'attemper, and expose the Votaries shame.
XXIII.
Be it prodigious therefore, and a She!
And a She this Mock-Love, if so it please!
Prodigious Astoreth! and prodigious He,
Who was her Slave, and Slave to 'a thousand She Is,
On the Rack put by 'his Lusts, or Marriages!
And if it is true, that each a Body makes,
(And true it is) how monstrous with all these,
Must his appear, who has so different stakes,
Where each with only 'him, he with each, and all partakes?
XXIV.
With all partook he, and made up with all
One monstrous Body, which did all contain;
All Lands, and Languages, from the huge Wall
Of Babylon, to where Nile bedds the Main,
Lusty in Egypts Spoils, with Pompous Train:
No Country was there, but sent in some Love,
No Love, but did its Countries Gods retain.
All which he served, if he were Service-proof,
Enough to satiate, not to satify enough.
XXV.
Marriage perverted thus from its design,
Love to enhance, and raise an Holy Seed,
To hinder Whoredoms, and the Rage confine
Of an unruly Passion, which did need
More Reins than Spurs, and of Hells fiercest breed;
But above all, nobly to typify
The Mystic Union, which shall intercede
'Twixt Christ and holy Church, Man and the Most High,
All Mischiefs thence brake in, and all Idolatry.
XXVI.
Thence Whoredoms, Fornications thence brake in,
And, which the Holy People did or'eflow,
Rapes, Incests, and than Incest worse, the Sin
Confusion called, foul Sod'omy, and a row
Of bestial Vices, which 'twere guilt to know,
But more to tell; The 'unwary Course some take
Lust to perstringe by action, or by show,
But teaching more, what does no teaching lack,
And those whose Ign'orance, their whole Innocence does make.
XXVII.
Yet Israel acted all, and did outdo,
Well thew'd in Villainies, their Teachers skill;
And Men with Men, with Women Women too
All Shame put off, and did both parts fulfill,
With Sexes counterfeited, (every Hill,
And Grove with Humane Beasts, and mingled Blood
Promiscuous cast, replenisht thick) the while
Brutes themselves, as less salvage, wondering stood,
Man over them made to reign, should with them change Manhood.
XXVIII.
No wonder thus debased, new Gods they chose,
Gods like themselves, and they new Gods did choose,
Prodigious as their Lusts, on whom they'impose
Horrors the Beast would, if it could, refuse,
Designed for Service and a nobler use:
Baalim and Astaroth, or whatever name
Those Generals include, bleeding Thamuze,
Dagon, and Moloch, Kemos, Moabs shame,
And thousand others, which into the Bead-roll came:
XXIX.
All which they worshipped, and did lowly bow
Before their Altars; lowest bowed their King,
Who Temples made them, and did first allow
Their Rites absurd, (by his strange Wives brought in,
Authentic made, when their establishing
Owned him as Founder) and some think that Verse
Then lustful Orgias first began to sing,
And prostituted Mysteries rehearse,
Which Fabulous Greece did after through the World disperse.
XXX.
Verse it is sure, did early suffer wrong;
And Tyran Love debaucht it, as he' had done
God's noblest Creature, for the chains are strong,
With which he holds it yet, as if his own
It purely were, and were enough alone
All that he else has lost back to regain,
And whence he had been tumbled, to the Throne
Once more advance him, in his Hands the Rein
Of all things, midst an holier People, put again.
XXXI.
It served a little then, its service now
Is wondrously encreast, since Sloth, and Age
Have heightened Vice, and made Verse to it bow;
To the Gods before it was in Vassalage,
And only knew the Pythonesses rage,
Or Priests inspired, to all beside most chaste,
To Virtue did, and to true Worth engage;
And if the Object had been rightly placed,
Might for a real Virtue, and true Grace have past.
XXXII.
But now, (what quickly after it begun)
Retaining nothing but the Ill 'it has gained,
By Lux, and Travel, it does counter-run
To all that is Good, or Honest, or maintained
In The Civil Worlds esteem, with Atheism stained,
(As the next step to many Gods is none)
And all Impiety, has place obtained
With the worst Men, and is so furious grown,
That in its fits it God and King defies alone.
XXXIII.
These it defies, and dare unmasked profess,
(Where'it may be free, and to its full pitch flown)
What e'ven to think abashed mere Heathenness;
Nay when reserved most, and most modest grown,
'Twill others Follies sing, and make its own,
And than the Wise much rather please the Vain:
But since its present State too well is known
To be concealed, my self I shall not pain
To tell it here, or further, tho provoked complain.
XXXIV.
Ah! that it would it self, or could complain!
And of the Lusts, to which it did submit
Unwillingly at first, nor without pain,
But willingly when Custom humbled it,
Betrayed by 'its false and treacherous Mi'gnon Wit:
Her Mistress Verse the treacherous Maid beguiled,
The false Love her; and up they both did set
One shameless Brothel, wanton that, this wild,
Till both of Honor, Virtue, and true Love were spoiled.
XXXV.
Despoiled they were, despoiled, what they did serve,
Honor, and Virtue, and the Love Divine,
And Wedlock, which did covertly preserve
Some Traces of a Vigor Masculine,
Which Lust could never to its Beck incline
So perfectly, but it resistance made,
Weak as it was, and sprung a Counter-mine:
But now aside all Enmities were laid,
And all with one consent did plot Heav^n to invade.
XXXVI.
Which as the Almighty saw into his Mind,
That Mind of his it came, which ever love
To 'his Works did bear, to Man was ever kind,
The madness of such Faytours to reprove,
And what none else could do, the Cause remove;
The cause was this Mock-Love, whom to debase,
Into his Mind it came, by him, who strove
So high to raise him, and who what he was,
Of all Men best did know, did all in Loves surpass.
XXXVII.
Wise Solomon, who when he long had tried
The vain Experiment, at length grew Wise;
Nor longer with him would the secret hide,
Than till the Almighty opened had his Eyes,
And to him cleared the blessed Discoveries:
Till his Repentance had his Loves effaced,
And Credence gained us, that, immerst to rise
Tho hard it be, the Doom may not be past,
If what is too long one Day deferred, yet come at last.
XXXVIII.
Him his great Prophet God most high did raise,
Turning the Tenor of his inspired String,
Of Ancient Honor, and of Future Praise,
But most of Love, the Cause, and end to sing,
And hidden Mysteries to the Light to bring;
The mighty Works the Almighty's Self would do,
For Loves, and his BELOVEDS ransoming:
All which he did to the Royal Prophet show,
And all which, by him taught, his Israel came to know.
XXXIX.
Not as those Works, when finished perfectly
Should be displayed, but as became their state,
And Him, who was, restored, the Type to be
Of all the Glories, which he should relate;
By Figures, and by Shadows adaequate
To Humane Reasonings, and Discourse finite;
By his changed Loves a Love to adumbrate,
Which cannot else be seen by Mortal Light,
God's Love to Man, which different Natures should unite.
XL.
That Love, which God in time from Heaven should bring,
With Man to dwell, and as true Man appear;
Which Man no less, of all his Works the King,
To Heaven, excluded thence by' his Fall, should bear,
And on his Wings triumphantly up-rear:
And all the way, as he does thither rise,
With Idees fill him of the Beauties there;
That Love, in fine, which does all Loves comprize,
Whence Man to 'his Maker lives, his Maker for him dies.
XLI.
Of all which Marriage is the Sacrament;
(Or Symbol call it, if the Name displease)
The closest Union to represent,
'Twixt God and Man, 'twixt Man and Happiness;
And if there closer Union be than these,
Or more (and whether more be meant who knows?
Tho closer none) in one Term all to express;
Marriage, which once confirmed by holy Vows,
Is Loves Reprizal, and in one all Unions shows.
XLII.
So God most High resolved, so Solomon,
By him inspired did carnal Love impeach
Of highest Treason, and Rebellion;
The first, who plainly did the Mystery preach,
And what himself, but late learnt, others teach:
That Love alone, whose long and outstretcht Line,
Through Natures Works, to Natures end does reach,
Their Love, whom God, not Lust or Interest join,
Unequal tho it be, comes nearest the Divine.
XLIII.
As near as was, or fit for us to know,
Or possible, in Mortal Flesh immured;
(Tho God himself the Form would take below)
When brighter Vision could not be endured,
Nor Loves invisible taught else, or secured;
For such is Man, tho of God's Works the chief,
Of things Invisible to be assured,
That from things Visible he must receive
The imperfect Image, and of God by' himself conceive.
XLIV.
His Bodies structure, and his Souls great Powers,
Both which as having God himself propounds;
Tho Acts Organical are purely Ours,
And he the Deity by the shift confounds,
Who makes it what the Parable expounds,
With Senses, and with Parts corporeal,
Loving like us, and with a Lovers Wounds,
Which from the Deity are excluded all,
And only used, that under Sense those Acts may fall.
XLV.
Yet so God loves, so would be known to Love,
As Love in the Marriage Bed, kept undefil^d,
Might figure best, if one the Vail remove,
Our Nature, which of Innocence dispoyl^d,
Till raised, whence sunk, beguiles and is beguiled;
But once restored, is worthy Him, and Us,
Him still to love, with Love us to be filled,
(Of Grace not Debt) become both Amorous,
The Churches Husband He, the Church his Bride and Spouse.
XLVI.
Thus loving, and beloved thus shame on those,
Whose either Atheism, or Impiety,
Dare the Tremendous Figure, or expose,
Or subject make it of foul Raillery,
And to vile Lust embase the Mystery!
That of their Happ'ness have so little sense,
Their God, their Souls, and their Civility,
That they with things most sacred can dispense,
And rather than not give it, take from Heaven offense!
XLVII.
From Thee, most sacred and inspired Song,
The humblest Condescension of Heavens King,
From which my roving Verse has strayed too long,
Led by the Mock-love, and now late does sing;
Next that the humblest, which with Saffron Wing,
Gabriel, the True-loves mighty Harbinger,
Foretold to the' EVER-VIRGIN, e're the thing
Was full accomplished, and thereof did bear,
When done first News, and what the signs to know him were.
XLVIII.
Signs too unlikely, till by Angels told,
Whereby the Maker of all things to find;
For who would think a simple Cratch should hold
Eternal Majesty, mean Swathings bind
The Incomprehensible, and Unconfined;
And that an Ox, and Ass were company,
(To Scorn enured, and labor by their kind)
In an Inns-Stable fit for God Most High,
And that a Babe should be that God, and exposed lie?
XLIX.
Yet signs they were with Heaven which suited best,
And best with God, when God should Flesh appear;
And so was Wedlock, thence to be expressed,
E're that time came, the Image he should bear,
Or rather what our selves, allied to' him near,
(Nearer by Purchase, than Creation)
Should thence become; like Glories with him wear,
And since to us it could not else be known,
Till Man to Heaven should rise, high Heaven to Man bring down.
L.
By Love to bring it: And by Love HE brought it,
Who all the Mysteries of Love did know;
Second alone to Loves dear self, who taught it,
As or above it was, or as below,
And in thee, Song, its Mystic Power did show:
Not as thy Words to 'us sound, but as thy Sense,
To the Church applied, by ^oly Churches Vow,
Must be expounded, with this Difference,
Of God it is Figure All, of Man all Innocence,
LI.
Of Soul and Body, but of Soul the most,
Whose Acts and Motions Thou do most intend,
By views Material, to our Light disposed,
But where the Matter does all Act suspend,
And shadows what it cannot comprehend;
Is nothing, or as nothing, how e're laid,
Compared with what comparison does transcend;
Nor meant at all, tho by it all is said,
That Is said of Love, which through its broken Pipe's conveyed.
LII.
So would Thou, Song, so must Thou be understood;
And short of this, who e're Thy Flight would bound,
To the Deity sacrifices Human Blood,
And fixes on the Impassible that Wound,
Which the Mock-love to offer had astound.
For He soon as he heard the words Divine,
His Sentence in them, and his Doom he found;
At which affrighted, back he did resign
All he before usurpt, nor ought had to rejoyn.
LIII.
Happy he so could escape, tho e're away,
Cast, and condemned, he into Exile went,
The True Love, who long waited for that Day,
Hymen his great Ambassador down sent,
To beg some Exemplary Punishment,
And Caution, that he would return no more:
But all the Caution, which to give he meant,
Was but his Word, nor longer that than Power
Should fail him to attempt, what he had done before.
LIVE.
For Punishment, 'twas yet too early Day,
To move, or hope; Himself enough had done,
By those Spoils, which unforce'd he down did lay,
The Jurisdiction of Heavens Court to own,
Whom that acknowledgment must serve or none:
And out he went, with stern, and bloody Eyes,
And bitter Railings on blessed Hymen thrown;
Who all his Railings did no less despise,
Content (since he no more could get) with the bare Prize,
LV.
Marriage restored to' its Just and Ancient Right,
And all the Intents, to which it was designed;
Marriage, which once secured, does Souls unite,
And made in Heaven, to Heaven so near is joined,
That only there we purer Love can find:
Marriage of th Fallen World the best Estate,
Marriage most Honorable with Mankind,
Which to abuse, a Man his Flesh must hate,
Marriage God's Blessing, when He gave over to create.
To the Muse, ODE PINDARIC.
I. STROPHE.
BUT whither bear Thou me, O Love,
Sovereign Disposer of all things,
Beyond the flight of Verses Wings,
Which after Thee a Course unequal move?
So swift Thy passage is, and wondrous light,
Scarce can I of Thee get the sight;
And all so here, and there,
So unconfined, and every where,
Appear Thou do, and disappear,
A Phantosm Thou would reckned be,
But that whole Nature lives alone by Thee.
I. ANTISTROPHE.
From Heaven to Earth, from Earth again
To Heaven, who has Loves Journey trace't?
Hail ye bright Stars, which saw him last,
And greater Luster by his grant obtain!
You last of all his parting Charge received,
And still to keep it are believed,
In times, and seasons thence,
Which you to all his Works dispense,
With kind and wholesome Influence;
And at his Thrones dread Footstool wait,
Illustrious Guards and Messengers of Fate.
I. EPODOS.
"Happy the Man, whose noble Soul
"Has raised him to a pitch so high,
"That he the Heavens, as they about him roll,
"Undazled can behold with curious Eye;
"And every Mystic note,
"That Is in their Mighty Volume wrote,
"As clearly understand,
"As if acquainted with no other hand:
"Yet happier He by far,
"Who like the Kings, first guided by a Star,
"Obtains like Royal Grace,
"Into the Sacred Presence, ushered thus, to pass!
II. STROPHE.
An Honor done but to a few,
As few there be, who care to join
Their quest to the search of Love Divine,
Thither applying, what from thence they drew;
Heavens Priests, and Loves, who have the lucky skill,
Both Services at once to fill;
Nor like the most all day,
Or Idle stand, or play,
And gaze though on Heaven their Age away:
Of Love, who largely boast the Name,
But never saw his Light, or felt his Flame.
II. ANTISTROPHE.
At once both Services he filled,
Whom the Muse sings, both knows the best
Of any, who e're those Gifts possessed,
In Books so different so divinely skilled;
Natures and God's, the Gloss that, this the Text,
And read together unperplext:
While that, by' it self alone.
Read, as too often is done,
But Altars rears to' a God unknown;
To the True this does a way provide,
But difficult to walk without a Guide.
II. EPODOS.
Not SETH, whose Pillars Fabulous
Survived the all-devouring Flood,
Taught the New World the Half he teaches us,
Were all he taught as well but understood:
Tho when of Love He treats,
And His high Mysteries repeats,
All hear, and what is heard
By all for certain Truth is so revered,
The ravished Soul resents
Its teadious stay, and future joys prevents;
Rapt in Ecstatic Fires
To the Divine Aboads, almost in the Flame expires.
III. STROPHE.
Witness Thou Sarums sacred Pile,
Which from the day Thou first came down
From the parcht neighboring Hill, the Town
Attending Thee, and shouting all the while,
None happier saw than that which made Thee His,
And Him Thine with united bliss.
Thy Streams, Thy Fields, Thy Towers,
By the' Graces led, and Hours,
Daunc'd to their Genius Crowned with Flowers:
Thy very Children bless that time
And their new Era from its date sublime.
III. ANTISTROPHE.
Not but that worthy Thee, and Verse,
There have been Praelats heretofore,
(Tho none so Worthy) had like store
Of sacred Poets been, as now rehearse.
But all (alas!) are in Oblivion drowned,
Unsung laid underground,
Because the Muses aid,
To all the Acquists they made,
Or lacking was, or else unpray'd:
And their fond hopes of Future glory,
Or to dumb Walls confined, or doubtful story.
III. EPODOS.
So Thy first Fathers, so others fare'd
Before, and since Thy great Translation;
And if to' escape 'twas not for some so hard,
The Works they wrote, were their Fames best Foundation;
In which an Age they live,
That Brass, and Marble shall survive.
So Jewel ever lives,
And life to' his very Enemy gives.
So shall Thy Reverend Lord
Long hence be Thine, and His own best Record.
Have to Him Muse, and take
At 'his Knees that blessing, which may Thee Immortal make.
To the Right Reverend FATHER IN GOD, SETH Lord Bishop of SARUM.
MINDFUL, whence first she learnt the early Skill,
In humble strains Almighty LOVE to sing;
And then of Her, to whom she' her Works does bring,
The CHVRCH, and College, both whose Names instill
Like Sentiments, and shall Fames Records fill
With Worthies, fit to 'employ the Noblest string
Of Heav'n-tun'd Verse, and almost tire its Wing,
To bear them, hence translated, up its Hill:
My Muse to You, my LORD, and your great Name,
This LEGEND of the one, and the other LOVE,
(As different as their Ends and Natures prove)
Doubly inscribes; that thence her purer Flame
To Heaven, so offered, may more grateful rise;
The grosser Parts be Wood for the Sacrifice.
FINIS.
A PARAPHRASE UPON Some Select HYMNS OF THE NEW and OLD TESTAMENT, With some
other Occasional Compositions IN English RIMES.

RIMES.
The VI great HYMNS of St. LUKE.
I. HYMN.
The ANGELS Salutation.
Ave Gratia plena. Lucae 1. 28.

HAIL of Heaven highly Grace'd,
Hail, O VIRGIN full of Grace!
Heavens Blessings all on Thee be place'd,
In whom Heavens GOD takes up his Place!
Blessed Thou among all Women be,
Blessed be Thy Name and Memory,
Blessed VIRGIN MOTHER be Thy Style to' Eternity!
II. HYMN.
The Salutation of ELIZABETH.
Benedicta Tu inter Mulieres. Lucae 1. 42.

I.
BLESSED Thou among all Women be,
And blessed the FRUIT, which Thou do bear!
But whence is this great Honor done to me,
That my LORD'S MOTHER should approach thus near
And greater joys suppressed at Home,
In mine to 'assist should hither come?
II.
Yet not at Home those greater Joys,
Tho there suppressed, could Thou wholly leave,
For scarce was heard Thy salutations Voice,
But my glad Womb the Signal did receive:
And there with joy the Babe did spring,
It Is present GOD to' attest, and King,
III.
Thrice blessed the Faith, which firm does hold!
For a Performance there shall be
Of all, that was by the Heavenly Message told,
And, or Thy happy FRUIT concerns, or Thee;
The Sender was the Eternal Lord,
Who having past will Crown his Word.
III. HYMN.
The Song of the BLESSED VIRGIN.
Et ait Maria, -- Magnificat anima, etc. Lucae 1. 47.

I.
MY SOUL does Magnify the Lord,
My Spirit in God my Savior does rejoice;
My Spirit his thankful Praises shall record,
And my Soul, thence awak't, provoke my Voice:
Nor Soul, nor Spirit, my Judgment, or my Will,
My Voice, or Lyre, shall in his Praise lie still,
But all my Powers I Will summon, and their noblest skill.
II.
For (Lo!) his Handmaids low estate,
He from on High has viewed with kind regard;
His Handmaids, to the increase of whose sad Fate,
The Royal Blood, from whence she sprang, pressed hard:
But now all Nations shall Record me BLESSED,
And David^s Seed, of David's Throne possessed,
To' his Blood the Glory shall return, to' his Land its rest.
III.
Great are the things, which He has done,
And done for me, who' Himself is great of Might;
Great tho He be, it is Holiness alone,
Wherein He of all Names does most delight:
All but of 's Mercy, which in him the same,
He to Gen'erations therefore does Proclaim,
And keep with them, who fear Him, and his Holy Name.
IV.
To all beside, by His Arm He is known,
And strength Almighty, which none can repel;
His Arm, that from their height the Proud pulls down,
And open throws their Plots hid deep as Hell:
That Kings and Kingdoms at its Will does sway,
And gives their Crowns to those in the dust who lay,
With good things the Hungry fills, the' Rich empty sends away.
V.
Israel thus has He filled, thus raised,
And raised up thus, will with his Hand uphold;
Israel his firstborn; And our God be praised,
Who mindful o^ the Inheritance past of Old,
Warrants to' His Child, and with it all Decreed
By' His Word to our Fathers, to' Abraham and his Seed,
And all whom Abraham's Faith makes Parties to the Deed.
IV. HYMN.
The Song of ZACHARIAS.
Benedictus Dominus Deus Israel. Lucae 1. 68.

I.
ISREAL'S Great God eternally be Praised,
Who Israel from the Dust at length has raised;
And mindful of his Ancient Care,
Forgotten, when we thought we were,
Israel to visit has himself come down,
The Prison Doors wide open thrown,
And by the Redemption wrought, made his great Presence known.
II.
Redemption for his People has he wrought,
And to the Throne the Royal Prisoner brought;
To David's Throne, and it is his Son
Sprung from his Loins, holds David's Throne;
With Empire, which no place or time can bound,
With Subjects, in all Countries found,
Subjects, which like him shall be Kings, and like him Crowned.
III.
This by his Prophets, which have been of old,
In every Age he wondrously foretold;
(For every Age have Prophets been,
From since the World did first begin,
This to foretell, which now we Celebrate;)
That God our Wrongs would vindicate,
And from one Ruin save us, and our threatened State.
IV.
Not for our Righteousness, but to perform
The Mercy promised, and his Covenant sworn;
Promised the Fathers, who first were,
But which himself did to' Abraham swear,
By Oath most Sacred; for to be no more
Doubted, tho stablisht 'twas before,
By' himself, Heavens greatest Oath, the God most Highest swore.
V.
So God most Highest, so by' Himself he Swore,
And that from Heaven we should receive the Power,
(Delivered from our Enemies Hands,
Their Captive Chains and Servile Bands)
His own more Noble Service to attend,
Fearless of all that may offend,
In Righteousness, and holy Praise, which never shall end.
VI.
And now he comes, O Child, who this has wrought;
He comes, who has the great Salvation brought:
Be thou his Prophet, and his Ways
Prepare, while He a moment stays,
Only till thou before Him can prepare
His Ways, which deep and wondrous are,
Bylaying down his own, the Life of the World to spare.
VII.
Teach the World, Child, and make his Israel know
Whence their Salvations mighty Source does flow;
That from Remission of their Sin,
The mighty Source does first begin:
Through our God's tender Mercy, who the Way
To Pardon does by Penance lay;
Penance, which does the glories of his Grace display.
VIII.
That Grace, whereby the Dayspring from on High,
Now visits us with Streams, which never shall die.
Streams of pure Ethereal Light,
To shine on those, who in darkness sit;
Which Death's pale shadow shall with Rays increase,
And hopes long Prisoners thence release,
And both theirs guide, and our feet into the way of Peace.
V. HYMN.
The ANGELIC Hymn.
Gloria in Altissimis, etc. Lucae 2. 24.

GLORY be to God on High!
In The Highest Great Jehovah bless!
Good will toward Men, on Earth be Peace!
Glory to God on High!
And may this Round, begun thus, last eternally.
VI. HYMN.
The Song of SIMEON.
Nunc dimittis servum tuum, Lucae 2. 29.
etc.
I.
ENOUGH, my God, enough! I beg no more:
Nor Thou, tho begged, can greater Grace bestow;
My Prayers at length are answered, and I 'adore
The Word, which from thy Mouth did go,
The Word, which like thy Self no change does know*
And now Thy Servant is content to die,
Now, as the best time, since Thy Word and Life's so nigh*
II.
Nigh is the Word, which Thou to me did pass,
Nor has Death come, me and Thy CHRIST between;
As nigh is Life, Thy other Word, which I embrace,
And who, that has thus happy been,
In two great Words, fulfilled one, the other seen,
Would not like me desire in peace to die,
And mortal Life exchange for Immortality?
III.
In Peace I die, and Thou dismissest me,
My God, in Peace, since with these very Eyes,
Before their change, I Thy Salvation see,
And lack not from the Dead to rise,
As Prophets must to 'approve their Prophecies:
By Faith they only at a distance saw,
What in my Arms I hold, the end of all their Law.
IV.
Hail, blessed Salvation of the Eternal King!
Hail Thou, who hither bringst it, Blessed Child!
In whom, as holy Bards inspired do sing,
Those wondrous Truths shall be fulfilled,
Which to Immortal Verse shall subject yield!
Hail to you both, prepared of God to be,
This Worlds Redemption, Heavens and Angels scrutiny!
V.
Such is the great Behest; such is Thy Will,
Who now before all Nations do prepare
What shall with joyous Praise all Nations fill,
As in Him all have equal share:
Thy Son, who shall to those who' in darkness are,
Rise, as the World's Sun does, with scattered Light,
But Isreal's Glory be, with Rays like 'his own Flames bright.
Comiato.
To Sir Nicholas Stuart, Baronet.
SONGS, made in lieu of many more,
(And more than Songs,) which to his Love I owe,
Who, when your Master waited at the Door,
First let him in, and sacred Honors did bestow;
(You, blessed Songs, in the Temple first to sing,
And then to Descant, on an humbler thing.)
To his, and your loved Patron go,
And tho you never can recompense
The ease, and leisure both of us have thence,
Proffer the utmost service Verse can do;
And as He is your Ornament,
Be of my grat'itude and his Virtue the fixed Monument.
The VIII Great HYMNS of the Apocalypse.
I. HYMN.
The Adoration of the XXIV ELDERS.
Gratias agimus tibi Domine Deus Optime. Apoc. 11. 17.

I.
WORTHY Thou art all Honor to receive,
Thrice Honored we, who may that Honor give,
Blessed King, who in One undivided now,
The scattered Parts of Time collected, have,
The Future, Present, and the Past;
And every Time and Age do in one moment know.
II.
We praise Thee', Almighty God, for that Thou' have taken
To Thee thy great Power, and at length do Reign*
Thou Reign'st, and tho the Nations troubled are,
Thy Wrath is come, and therewith come the Time,
When Thou will sentence every Crime,
And all the Dead shall for the Great Assize prepare.
III.
Up shall they rise; and as their Works have been,
Or Shame, or Glory, on all Brows be seen;
Thy Prophets, and Thy Saints shall shout for joy,
And all who fear Thy Name, both small and great;
But Vengeance, from Thy Judgment Seat,
The Earths bold Destroyers shall eternally destroy.
II. HYMN.
The Acclamation of Heaven, upon MICHAEL's overthrow of the Dragon and his
Angels.
Nunc facta est Salus and Virtus, Apoc. 12. 10.
etc.
I.
NOW is Salvation, now is come the Hour,
That long expected, never shall be done;
Now Reigns our God, with whom in equal Power,
And strength Enthroned, sits his Anointed Son:
They Reign and Judge, and having Judgment past,
The Brethrens great Accuser, over the Bar have cast.
II.
Both Night and Day the Brethren he accused,
Heavens common Barrettor, with Charge unjust;
(Their Patience, and the Judges Grace abused)
Tho from them the forged Calumny they thrust,
And his false Evidence or'e-rul'd, bore down,
By the Lambs Blood in Court attested, and their own.
III.
These were the Pleas whereby they overcame,
And these the Witnesses called, and allowed;
Which even the Devil their slanderer heard with shame,
And self-condemned, to the just Sentence bowed:
Greater their Word was than could be denied,
But greater yet their Testimony, that they Di'd.
IV.
For this, O Heavens, rejoice, and ye who there
In Sacred Bliss uninterrupted dwell,
Rejoice, and a part with you let them bear,
Who from below shall of your Justice tell!
With joy shall Sing, how the' Dragon overthrown,
From Heavens high-top to the Earth was tumbled down.
V.
But woe worth you, to whom in wrath he is come,
Of Earth and Sea the miserable 'out-cast,
On whom he will seek to be avenged, the Doom
Was on himself, and cursed Abettors past;
With rage he comes, and whole Hells last effort,
Fury incenst, because he knows his time's but short.
III. HYMN.
The Happy Dead.
Beati mortui qui in Domino moriuntur. Apoc. 14. 13.

I.
BLESSED are the Dead who in the Lord depart,
From henceforth Blessed write them down!
For Labors tho, and Pain they have known,
Of neither feel they more the irksome smart.
II.
So says the Spiri't; for but to' enjoy full rest,
From all their Toils are they removed;
And of their Works, by God approved,
That follow close, in Bliss they are possessed.
IV. HYMN.
The Song of MOSES and of the LAMB.
Magna and Mirabilia sunt opera, Apoc. 15. 3.
etc.
I.
GREAT are thy Works, and marvelous thy Praise,
Lord God Almighty, just and true Thy Ways,
Blessed King of Saints; who would not fear
In Thy dread Presence to appear,
Whom Angels, and attending Thrones revere?
II.
Who would not Fear Thee, Lord, and Glorify
That Name of Thine, which Thou have raised so high!
Thy Holy Name by which Thou art known,
(For Holiness is Thine alone,)
But better, than each single Man by' his own.
III.
Take then, Blessed King, what is Thy proper due,
And through all Land^ and Coasts, Thy Right pursue!
That eve'ry Coast and every Land,
Who wondering at thy Judgments stand,
May Worship Thee, and stoop to their Command.
V. HYMN.
The Angel of the Waters Lauds.
Justus es Domine qui es and qui eras, Apoc. 16. 5.
etc.
I.
RIGHTEOUS art Thou, I. Angel.
O God, who art, and wast,
And shall be, when all time is past,
The first who reignedst, and the last:
Righteous art Thou, who have this Day,
Thy Kingdoms Justice to display,
Righteously judged, that Blood shall Blood repay.
II.
Thy Servants Blood they without mercy shed,
And to be' avenged of the happy Dead,
Made by cursed hands the Living bleed:
With big swollen Veins Thy Prophets stood,
Each Saint, the Genius of his Flood,
Poured from his opened Heart true streams of Blood.
III.
For Blood they thirsted; II. Angel.
and to drink have now
Pure Blood, wherewith their Rivers flow:
So worthy they, so Righteous Thou!
Et andivi alterum dicentem, Etiam Domine.
So Righteous Thou, so worthy they,
Who did Thy Saints, and Prophets slay,
For Blood to' have Blood, and Blood in Blood repay.
VI. HYMN.
The holy Company of Heaven rejoicing at the overthrow of BABYLON.
Allelu-ja. Apoc. 9. 1.
Laus and Gloria and Virtus.
I.
HALLELUJAH! To our King,
Honor, Power, and Glory sing:
Laud and Worship to Him bring,
True and Righteous are whose Ways;
Both deserves, and has our Praise.
II.
True and Righteous is his Doom,
For in Judgment overcome,
Chained the great Whore stands, and Dumb*
Chained she stands, and Dumb withal,
Without Friend, or Voice to call.
III.
Judged she is, who the Earth did stain
With her Prostituted Train:
Justly judged to 'Eternal Pain,
And avenged the Blood she shed,
Dying ever, is never dead.
Et dixerunt iterum Halleluja.
IV.
Hallelujah; to our King
Worship, Laud, and Praises bring,
Glory, Power, and Honor sing!
Lo! how up her Smoke does rise,
Who dead ever, never Dies!
Cecideruntque viginti quatuor Seniores, etc.
and dixerunt Amen, etc.
V.
Even so, Lord, be it so!
That the Earth Thy Power may know,
And thence make Thy Praise to grow!
One blessed Song with us may sing,
Halleluja to our King!
VII. HYMN.
Epithalamium on the LAMBS Marriage.
Et vox de Throno exivit dicens, Apoc. 19. 5.
laudem dicite Deo, etc.
I.
PRAISE Him, ye Servants of the Eternal King,
To God your noblest Praises sing,
And make Heavens vault with the loud Echo ring!
Praise Him, all ye, who fear his Name,
And over the Expanse his Lauds proclaim;
Both great and small in tuneful Shouts accord,
And with one Heart and Voice adore our Lord!
Et audivi quasi vocem turbae magnae, and sicut vocem aquarum multaram and
sicut vocem tonitruorum magnorum dicentium Alleluja! quoniam, etc.
II.
Hallelujah! Sing aloud!
Louder still the harmonious Voice,
Till it drown a Thunders Noise,
And with gay Horror fill the admiring Crowd!
For (Lo!) our God Omnipotent does Reign,
And to himself resumes the Rule again.
III.
Let us be glad now, and let us rejoice,
And give him due Honor, who made us this Day;
A Day that compensates for all our Annoys,
And Ages that we for its Coming did pray:
The Lamb's Marriage-day, which tho long e're it came,
We meet, and greet thus with our Songs purest flame.
IV.
It Is come, and does with hallowed Glory shine,
So bright a Day in Heaven did never appear,
(Tho ever Day, and bright it is ever there)
As this, illumined by the Sun Divine,
And for 'his own Nuptials made with high Design.
But yet how bright so e're it is,
His Bride new Luster to it gives,
Who in her Glory waits Him, as He her in his.
V.
See, where She stands arrayed in Glorious wise,
Second alone to Him, who ever lives:
Above the boldest flight winged Seraph strives,
Who never such Beauties with Immortal Eyes,
E're gazed on, save in Him whose is the Prize.
For beside what is Her proper Dower,
(Charms, which the Almighty overcame)
Heaven all its riches on Her Dress does pour.
VI.
Choice of rich Garments has She thence, and store,
(Presented from the Wardrobe of the Lamb)
Of Gems that blaze with an Ethereal flame,
Enough all but the Wearer to devour,
And all but Heaven to apprize them find too poor:
Choice of fine Linen, clean and bright,
Beyond the skill of Mortal Art,
Washed in the Lambs pure Blood, and there made White.
VII.
These are Her Robes, and these Her Lord's delight,
(Who from His Presence never will let Her part,
But thus Arrayed, resigns to Her his Heart)
The Righteousness of the Saints, with sacred Light,
Communicated from his own blessed Sight.
Thrice happy they, thrice happy he!
Who when their Mortal Life is done,
The Bridgroom's Friends, to' his Supper called shall be!
L'envoy.
Thrice happy I, my God, if I be one,
One of the few, whom then Thou shall invite!
No matter how Thou shall dispose of me,
The meanest service Regal Honors does impart.
VIII. HYMN.
The Exultation of Heaven, at the Descent of the New JERUSALEM.
Et audivi magnam vocem de Throno dicentem, Apoc. 21. 5.
Ecce Tabernaculum Dei cum, etc.
I.
BLESSED state of Innocence,
When out of his just Makers Hand,
The first Man came, with sacred influence,
If what he might, he' had kept the' unh^ard Command!
But happier far that state,
To which he is restored, tho late,
By him, who, (made Man) Man did first Create!
II.
A greater Work this was,
(As to redeem it more did cost,
Than barely make;) the one by a Word did pass,
His Life, the WORD to' effect the other lost;
But therewith did provide,
What none could hope, or do beside,
That they should the happiest be, for whom he Died.
III.
Happy were both, but these
By this the more, that when God went
To Paradise, himself in' his Works to please,
And pleased to Heaven strait took his great Asecnt;
A longer stay below,
To make with his Redeemed now,
Heavens richest Grace he does on Earth bestow:
IV.
For now (behold!) his Tent
With Man is pitched, and there He 'l dwell,
Late to (...), and I Am his Herald sent,
(...) News to the whole World to tell;
(...) God to Man comes down,
(...) Man to dwell, as Man be known,
Whose Form assumed, he keeps still joined to' his own.
V.
With them He 'l dwell, and they
His People, he their God will be;
Not as of Old alone, but in a way
Unthought, and of exalted Dignity:
Where each Relations Band,
By various Titles on each hand,
Made Indissoluble, fast knit does stand.
VI.
God from their Eyes all Tears
Away shall wipe, they in his Praise
Endless Eternity shall spend: No fears,
No fighs more tempests in their Breasts shall raise:
Eve'n Death shall be no more,
Nor Sin, that to it opened the Door,
Nor pain: for former things are all past over.
VII.
Past over they are, and gone,
So says the Faithful and the True,
The High, the Holy God, who from his Throne
Pronounces thus, (Lo!) I make all things New!
And thou to whom it is made known,
O Man beloved, The words write down,
Nor doubt; for he who does it, says, It Is done.
Our Blessed Lord's Thanksgiving.
Confiteor tibi Pater Domine. Mat. 11. 25.

I.
I THANK Thee, Father, Lord of all,
Of Heaven, and Earth the Blessed King,
That to reveal Thy Discipline,
The Prudent of this World Thou do not call,
The Mighty, Noble, and the Wise;
But such, whom they, as Foolish, Base, and Weak despise.
II.
From those Thou have Thy Counsels hid,
Who first Thy Counsels did refuse;
And Babes to publish them do choose,
That no Flesh take what Power Almighty did,
And when Thy Hand alone has done
The weighty Enterprise, ascribe it to their own.
III.
Eve'n so, O Father, so it is!
Thy Will, Thy Love do hence appear,
And that great Power, which every where
In all things Rules, but more in none than this;
For so to Thee it seems good,
Thy Grace should none withstand, but those who it withstood.
The Song of MOSES.
-- Tunc cecinit Moses, Exodus 15.
and Filii Israel carmen hoc Domino; and di^^runt, Cantemus Domino, etc.
(...).
I.
SINCE the Almighty has in Triumph led,
Not by c^r Battles, but his Arm ^'re-thrown,
The Proud (...) King, and from his Head,
On ours to put it, (...) Crown,
What more accept^^ Trophy can we raise,
Than an eternal arch of Duty and of Praise?
Begin, my Song and thou Immortal Verse,
(Now truly such, since him Thou do Record,
Who only is the Immortal Lord)
I my self will begin too, and rehearse
The wondrous Conquests of his Word:
To him I Will Sing, who gloriously,
His Promise, and his Ancient Faith to keep,
Has gotten himself, and us the Victory,
And Horse, and Rider hurld, at once, into the Deep.
II.
God is my Strength, and he shall be my Song,
From whom my great Salvation came;
Who is my God, approved in dangers long,
My Father's God, the Puissant, and the Strong,
Israel's tried Champion, and Jehovah is his Name.
Him will I Sing, of him shall be my Praise,
And of the Works, that he has done;
The Eternal Glories he has won,
But ours no less, than his own Fame to raise.
For Pharao and his host,
(Their way, their hopes, themselves in the Ruin lost)
Into the Sea he hurld, the Red Sea, as a Stone
By a Giants arm is lightly thrown,
Him and his chosen Captains at a cast.
They flew; they sank; the wounded Sea did groan,
But into it is bottom headlong let them down,
Through thousand Waves that murmured, as along they whirling past.
III.
There were they Drowned; there covered with the Deep;
There sank they, never to be raised again;
In Adamantine Chains Thou did the Prisoners keep,
The same Thou thr^wst before upon the Main,
And now on them, over both to show Thy equal Reign.
Glorious Thou did that Day, O Lord, appear;
Glorious in Power appeared then thy Right Hand;
Thine Enemies the Shock could not withstand,
And found too late its Wrath was heavier than their fear;
When dashed in pieces they all scattered lay,
And to the Leviathans became a Prey.
So on a sudden were they gone,
So soon, so easily, by Thy Breath o're-thrown,
As when a raging Fire does Stubble seize,
And with it Thorns, to' unequal Battle press.
Such was Thy Wrath, tho in a different kind,
(And different was the Element,
But Death as certain, and as sudden sent)
Thy Wrath toward them, which like a mighty Wind
Stood up and up the Floods bid stand,
As on an heap to let Thy People go;
The Floods obeyed the great Command,
And like a Wall, on either Hand,
Of congealed Crystal, in the heart of the Sea did show.
IV.
This as the Spoyler saw, "It Is now, he said,
"Now is the time an end to make;
"I will pursue, I will o're-take,
"Lo! how the Sea the Fugitives has betrayed,
"And, to leave us a way, its dried up Channel does forsake!
"Now shall my Sword with slaughter glutted be,
"My Lust shall on them now be satisfied,
"Nor till they 'are totally destroyed,
"This Arm withdraw, or let them other Conquerors see;
"With Charms their Leader staind the sacred Flood,
"But I Will the Sea, or perish, new Dye with their Blood.
Scarce said; Thou with Thy Wind
Upon the parted Sea did blow;
The parted Sea its hold invisible let go,
And hollowed to its fellow Waves behind.
They came; and having traverst them around;
The Chamiam Hosts did in their Arms enclose;
A while they floated, diving where they rose,
Till touching thrice the fatal Ground,
Like Lead they sank, and all the Deaths they had threatened, found.
V.
Amongst the Gods, Who is there like the Lord?
Or with Him, who can once compare?
So 'unsearchable His Counsels are,
So great the Wonders of His Word;
In holy Glories, who does all excel,
And Terror strikes in us, who would his Praises tell.
For Thou thy Hand did only wave,
And Isreal's way was Egypts Grave,
Which the opening Earth, and Seas, conspiring gave.
Thus perished they: Thus saved by Thy Right Hand,
Thy ransomed People hitherto Thou' have brought;
Whilst of the Miracles it wrought,
We and our Children living Monuments here stand.
Nations shall hear this, Lord, and be afraid;
Horror on Palestine fast hold shall take;
Courage the Dukes of Edom shall forsake:
And Moabs daring Warriers, ill appaid,
Shall on themselves feel unknown Terrors laid:
Whole Canaan ready to expire,
With dread dissolved, as Wax shall melt before the Fire.
VI.
Trembling and Fear, Anguish, Despair, and Dread,
On every Land shall fall, on every Head.
As Thine Arm is, such shall their Horrors be,
And not a Soul from the' Consternation free.
Close shall they lie within their Dens,
Still as the Rocks, wherewith they are covered, lie;
And fearful once to issue thence,
Scarce to their helpless Gods send forth a cry,
Or let their Groans be heard, while Israel passes by.
Till to Thy Promised Land they are come,
And, where they have been strangers long, their ancient Home.
Thither, my God, through thousand dangers past,
To Sion, by Thy self prepared to be
Thy Dwelling Place to 'Eternity,
The Sanctuary, with Thy Presence graced,
Thither be pleased Thy purchased Flock to bring at last!
So shall Thou through all Ages Reign,
And in all Lands new Subjects to Thy Empire gain.
Sumpsit ergo Maria Prophetissa soror A^rm tympanum in manu sua: egressaeque
sunt omnes Mulieres post eam cum tympanis, and choris, quibus praecinebat
dicens. CHORUS Mulierum.
SIng to the Lord, who gloriously,
His Promise, and his Ancient Faith to keep,
Has gotten Himself, and us the Victory,
And Horse, and Rider hurld at once into the Deep!
The WELL.
Number. 21. -- Tune cecinit Israel Carmen istud. Ascendat Puteus! concinebat.
Puteus quem soderunt Principes, and paraverunt duces multitudinis, in Datore
legis, and in baculis suis.
SACRED Fountain, Mystic Spring,
Lo! how to Thy Dance we Sing,
And Cymbals tuned by thy soft Purlings ring!
Spring eternally, O Well,
Spring up, and into Rivers swell!
For why, no common Well art Thou,
Nor was it chance first found Thee out;
But having for Thee searched about,
To Princes Thou thy rise do owe,
Who dug Thee first, and made Thee flow.
Spring, happy Well, by Princes made,
Without or Mattocks help, or Spade,
(The' ill-boading Instruments of Graves)
But dug by Princes, with their Staves,
And by their Lawgiver surveyed!
'Twas He to the Work those Worthies sent;
Spring Well, and teach his great Intent,
And what He by the Mystery meant!
"That as to all Thy Wate^ ^^ow,
"So should their equal Justice do.
Sacred Fountain, Mysric Spring,
Lo! how to Thy Dance we Sing,
And Cymbals tuned by Thy soft purlings Ring!
Spring Eternally, O Well,
Spring up, and into Rivers swell!
The Prayer of JABEZ.
-- Invocavit autem Jabez Deum Israel, II. Part. 4.
dicens. Si benedicens benedixeris mihi, and dilataveris terminos meos, and
fuerit manus tua mecum, and feceris me a malitia non opprimi! -- Et praestit^^
Deus quae precatus est.
I.
THUS Jabez prayed, and thus pray I;
Great God, O, that it might Thee please,
Thy Servant, who upon Thee does rely,
With Thy choice Favors, and indeed to bless!
It Is Prayer alone can this obtain,
And, but to Thee, even Prayer is vain.
II.
Enlarge those Coasts, wherein I dwell,
Nor let me ever live retired;
But in Thy CHURCH those mighty Wonders tell,
Which have my Verse with Heavenly Flame inspired:
Lord to my House Thy Love secure,
And let it, like Thy Word, endure!
III.
Yet not for Greatness do I pray,
Nor, e're Thy Time is, to be known*
But do Thy Will, I can its Pleasure stay,
Waiting that Harvest, for which Thou have sown:
Only till then, make me content,
And leave to Thee the whole Management!
IV.
Thou know I do Thy Temple love,
I know there is time enough behind;
Why should I make the Enjoyment bitter prove,
By hasting, what I Am sure Thou have designed?
In hope at present, I rejoice,
And both my Heart employ and Voice.
V.
For what concerns my poor estate,
Since I to Thee my All commit;
No time, when e're it falls, shall be too late,
To soon it may, if I encumber it
With Wishes, that unlawful be,
And, vain affects, which move not Thee:
VI.
No! I Will never think the time too long,
How long so e're the time may be;
Let but Thy Hand, through Patience make me strong,
And keep off Evil from disturbing me!
If Jabez thus to Thee did cry,
And could be heard, Lord, why not I?
