Nonas December. M.DC.LX.VII. In quos post multas hujusce vitae varietates, media
and matura aetate, ab aerumnosis saeculi curis, and otio (neutiqam licet
ignobili) hoc juxta Vaticinium, biennio post redemptus fui XIV. Kalend. Januar.
Per Reverendum admodum in Christo Patrem and Dominum Dominum GEORGIUM Episcopum
Wintoniensem, cujus R. R. Paternitati hic, impares licet tanto Beneficio,
grates, quas tamen Divino Numini imprimis debeo, secundas saltem, ex toto animo
in conspectu Populi and Ecclesiae refero. -- Manda Deus virtuti and confirma
quod operatus es a Templo Sancto tuo!
Ode.
The Song of DEBORA.
Cecineruntque Debora and Barac filius Abinoem in illo die, Judic. 5.
dicentes. -- Qui sponte obtulistis de Israel, etc.
I.
ISRAEL, the blessed and happy State,
For whom the Almighty all his Wonders showed;
Israel, your great Redeemer celebrate,
And what for you he did, rehearse aloud!
Tell, how he made his ^and and appear,
And when the willing Tribes their Youth an Offering sent,
How God before the Sacred Army went,
And vengeful Ruin followed in the Rear!
And ye, O Kings, (whose awful Sway
The many-headed Multitude obey,
And at whose feet they both their Necks and Tributes lay;)
Princes and Rulers, to my Song attend,
Whose lofty Subject challenges your Ear,
By all that can a Verse commend,
Or greatest Kings persuade to hear,
A tuneful Voice, with Charms divinely strong,
A Woman begs your audience now;
And if that will not do,
Deb'rah a Judge in Israel sings, and Isreal's God's the Song.
II.
Domine cum exires de Seir.
Lord, out of Seir, when Thou did go,
Marching the Hill, before Thine Host adown,
When Thou made Edoms Fields Thy Conquests know,
And with Armed Troops the Wilderness did crown;
Affrighted Earth did at Thy Presence quake,
Heavens labo'ring Machin did the Warning take,
And from its torn sides dreary Tempests shake.
It thundered, and down fell from Heavens high Tower,
Of Stones and scalding Rain a mighty shower;
With Darkness all the Sky was covered over,
Through which the blew-wing'd Lightning flew,
And after it a trail of kindled Brimstone drew.
It struck the Rocks, and they took fire;
The Mountains with excessive heat did melt,
Unusual scorching Sinai felt;
And tho than other Hills exalted higher,
Tho with its head it did to the Clouds aspire,
Sinai nor could resist, nor bear the Flame,
But down in a burning Torrent headlong came.
III.
In diebus Samgar, etc.
Samgar and Jahel wondrous Saviors were,
And did in Counsel and in Arms excel;
Of Jahels Prudence we the Trophies are,
Six hundred Philistims by Samgar fell,
All with an Ox-goad slain, and driven like Beasts to ^ell*
But yet the Roads untrodden lay,
Scared Passengers through Woods did stray,
And but to Dens and Caves led not the Mountainous highway.
Woods, and thick Bushes in all Pastures grew,
The Plough-man, and his Labor ceast,
The Land as Cursed enjoyed its rest,
And not each Seventh alone, but every Year a Sabbath knew.
It rested, till I Debora rose at last,
At last in Israel I a Mother rose,
Then when new Gods the People chose,
And from Rebellion to Idolatry had past.
But see the Vengeance that pursued their Sin,
Slavery and Cowardice at the Breach rusht in,
And not a Shield, or Spear was found midst forty thousand Men.
IV.
Cor meum diligit Principes, etc.
Fly, fly, my Muse, from this unmanly race,
And to new Pomps thy strains address;
Isreal's great Generals, who to efface
Of all our foregone Miseries the very trace,
Courageously did to the Battle press,
And, where in Irons we lay, with Laurels strewn the place.
With them my Heart, with them my Praise shall be.
And ye, O Fathers, who at Home reside,
And you, who equal Justice to dispense,
Unguarded to your peaceful Cities ride,
And Justice done, return unguarded thence;
Judges, and People by their arms set free,
Come in and join with me,
And let us bless the Lord, as we their Spoils divide!
Come let us bless Him, and proclaim
The Wonders of his Reverend Name;
There, whence we exiled were of late,
And at our Fountains first begin,
Where we so often have rescued been,
Scaping the Death we saw upon the Wing;
Our Fountains tuneful numbers will inspire,
And by their purling falls direct the Quire,
Which well tuned there, we may advance in state,
And bring with Harps and Songs our Praises to the City Gate.
V.
Surge, surge Debora, surge, etc.
Awake, O Debora, awake,
And from this hint fresh vigor take!
Encourage, and provoke Thy Lyre,
Till all its speaking Chords conspire,
And with Thy Voice a perfect Concert make!
Up Barak, at the harmonious Sound,
Abinoams warlike S^n arise!
Lo! Thy Captivity stands in Fetters bound,
To be Thy valors early Prize,
And dreadfully adorn Thy entrance with its Exequies!
Lead on, Great Prince, by God ordained
To be Thy Nations Glory, and this Days;
Who have Thy self the heat of the War sustained;
Tho Women with Thee share divided Praise!
And all the while Thou do the sacred Mount ascend,
Boldly Thy Ransomed Peoples shouts attend,
The Bays Thou wearest will Thy Head defend!
Yea, speak Thy self, how God made Thee,
The Captain of his Armies be;
And when retired Thou long had lain at Home,
How forth he called Thee to overcome:
The strange deliv'erance by Thy Hands he wrought,
And how to mine he Judgment gave;
And let the Tribes, which with us fought,
A just Memorial in our Triumphs have!
VI.
Ex Ephraim delevit eos.
Place Ephraim here, Ephraim whose Fortitude
In Amaleks first overthrow was tried,
When stoutly he the Uncircumsised defied,
And with unerring Shafts their flying Troops pursued.
Benjamin, with his Squadron followed close,
And his scorned Life more nobly to expose,
The Tribe, he dearest loved, for witness of his Courage, chose,
From Machir Princes to the Battle came,
And Counselors from Zabulon,
Who to increase the glory of their Name,
Kept by the Sword, what by the Pen they won.
Then Issachar and Napthali,
By Debora that, and this by Barak led,
Both valiant Tribes, and both resolved to die,
Or conquer with so brave an Head.
But had you seen the Emulation there,
And how they strove each other to out-fight;
You would have thought them armed with Heavenly Might,
And (all so terrible they did appear
Such Trophies of Mail'd Corpse did round them rear,)
That every Isra^elite a destroying Angel were.
VII.
Diviso contra se Ruben.
Ruben the while did with his Flocks abide,
And blessed the Flood, whose streams the parted Land divide.
Careless of what his Brethr'en thought,
And what the fears his cold indiffernce wrought;
Strange and distracted fears his cold indifference brought.
But why, O Ruben, why did thou refuse
An Enterprise so great and good?
Was it to hear thy bleating Ews;
Or could thy Honor be so little understood,
That their plain Fleeces thou should choose,
Before a Robe di'd Purple, in thine Enemies blood?
This cold indifference, Ruben, lost thee more,
Than ever thou in Arms had gained before.
Ah! had thou been alone! but far behind,
By thy example, Gilead stayed;
On Shipboard Dan drove on his Trade,
And Asser, that he might be signally unkind.
Tho of pale Death too Womanly afraid,
Rather than yield his aid,
Watched on his naked Beaches, torn with Seas and Wind.
Unlike to Zabulon, and Nepthali,
Who best knew how to Live, yet feared the least to Die.
VIII.
Zabulon vero and Nepthali, etc.
Thither, my Song, behold their Ensigns spread
On the High Places, and how equally endu'd
With Learning, and with Conduct too, they showed,
That never friendlier those best gifts inhabited,
And softer thoughts designs more noble bred.
The Cananitish Kings approacht the Hill,
Tabor, by their defeat to be renowned,
But fearful to ascend its top, did fill
The Plains of Tanaach, with their Camps around,
And loud Megiddos Waters with their shoutings drowned.
They came, and fought, but Heaven, that took our part,
Bore the first shock, and on them turned the War;
A mortal shaft was sent from every Star,
Which sank like Lead, into the Spoilers Heart.
No Gain or Pay, the Sacred Legions took,
But to the Service armed, in Diamond marched on,
And, whom they spared, Kison, that ancient Brook,
Kison in its swollen Torrent carried down.
In vain the Horse assayed the Flood to stem,
Which hurld their Riders with them down the rapid stream.
IX.
Conculca Anima mea robustos.
Enough, my Soul, enough, the chase give over!
Those Enemies thou have seen, thou shall behold no more.
Stop, for at length the War is done,
And thou in Blood, I know, take no delight!
Sound a Retreat, the Day's thine own,
And so shall Sisera too, e're Night,
Finding the Death he would shun by an inglorious flight.
But first confirm a Curse was laid
By our God's Angel, and a Charge divine,
"Curse ye Meros, the Angel said!
"Curse Meros bitterly, and join
Yours to Jehovah's Curse and mine!
Curse all, who dwell there, and be this their Doom,
Who like them to the Almighty's ^tandard will not come!
But blessed above Women be,
Isreal's and Hebers Ornament,
Jabel above all Women blessed in the Tent.
And let this Song preserve her Memory!
Never was greater Name
Recorded, in the Eternal Monuments of Fame.
X.
Aquam petenti lac dedit.
To her on foot Sisa'ra his flight addressed:
The courteous Wife to meet him went:
The courteous Wife invited him into her Tent,
And future Joys with thoughtful Cares suppressed.
He asked her Water, and she ran in haste,
To execute her own, and to prevent his Wish,
And poured him Milk into a Royal Dish:
(A ready and a quick repast.)
And having give'n the fatal Bait,
Humbly at his Feet did wait,
And smiled to see how greedily he drank and slept his last.
"Sleep, Tyrant, sleep she said!
And up a Nail and Hammer took,
The Nail into his Temples struck,
And with his own unbloody Sword smote off his Head.
He bowd, he fell, and at her Feet he lay,
Down at her Feet he bowed, fell, groaned his Soul away,
Where he bowed, there he fell down dead.
XI.
Per fenestram respiciens.
Out at a Window his blithe Mother gazed,
And waiting there his coming, cried,
"Why lies the Dust so long unrais'd,
"Nor Sisara yet, with Captives by his side,
"Exalted high in his triumphal Chariot ride?
Her Ladies answered her, Those Ora'cles of the Court,
Yea, to her self she made her own report.
"Have they not sped, have they not gained the Day,
"Have they not shared the Israelitish Prey?
"To every Man a Dame, or two,
"To Sisara as the General's due,
"Choice of rich Slaves, and choice of Garments too?
"A curious Vest, with Needles wrought,
"With curious Needles wrought on either side,
"And all in Royal Colors died,
"By the Hebrews of their Neighbor Tyrians bought;
"And only sit the Victors bloody arms to hide?
So, Lord, may all Thine Enemies die,
So Conquer, and be Conquered so;
When such, as on Thy Power rely,
In Heaven alone their equals know,
And like the Sun, which triumphs there,
Crowned with illustrious Beams, and robe'd in Light appear.
Comiato.
To the Reverend the now Dr. James Gardener Sub-Dean of Lincoln.
SONG, in the Country little understood,
For my dear Gardener, at the Town inquire,
And all thy heat into his generous Breast inspire,
To mingle with a nobler Fire,
Which lies at present smouldring in his Blood!
Perchance thou may effectual prove,
To make that upward, toward its Center, move,
And him in softest lays rehearse the HOLIEST LOVE.
1668.
David's ELEGY Upon the death of SAUL and JONATHAN.
2 Samuel 1. Considera Israel pro hiis, qui mortui.
I.
ISREAL'S delight, the glory of our Land,
How are the Mighty overthrown!
Before their Enemies Swords they could not stand,
Nor conquered fall by any but their own:
In Thy High Places Israel, both did fall,
A public Victim for their Land, in view of all.
II.
Let not in Gath the mournful News be known,
Nor in Philistia publish it;
Stop the Report, e're it reach Ascalon,
Nor let our Captive names their Arches fit:
Lest fearful Women, whom they left at Home,
With Songs to share the Spoil, and meet their Triumph come!
III.
And ye, Gilboas Mounts, may never Rain,
Or fertile Showers descend on you;
But on your Heads let there abide that stain,
Which Seas, should they pour down, would but renew*
Let the cursed Earth no more an Offering yield,
Nor God expect his First Fruits from the empty Field.
IV.
'Twas there the valiant Saul resigned his Breath,
And there his Shield was thrown away;
Never was such a Trophy reared to Death,
Nor ever sacred arms so scattered lay:
The valiant Readiness, as if he never had been
The Lord's Anointed, or his Chosen Peoples King.
V.
From the pursuit the Bow of Jonathan,
Some Regal Spoil, did daily bring;
Destruction after his fleet arrows ran,
And at the Wounds they made, Death entered in*
The Sword of Saul did never empty come,
But a new Purple, from the Blood of Kings, brought home.
VI.
Alike in Life, in Death alike they were,
Not more in Blood allied, than Mind;
Themselves alone, you with them could compare,
Who none their equals saw, or left behind:
And on their arms there hung such Victory,
That Men they only seemed, because they both could die.
VII.
For as the Eagle, to her Prey does haste,
And hovering over the Quarry flies;
Or the fierce Lion, having once a taste
Of Blood, does all the Shepherds noise despise,
And at their Slings no show of fear does make;
Like Lions they o're-came, like Eagles did o're-take.
VIII.
And you, O Daughters of so great a King,
(Our Tribes support) his Death lament,
Whose Victories you before were wont to Sing,
And clad in Scarlet to adorn them, went:
Now put on Mourning, to attend his Hearse,
Sad as your own complaints, and mournful as my Verse.
IX.
For (Lo!) the Glories of our State, and Land,
(Lo!) how the Mighty are o're-thrown;
Before their Enemies Swords, they could not stand,
Tho Saul by none could perish, but his own:
In thy High Places, Jonathan, thou did fall,
A public Victim, where thou should have reigned over all.
X.
For thee, my Brother, it is for thee I grieve,
The best of Friends, as well as Men;
In whose Death I that fatal Wound receive,
Which closed will never be, till we meet again;
And in the Mansions of the Saints above,
Enjoy, what here we vowed, our more than mortal love.
XI.
Isreal's Delight, the Glory of our Land,
How are the Mighty overthrown!
Before his Enemies Sword one could not stand,
Nor the other fall by any but his own:
In thy High Places, Israel, both did fall,
A public Victim for their Land, in view of all.
DAVID's Thansgiving and Prayer.
2 Samuel 7.
-- Ingressus est autem Rex David and sedit coram Domino, and dixit, Quis ego
sum Domine and quae?
I.
WHO am I Lord, and what is my Family,
The youngest House of the Jessean Race, 1 Paral. 17.

In all things little, but that Grace,
Which Thou on us have shour'd, but most on me?
Who am I, that Thou hitherto
Have brought me, Lord, Thy Bounty and Thy Power to show?
II.
Hitherto Thou have brought me, and that Hand,
Which for a Sling, and Sheephook was designed;
A nobler Service is enjoined,
And Men, instead of Flocks, are my command:
Israel the Flock, and care Divine,
And my exalted Name does midst rich Trophies shine.
III.
Like the great Men's of the Earth, Thou' have made my Name,
And yet (as if all this were not enough,
And thousand Pledges more of Love,
But the foundation only of that Frame,
Thou in Thy mind had cast to raise)
Of future Glories Thou foretell'st, and growing Praise.
IV.
Of Times to come Thou' have told, long hence to come,
And that my House, and Throne upheld shall be;
Like a Prince born, Thou' have treated me,
As having, what Thou' art making for me room;
And are Men, Lord, thus wont to do,
Who rather than exalt the Poor, will keep them low?
V.
Yet thus Thou' have done, and what can I say more,
Or greater, for Thy Honor, Lord, or mine,
Which both here equal issues join,
That all, who the Work admire, may Thee adore?
Greater I Would say, Thou know full well,
And more, but what I know not, how I cannot tell.
VI.
Unless I add, that for Thy great Words sake
Thou did it, that Thou might at once fulfill
The secret Counsels of Thy Will,
And, what they were, known to Thy People make:
For Thine own sake, my God, and mine,
For Thou thy Servant know, and that his will is Thine.
VII.
Great art Thou, Lord, and wondrous are Thy Ways,
The best and greatest, the only God alone;
Beside, or like whom, there is none,
Glorious in Holiness, fearful in Praise:
Thus sing we, who did first receive
The Truth from our Forefathers, but seeing, now believe.
VIII.
For what one Nation, as from them of Old
We' have often heard, with Israel can compare?
For whom Himself God did not spare,
But came from Heaven in Person to behold
The Mise'ries they did undergo,
And not to see alone, but to Revenge them too?
IX.
From Heaven he came, from Heaven himself came down
All clothed in Tempest, and sulphurous Flame,
To get Himself the greater Name,
And do, what by His Word He might have done:
That thus from Egypts Gods set free,
Himself he might declare His Peoples God to be.
X.
This our Forefathers told us; but we' have seen
Our selves as mighty Wonders of Thy Love;
Nor need we fetch from them our Proof,
Who' our selves as mighty Wonders often have been:
Confirmed by Thy after vast expense,
(And more's to come) to be Thy great Inheritance.
XI.
And now, my God, the Word, which Thou have said,
The irrevocable Word concerning me,
Let it for ever stablisht be!
And stablisht be that House, which Thou have made!
Thy Servant David, thus approved,
Establish with his House, nor let them be removed.
XII.
So shall Thy Word and Name be ever praised;
"And Isreal's Holy God, shall Israel sing,
"Is God alone, and Israel's King:
"He His Anointeds Horn on high has raised,
"Of David, and his House approved,
"David and 'his House established never to be removed.
XIII.
Not that I Am worthy, Thou should hear my Vows,
Only Thy self was pleased the Word to pass;
Lord, since I 'have with Thee found this Grace,
To be assured Thou It build Thy Servants House,
Another Grace turn not away,
Which in my Heart I since have found, even thus to pray.
XIV.
Thy Promise it is this holy boldness gives,
That Word of Thine, which like Thy self is sure,
And through all Ages shall endure,
True, as its Speaker, who for ever Lives:
The God of Truth, who cannot Lie,
Nor his own Goodness, promised thus to his Child, deny.
XV.
Please it Thee therefore, may it please Thee, Lord,
Thy Servant, and Thy Servants House to bless,
With Blessings that may never cease;
Blessings, as fixed as Thy Eternal Word,
For Thou, my God, can bless alone,
Thus bless; and once thus blessing me indeed, I 'have done.
The last words of DAVID.
Dixit David filius Isai, 2 Samuel 23.
dixit vir, etc.
Terzetti.
THUS David, Jesse's Royal Son did sing,
Thus spoke the Man, who the great Promise had,
That from his Loins the CHRIST of God should spring.
On whose Eternal Shoulders should be laid
The Government, Psalmist of Israel,
Thus sang he, thus from Heaven inspired he said:
The Spirit of God in Vision on me fell,
And by my Mouth Himself the Almighty spoke,
His Words they are, which I his Prophet tell,
His, who the Care of Israel does take,
And hitherto never falsifi'd His Trust,
Nor will, tho Heaven and Earths Foundations shake.
"The Man, who rules over others must be Just,
"Ruling himself, and them, in the sacred Fear
"Of Heavens dread King, to whom account he must;
"For all the wrongs he does, or makes them bear;
"By an Impartial Judgment to be tried,
"Whose Doom definitive he forced shall hear.
Thrice happy Prince, who (e're that Oyes cried,)
The God within his Breast, his Conscience,
Appealing, and appealed has satisfied!
There, first absolved, with approved Innocence,
His Righteousness shall shine, as Morning Light,
When the early Sun, his Glories to dispense,
New guilds the Sullys of the murky Night;
And without Cloud between his Beams to pass,
As higher he ascends, appears more bright.
So shall he shine; or as the tender Grass,
Shooting its verdant Head above the Ground,
With gems of pearly Dew, midst Flowers, takes place.
This I Will not say, that in my Rule I Have found,
A perfect and uninterrupted Bliss,
(For what is my House, or Rule thus to be owned?)
Yet for my Kingdoms Justice I Will say this,
That with me God a lasting Cove'nant made,
In all things sure, as His great promise is:
Ordered and sure, nor e're to be o^resway'd,
Tho I too often have forfeited His Love,
And when I should have ruled, my Lusts obeyed.
But even then did I, and do now reprove
The Follies I so passionately pursued,
And whose remembrance greater Passions move.
Blessed God forgive me! be Thy Word renewed!
For all before Thee is my whole desire,
All in Thy sight, as it by me is viewed;
Confirm Thy Promise, humbly I require,
Not for my sake, O Lord, but for Thine own;
And double Zeal into my Son inspire!
So shall he grow up as a Plant alone,
And in his Fame, tho dead, I too shall grow,
The happy Father of an happier Son:
Whilst those, who will not to his Scepter bow,
As Thorns shall all of them be thrust away,
Thorns, which the unguarded Hand tho they pierce through,
To the Hook and Fire shall be an easy Prey:
Justice, which in his time from the Earth shall spring,
And Peace, from Heaven descending, meet half way.
The Song of HEZEKIAH.
Ego dixi in dimidio dierum meorum. Isa. 38.

I.
REVOLVING the sharp Sentence past,
And how an end, e're thought was on me come;
How soon, said I, have I approacht my last,
And unawares reached Natures farthest Home?
Ah! now I to the Grave must go,
No more, or Life, or Pleasure know,
But a long doleful Night, in darkness deep below.
II.
No more, my God, shall I see Thee,
Nor the great Works of Thy Almighty Hand;
No more a Votary at Thy Altar be,
Nor in the crowds of them, who praise Thee, stand*
Mankind no more shall I behold,
Nor tell, nor of Thy Love be told,
Eve'n mine to Thee, shall like my ashes, Lord, be cold.
III.
Lo! as a Tent am I removed,
And my lives thread, which I thought wondrous strong,
Too weak to bear the Looms extension proved,
In The midst broke off, too sleasie to run long:
With Sickness I am pine'd away,
And feel each moment some decay,
All Night in Terrors, and in Grief die all the Day.
IV.
For as a Lion hasts to 'his Prey,
And havieg gripe'd it, breaks the yielding Bones;
So on me came the Almighty, whilst I lay
In vain expecting help, but from my Groans:
O take, said I, Thy Hand away,
See how I feel my Loins decay,
All Night in Terrors, and in Grief die all the Day!
V.
Then like a Swallow, or a Crane,
I chatt'red over my Fears, his Heart to move;
The widowed Turtle does not more complain,
When in the Woods she' has lost her faithful Love:
My Eyes, O God, with waiting fail,
Why should Thou thus a Worm assail?
I Am Thine, O let for once the Almighty not prevail!
VI.
Yet do Thy Will, I must confess,
Worse Plagues than these, my Sins deserve from Thee;
The Sentence past is than my Crimes far less,
And only Hell a fit reward can be:
Ah! let my Prayers that Doom prevent!
My age in Mournings shall be spent,
And all the Years Thou giv'st, shall be, but to repent.
VII.
On Thy great Pleasure all depend,
During which only, I and Mankind live;
To teach us this Thou do Diseases send,
And daily claim'st the Life, which Thou did give:
Yet such is Thy resistless Power,
That when our age is quite past over,
What Thou at first did give, Thou can our Life restore.
VIII.
And thus with me, Lord, have Thou dealt,
Tho I for peace had only bitterness;
The effects of mighty Goodness thus have felt,
Beyond what words, or numbers can express:
For from the Pit Thou drew'st me back,
And that I might no pleasures lack,
Upon Thy Self the burden of my Sins did take.
IX.
Triumphant Savior! the still Grave,
For so great Love, Thy Name can never praise;
Nor in the Pit can Thou Memorial have,
Thy Truth, or hoped for, or adored Thy Ways:
The Living, Lord, the Living are
The Men, who must Thy Power declare,
And of them chiefly such, whom Thou like me, shall spare.
X.
They to their Children shall make known,
As I do now, the Wonders of Thy Hand;
How when we eve'n to Hell did headlong run,
To stop our passage, Thou in the way did stand:
Lord, since Thou 'hast thus delivered me,
Thus made me Thy Salvation see,
My Life, and Harp, and Song, I Will consecrate to Thee.
(...) III. ID. NOV. 1667.
Three Psalms according to the old Version, and Meeter, ordinarily used in
Churches, a little altered.
PSALM. I.
Beatus Vir qui non abiit.
THE Man is blessed, whose doubtful Paths
Unrul'd by Sinners are;
Who in their Council never stood,
Nor sat in the Scorners Chair.
But in the Law of God the Lord
Has fixed his whole delight;
And in that Law does exercise
Himself both Day and Night.
He shall be like a Tree, which grows
Close by the Rivers side;
Whose loaded Boughs in Fruits return
Their Tribute to the Tide.
No Storm or Drought shall make him fade,
But he unmoved shall stand;
Nor shall Success less prosperous crown,
What e're he takes in hand.
No so the Wicked, who as Chaff
By Tempests raised on high,
The triumph of fierce Winds are made,
And as they drive them, fly.
Unlike in Life, unlike shall be
The ends which on them wait;
Whilst these in Judgment cannot stand,
And those are praised in the Gate.
For why the ways of Righteous Men
Unto the Lord are known:
But Sinners ways, hid to themselves,
Unto the Dead lead down.
PSALM. LVII.
Miserere mei, Deus miserere.
GREAT God, on whom I have reli'd,
Whose Mercy is my stay;
Under Thy Wings, or let me hide,
Or on them fly away!
Or hide, or fly, until the Storm
Which threatens me is past;
Thou all things for me do perform,
In Thee my hope is placed.
To God I Will cry, who shall descend
From Heaven, in the Fight to close,
And while his Love does me defend
His Truth shall slay my Foes.
With Lions, Lord, my Soul lies down,
shut up within their Den;
Lions so fierce were never known,
Cruel, and bloody Men.
Whose Tongues are Swords, and Eyes all Fire,
With gore and slaughter Red;
And who against me all conspire,
To look, or speak me Dead.
Yet set Thy Glory 'above the Skies,
Over the Earth exalted be;
For tho so high I cannot rise,
Thou may stoop down to me
Thou did so, for as I looked round,
Pensive and full of care;
My prostrate Enemies strewn the Ground,
Each taken in his own Snare.
Fixed is my Heart to sing Thy Praise,
It Is fixed, and I Will rejoice;
Awake my Harp, and with Thee raise,
To Heaven my tuneful Voice!
I will awake too, and my Song
To the Nations shall rehearse
Mercies, whose Praise to Heaven belong,
Worthy an Angels Verse.
Lord, set Thy Glory 'above the Skies,
Over the Earth exalted be!
Lo, how thy Son does thither rise,
Lift from the Grave by Thee!
Turned and Transcribed with the following Psalm, for an Hymn upon Easter Day,
1671.
PSALM. CXIV.
In exitu Israel de, etc.
WHEN Israel was by God's address,
And his Almighty Hand,
From Bondage led, and wondrously
Brought to the Promised Land;
In Judah God his Glory showed,
And did his Power declare;
Israel his great Inheritance,
Temple and Empire were.
The Sea it saw and suddenly
Amazed rose up and fled;
The parted streams of Jordans Flood
Ran trembling to their Head.
Aside the Mountains leapt like Rams,
And to the Hills did show,
(The Hills, which shook like frighted Lambs)
The way which they should go.
What ailed the Sea that all amazed
So suddenly it fled?
And what made Jordans parted streams
Run trembling to their Head?
Why did the Mountains leap, like Rams,
And to the Hills first show,
(The Hills, which shook like frighted Lambs)
The way which they should go?
Confess, O Earth, thy Sovereign Lord,
And at his Presence quake!
Before the Face of Jacob's God
Bow, and Obeisance make!
It Is he, who caused those Rocks to hear,
And when Thy Springs are dry,
Can from their flinty Bowels fetch
Streams, which shall never die.
PSALM. CLI.
According to the LXXII. -- (...). etc.
I.
YOUNGEST of all my Brethren, and the least
In the Jessean House, to' a Service I inclined,
Which both my Age, and Innocence suited best,
And best the Throne, to which I was designed:
My Fathers Flock, was early set to keep,
And how to govern Men, first learn among my Sheep.
II.
I kept and fed them with a pious care,
And as they fed, my Harp, and Pipe assayd;
Them, and my self to please, did Songs prepare,
And variously, as pensive Shepherd, played:
Till having wearied out an humbler String,
A bolder flight I dare'd, Isreal's Great God to Sing.
III.
But who such Praise can worthily rehearse?
I strove my best, and it acceptance found,
With Heavens Blessed King, who to approve my Verse,
A double Glory round my Temples bound;
By' his Prophets Hand there placed the Regal Crown,
(From the Flock calling me) the Poet's with his own.
IV.
I many Brethren had, and great of Might,
All valiant Men, and all renowned in War,
Often tried, but God in them took no delight,
For Causes, tho from us removed far,
To' himself best known, who sees not as Man sees,
But as the Heart is, judges, and gives Dignities.
V.
For that, and his own Pleasure He chose me,
And having chosen, called me forth to fight
With Raphas Giant-son, whose blasphemy
Levelled at Heaven, on his own Head did light:
By' his Gods he cursed me, and his Gods he cursed,
Himself as the chief God propitiating thus first.
VI.
But such curse Proof, I out against him went,
The firmlier armed, as he disarmed thereby;
And certain Death into his Forehead sent,
E're he the place could guard, or turn to fly:
He fell: I ran, to the lifeless Monster came,
From him with his own Sword took his Head, from Israel shame.
Job cursing his Birth.
After this opened Job his Mouth and cursed his Day: And Job spoke and said.
Pereat dies in qua natus sum.
MAY the Day perish, and its hastly flight
Be still, be still retarded by a sluggish Night!
The Day, unhappy Day, whereon 'twas known,
My Mother could that name, and sorrows own.
Let it look black as Hell, no Ray appear,
Nor on it God in common Light draw near!
But unregarded may it from above,
To 'all other Days a different Circle move!
Augment the last Nights gloom, and never be found,
But in a Sea of Rapes, and Murder drowned!
Let Deaths grim Terrors on it ever dwell,
Of if it has Light, let it be such as fell
On Sodom, when avenging Heaven did shower
Tempests of Fire, and floods of Lightning pour!
And for the Night (if yet it were the Night,
For any Day too bad, which first disclosed the sight)
Dark of it self, let Horror on it seize!
And when all others welcome are for the ease,
And respit, which they bring the toilsome care
Of pains, which in their Curtains hidden are,
Let it be Cursed too, and by' a fatal Breath,
Doomed not the shadow of it, but very Death!
Sad, dismal, solitary, know no Joys,
No cheerful shouts, but a dull confused noise
Of Groans and Shrieks, as when the parting Soul
Labors in vain its dest'iny to control!
And as the Criminal, who, to die next Morn,
The pity of the many, and their Scorn,
Curses its shortness, and does think it done,
Sooner than other Nights are well begun;
Let it abide Cursed, and grown Ominous,
Its Tale in some prodigious ruin lose!
Black be its Twilight, in it rise no Star,
But such, as when it is seen, tho from a far,
Famine portends, and Blood, and the Worlds flame,
And all the Plagues that have, or have not Name!
Let it expect the Light, and pine away
To Darkness palpable, but see no Day!
With thousand Curses more --
And Day or Night be' it, Evening, or the Morn,
From the Years account let it be ever torn!
To me, it self, and Heaven and all be lost,
And from the number of the Days be crossed!
O had it never been, or had that Hour
But barred the Gate, and damned the fertile Door
(Unhappy Gate, but Hour unhappy more!)
Sorrow I never had known, nor had these Eyes
Beheld the Light, which none but Fools can prize.
Rather why di'd I not, making the Womb,
At once my busy tyring House and Tomb;
But by the Knees I must perverted be,
And live more Deaths than one to act, more Plagues to see
Draw the hated Breasts only to fetch supply,
After ten thousand Deaths, new deaths to try,
And at the last with greater sense and torment die!
Had I then died, still as the Night, or Grave,
My Voice had been, without a Death to crave.
Still had I lain, and in Oblivion's breast
Enjoyed a sweeter sleep and sounder rest.
The Earth, which does in its cold Lap enfold
All Arts and Arms, Princes and all their Gold,
Which Sepulchers does for their Tombs prepare,
Great in their Dust and in their Ruins fair,
For me, to Die then had I been allowed,
Had marked a place, amidst the awful Crowd,
There where untimely Births in the Pit are thrown,
And through the Earths soft pores the Plains with verdure crown.
An awful place it is, with Company
The best and great'st, where in appartments lie
Kings, and their Counselors, each in his Bed,
With each his Sword clapped underneath his Head.
For there the proud Usurpors terrors cease,
And there the weary are at perfect ease,
And the whole Region riots in the spoils of Peace.
Prisoners enjoy their Liberty, at least know
No other Chains, than what their Jaylors do.
Both small and great there undistinguisht be,
Undisturbed by outworn Authority.
Masters and Servants throw those Names aside,
And for a nobler freedom both provide.
No fear of the Oppressor's there, no wrong,
No Clamours, no Reproach amidst that throng;
But a deep silence fills the profound wast,
Deaf to all calls, but the last Trumpets blast.
Ah, might I rest there! Why is Death denied
To him, who seeks it, in those shades to hide?
Who for it digs, and would more gladly find
That Treasure, than the mines he leaves in the way behind!
Light and this Life, will but increase his pain,
Light and this Life, of which he does complain,
And would for 'one Death exchange, but all in vain.
Why is Life thrust on such a Man, who is dead,
Dead to himself, and God, all comfort fled?
Me why is it thrust on, who the Gift despise,
As the worst of this Worlds great impert'nencies;
Nay more, its greatest Curse; unwelcome Guest,
That never, lets me never be at rest,
Nor Bed, nor Board their just refreshment give;
Which! (who would thus?) thus I Would not always live;
Too long already, to feel what I feared,
Sadder than can be told, too doleful to be heard;
At rest I never was, but compared with this,
All former Grief as gone, and vanished is,
And all, but very Hell, would be a kind of Bliss.
1660.
The Prayer of HABAKKUK.
Hab. 3. Domine, audivi auditionem.
I.
MY God, I have Thy Wonders heard,
And their report like those, who saw them feared,
I heard, what Thou of Old have done,
Revive Thy Work, nor let it die;
But since to make us hope Thou have begun,
Let our Deliverance too draw nigh!
Lord, in the midst of the Years appear,
Nor ever, ever thus forbear,
To put an happy issue to our Fear!
In The midst of the Years Thy Greatness show,
For we are ready if Thou 'art but so!
Let us in Wrath Thy Mercy see,
Remembered this, let that forgotten be!
What tho with us the full Time 's not expired,
With Thee it is ended, and by us desired.
Ages to come, and Ages long since past,
In Heaven, where Thou art, present are,
It Is ever now and now will ever last.
O, Now from Heaven Thy Power declare,
And let it once be here, what it is ever there.
II.
Deus ab Austro veniet.
God came from Teman, and the Holy One,
Descended from Mount Paran, with a mighty Train:
The Earth to Heaven did dart the Rays again,
And as He past the Sky with Glory shone.
Refined Light, without allay,
Such as above makes Angels Day,
Such was His Brightness, and such was His Way.
He was all Light, but from His Side
Shot forth a Beam, so clear, and pure,
That none to see it could endure;
And there, as in the dark, He did His Glories hide.
The Pestilence before Him went,
Gathering new Poisons, as the old were spent;
Ruin and Desolation, at His Feet,
Never to part again did meet,
But sworn to execute His Wrath on Man,
Kissed and embraced each other close, as they before Him ran.
III.
Stetit, and mensus est Terram,
He stood and in His Hand
He held a Line, and measuring Wand,
Both to mete out, and to destroy his Land.
Over the Earth the fatal Line He threw,
And that it level on all sides might lie,
He smote the Nations, and they' in haste withdrew;
The affrighted Earth, that fain would fly,
Seeing it could not stir, the Line did take,
But did with horror, and amazement shake;
The Rocks as it came over their Backs, did quake;
Bowed down their Heads, and grieved they were so high.
The everlasting Mountains scatt'red lay,
And the perpetual Hills sank down, and stole away.
IV.
Pro iniquitate vidi Tentoria.
I saw the Tents of Egypt in distress,
Methoughts I heard their doleful groans;
The Land did tremble, and its emptiness
An hollow murmur added to its moans,
And shriekt a deadly echo from the wounded Stones,
When not content to see their Firstborn slain,
Conquered on Land, they once again
Would try the fortune of the Main.
Since they the Tenth Shock could so stoutly brave,
They scorned to fear the Eleventh Wave,
Till they themselves, and that saw buried in a Grave.
What ailed the Rivers, Lord, what ailed the Flood,
That Thou should make their streams true Veins of Blood?
What could the Sea, against Thee do,
So small against so great a Foe,
Exalted Thou so high, and that so low?
Could it deserve Thy Wrath, or roar so loud,
From Heaven, Thy Throne, to call Thee down;
Or in its swellings was it grown so proud,
It 'sdeign'd a check from a single frown,
Unless in Triumph God would over it ride,
And Seas, from Seas below, as first from those above divide?
V.
Suscitans suscitabis Arcum,
So on the Sea; in the Air his Bow was seen;
Not by Reflection, like the Rainbow made,
Where all the pleasing Colors are together laid,
That Man might be no more afraid
Of a new Deluge to be unsherd in,
And once more drown, what it could never purge, his Sin.
That is his Bow of Peace, but this of War,
The Sky about it was with Darkness spread,
Slaughter, and Gore had stained it red,
Ghastly and terrible it glistned from afar.
A poisoned Arrow on the string did hang,
It hung a while, but when the Bow He drew,
Drawn to the head away it flew,
And flying gave a deadly twang;
The Air a good while after rang.
The sound how loud, the Pile how keen,
How would it enter, when no Mail could come between*
Such was his Word, which did their Way prepare,
The Oath, which to the Tribes He past,
Making them Conquerors every where,
Till they were in Canaan placed,
Till to the Promised Land, He brought them safe at last*
VI.
Semper fluvios scindes T^rrae.
But first the War did rage at Home,
Thirst, a worse Foe than Amaleck, to be overcome.
To God for Drink they cry,
Not with a Wet, but envious Eye;
For Drought long since had made those Cisterns dry.
They cried, and murmured, to' Egypt back would go,
Till from the Rock God bid the Waters flow;
The Rock obeyed, and to the sacred Rod did bow.
Out gusht new Streams, the admiring Earth gave way,
But wondered how such Rivers should come there;
Yet looked again to see her fear,
And as she saw it quak't, and ready cut in Channels lay.
VII.
Viderunt te and doluerunt Montes.
There as He marched, the Mountains saw their God,
And staggered as he shook His Rod;
The surly Deep past silent by,
And fearful any more to look on high,
In humble plains of liquid Crystal flat did lie.
The bolder Waves, which yet would rise,
And with their towering Billows dare the Skies,
Seeing Him, started and shriekt out;
No more of their Defiance thought,
And of His Presence were ashamed to doubt.
As when one sees some Ruin near,
Ready upon his Head to fall,
Which yet he cannot help at all,
Cannot prevent; but with unequal strength must bear;
Expecting the dead weight he stands,
Shrinks in his Shoulders, and lifts up his Hands:
So stood the Waves, and without power to fly,
With raised up Hands and Eyes, had hardly strength to cry.
VIII.
Sol and Luna steterunt.
The Sun amazed stood still, and at the sight,
Bid the Moon stop, and see the bloody Fight;
Never was such a Fight, never so long a Day,
When Heaven it self did waiting stay,
Nor till it saw the Victors went away.
The Lord Himself that Day marched out,
Hailstones and Coals of Fire hurled all about;
In wrath He marched, through the whole Land,
And thresht the Nations, as He past along,
His Arm so weighty, and his Wrath so strong,
None durst against Him stand.
To save his People did He thus appear,
To them so Glorious, to His Foes so full of Fear.
IX.
Percussisti Caput de domo, etc.
To the Earth He stroke their Princes down,
Their Villages destroyed, sacked every Town;
Tho like a Whirlwind, they against us came,
God for us fought in arms of Flame.
Flames, which their blasts made fiercer burn,
And on themselves with double Vengeance turn;
In Fire God came against them, and o'recame:
Did through the Sea on His great Horses ride,
Whilst Waves to make Him room, stood up in heaps, on either side.
X.
Audivi and contu^batus est, etc.
This as I heard my Joints unloos'd,
Through all my Veins chill Horror was diffused.
My Belly trembled, and my Lips did quake,
My Bones for very rottenness did shake.
Afraid I was, yet could not choose but fear,
When I such mighty things did here,
When e're I was aware,
The God, who did them I perceived drew near.
O may I rest, when he to judge shall rise!
For when He does the wicked World chastise,
How heavy then will be His Hand, how red his Eyes!
XI.
Ascendam ad Populum.
From Thee, Lord, then, to my God now I fly,
And for Thy Mercy, on Thy Power rely,
Propitious Thee thy Land has found, and so shall I.
Nothing shall make me quit my trust,
For Thou art Pitiful, as well as Just.
No, tho the Fig Tree blossom not,
And on the Vine the generous clusters rot;
Tho the labors of the Olive cease,
And all the lesser Plants of Life,
With Man, as He is with his God at strife,
Deny to give their rich increase.
Let the Earth threat Famine, bear no Grass,
Iron below, as Heaven above, is Brass.
No Fruit, no Pasture yield,
But be with Thorns and Brambles filled,
And they burnt up, whilst there is a Furnace in each Field;
Let the Flocks die, and in the Stall
The Ox, not by the Knife, but want of Fodder fall;
Yet in my God will I rejoice,
Whose care I am, as I made him my choice.
It Is He 's my strength, and freed from fear,
For me on high He shall His Truth display;
Or when the Desolation's near,
Give me Hinds Feet to escape away. 1663.

Comiato.
Song wherewith I first Begun,
My Great Redeemers Praise to sing;
And from a far more noble string,
(Than I was wont) an Hebrew Descant run,
For the great Harp of Jesses Son,
To be prepared, when Time should be:
Preserve that Times blessed Memory,
And all that, by Thee' inspired, I since have done,
That if with Men no Grace I find,
With Heaven I may, and Peace in my own Mind!

The Saying of CLEANTHES.

DVC me Parens, Sen. Ep. 107.
Celsique Dominator Poli
Quocunque placuit, nulla parendi mora est,
Adsum impiger; fac nolle, comitabor gemens,
Malusque patiar, quod pati licuit bono.

I.
LEAD me, O Providence Divine,
Where e're Thou have appointed me to go;
I Will follow willingly, and show,
By my quick pace, that one design,
Tho hid to me, acts Thy unerring Will and mine.
II.
Briskly I Will follow Thee; for so
I shall prevent my Fate, which to decline
Beyond my Compass is, and Line;
Worse by resistance I shall grow,
And after all be driven, whether I will or no.
Upon a terrible Storm of Thunder, Wind, and Rain, 25 July 1670, done by Night
in the midst of it.
I.
GREAT God of Thunder, at whose Voice
The Earth and its Foundations shake,
And Man, whom Thou have made its Lord, does quake,
Still the dreadful, and amazing Noise!
Lo! as Thy People Israel did of old,
By Fear surpised, yet by our fear made bold,
Lord, lest we die, we beg Thou would Thy Voice withhold!
II.
Yet speak, for (Lo!) Thy Servants hear!
And speak Thy self, but not in Smoke, and Flame!
The mighty Storm, that by the Tishbite came,
And rent the Hills, and did the Mountains tear,
The Tishbite saw unmoved, knowing Thou were not there.
At length was heard an awful sound,
Whispers and murmers undistinct around,
With silence waited on profound,
And a soft Voice, in which the Thunders shouts were drowned.
The Prophet listened, and inclined his Head,
Filled with sacred and unusual Dread;
His Face did in his Mantle hide;
For Thou in triumph on the peaceful sound did ride,
And He, who braved the Thunder, bowed and worshipped
III.
With such another Voice Divine,
Lord, speak to us, and we will hear!
Thy Thunder is too loud for our purged Ear,
And dreadfully Thy scorching Lightnings shine.
That voice of Fire, till the Great Day restrain,
Where to be slept out 't shall be strove in vain;
For, even the Dead by it awaked, shall rise again.
The NATIVITY.
An Ode.
I.
WHO would not envy, if he durst, your Grace,
Blessed Shepherds, to whom first the Tidings came,
That God, who neither Time can bound, nor Space,
The Almighty, who upholds this rolling Frame,
Deigned to be born, and did the Breast embrace:
Of the Worlds Maker made himself a Child,
And wrapped in swaths, tho He whole Nature filled?
Too happy News this, in the City to be told,
In The Palace, and at Herod's Court,
Where all the learned Father Jews resort;
E're it reach them, let the report grow cold!
There Is too much Spleen, and Malice there,
Hypocrisy, Distrust, and servile Fear,
Intemp'rance, Lust, Extortion, Cruelty,
And, if than these there greater Vices be,
Pride, which of ills the worst, pollutes the Air.
The Country 'a better place, God for his Birth did choose,
(Tho not so gay,) and Men more Innocent,
To whom He Would show His great Descent:
And when He did Jerusalem refuse,
'Twas to recall the Ancient Time, and Use,
When He to Man in Paradise first went:
That He to Peace, and Justice, might the preference give,
And all the Graces that in the Country safely live:
And lest the Truth should be denied,
In The Country He Would be Born, but in the City Died.
II.
"Fear not, O Shepherds, the Angel said!
And need there was, to bid them not to fear,
Since greater Souls than theirs might be afraid,
That God, unlookt for, should approach so near,
And full Spring-tides of Light, at Midnights Ebb appear.
For Night it was, and posting toward the Day,
Grown darker on a sudden, than before,
The Shepherds by their Flocks expecting lay,
Till their bright Star should ope' the Mornings Door;
When, Lo! a brighter Star brake out,
And sacred Beams Angelic Forms did show,
Fairer than thousand Suns, tho all about
They their united Flames should throw.
'Twas Gabriel, who the Message brought
To the 'ever Virgin that she should conceive;
Gabriel, who now the happy Minute taught,
When his Great Lord the Father's Throne did leave,
A Body fitted for him to receive;
Bright in His Princes glory, bright in 'His own,
All clad in Hallowed Light came down,
Ambassador of God, and Herald to His Son.
III.
On Heavens high Top awhile He stood,
And viewed all Palestine around;
All Palestine in sleep lay drowned,
Eve'n Jordan slumbred to the murmurs of his Flood.
Nor voice of Man, nor noise of Dogs was heard,
The bowing Mountains seemed to nod,
And at the Presence of their God,
Who over them waved his All-commanding Rod,
Inclined their Heads, and the great Spell revered.
Each Field, each Hill did rest,
And equal Night possessed
The painful Labourer, and his weary Beast.
Bethle'hem alone, this Transient Death survived,
And in her Plains some lived,
Yet whom the Sight did so surprise,
They hardly durst believe their Eyes,
Yet durst not but believe, and sent to Heaven their cries.
IV.
So at the last Day shall there some be found
To hear, alive, the Trumpets dreadful sound;
Amazed and trembling shall they stand,
Feel on themselves a Powerful Hand,
And willing, or unwilling take, the great Command.
"Be changed ('twill say) ye Living! and ye Dead
"Wake and arise, and to the Judgment come!
The Living, soon as e're the word is sed,
Shall feel the Terrors they did dread,
And without help of Death, or Grave, reach their Eternal Home.
The Dead with their own Bodies shall arise,
And then the Earth, and Sea, and Skies,
The scattered Atoms shall restore
Of Bodies, which they did devour,
To join with parted Souls, but never to be parted more.
No guilty Criminal his Face shall hide,
Or undiscovered the great Judge avoid,
But from his Hold, tho self-condemned, come and be tried.
V.
Nature it self shall to 'its old nothing roll;
And then Heavens beauteous Scroll,
With all the Mistic Notes that there
(Writ by the Hand Divine)
So wondrous and, and so bright appear,
Shall in one flame with Earth and Sea, more dreadful shine.
Like that, which once the Prophet sent
To Judahs stubborn King; Jer. 36.

A while he heard the Woes, but grown impatient,
With Hands profane the Parchment rent,
And into the Fire the severed parts did sling;
And there they crackled, there did together shrink,
Till all-consum'd they were to Ashes burned.
Ah! sottish Prince, and vain to think
Decrees of Heaven so easily overturned!
Lo! God himself resents the wrong,
Thy self shall be the subject of a longer Roll ere long!
VI.
And so he was, for by the dread Command,
A larger Roll the Prophet did prepare.
By the same Command another Heaven more fair,
In place of this, when it is consumed shall stand.
And there the Thrones shall be for Judgment set,
From awful which the Son with Majesty,
And doubled Grace shall Reign illustriously,
In his own Godhead, and the Exalted Manhood, great.
All Nations then, and Languages,
The Rich, the Poor, the Simple, and the Wise,
With all who Tents inhabited, or Palaces,
Naked to the Bar shall rise,
And answer each Man to his name:
Distinctions shall aside be thrown,
Nor Kings be by their Scepters known,
Or the great Houses which they made, or whence they came.
Around the Bar shall Angels wait,
For Execution armed, and clothed for state,
Sole ministers of Wrath, who only were of Love alate.
VII.
Thither, O Muse, thither bring back my Song,
From which thou wandered have too long;
Of Gabriel, and his second Message sing,
Who now upon the Wing,
The happiest News, e're heard by mortal ears, does bring.
"Attend, he said, and those glad Tidings hear,
"Good Tidings, and of great Joy, which shall be
"Not unto you alone,
"Or from the Father to the Son,
"For one descent alone continued down,
"But unto all who are to come, or wished this day to see,
"For Lo! to you is Born this Day,
"A Savior, which is Christ the Lord,
"In David's Town, which long expecting lay,
"Promised Messiah, and the Incarnate Word.
"To Day He ^s Born, and this shall be the Sign,
"By which the Mighty Infant shall be found,
"In Swaddling Clothes he shall lie bound,
"And in a Manger rests the Babe Divine.
Never was Sight of equal Fame,
Not the first Man of God-like Frame,
For God himself thus Born, a Mortal Man became.
VIII.
He spoke, and with him strait were seen
Myriads of Angels, in their best Array,
Myriads of Angels, who kept Holy Day,
And all their Glories did display,
No Cloud to 'eclipse the Luster came between.
And then they Danced, and then they Sang
Praises, themselves did first compose;
The Starry Vault, with the loud Echo rang,
And the whole Concert doubled over this close;
"Glory to God on High,
"In The highest great Jehovah bless,
"Good Will toward Men, on Earth be Peace,
"Glory to God on High!
And may this Round begun thus last Eternally!
And up they rose, with Bays and Ivy Crowned,
Not such as Mortal Poets wear below,
But what in the Heavenly Temp^e grow,
And with whose Wreaths the first great Makers Brows are bound.
IX.
Go! Shepherds, go! and kiss the Eternal Son!
To Bethle'hem go, and the first Tributes bring
To Isreal's Savior, and Heavens Newborn King!
To you this more than common Honor's done,
To' approach your God, and Worship at his humble Throne.
Make haste, nor by your own delay
For others to prevent your Joys give way;
Why should they first be happy, whilst you only stay?
For Kings shall come e're long, from the East,
By a less Flame than what is your Guide,
Directed hi'ther, to find that Rest,
Which seems not theirs, till by 'you accepted, or denied.
Of you God takes the first, and greatest Care,
Who thus by Angels Summoned are;
When they, tho Kings, and coming from afar,
Shall wait, and both to call, and lead them, only have a Star,
14 Jan. 166^.
The STAR.
A Carol For the Epiphany by the III Kings.
SEe how that Glorious Star, 1. King.
at Noon does rise,
And like another Sun new Guilds the Skies!
Look how it dares the Ruler of the Light, 2. King.

And in His clearest Beams appears more bright,
Calling before its time the sluggish Night!
Rather the Conquered Sun to' its Rays gives way, 3. King.

And but a Phospher seems to its new Day.
The Conquered Sun, etc.
Chorus trium. Sure it is no common Star, 2. King.
see where it goes
A daring Passage it self only knows;
And cross the Heaven points out to Palestine, 1. King.

And as it that way leads more bright does shine:
Come let us follow, where it leads, and see
What may the Cause of its appearing be, 3. King.

Whether it set a Star, or some Divinity.
Come let us, etc.
Chorus alter trium. Some greater Power, 3. King.
which to direct our Way,
Has chose this borrowed Shape, and glorious Ray;
And when we knew not well which Road to go,
Does toward Judea our great Journey show: 1. King.

That way it points, 2. King.
that way we must along,
No fear, when Heaven's out Guide, we should go wrong.
That way, etc.
Chorus trium tertius. Over Bethle'm lo! 1. King.
at length the Flame does rest!
Bethle'm, that with the Prince of Peace is blessed:
Bethle'm, 2. King.
which must by ancient Prophecy
The Tyring-House of the Almighty be,
Where he will cloth Himself with base Humanity.
And that is the House, 3. King.
where we our Gifts must bring
To the World's God, and Israel's Infant King.
Chorus Omnium. Hither 'twas, hither the bright Star did lead,
Let Us enter humbly, and approach with Fear:
The Star, which brought, will show him us more clear,
And be a Glory round the Infants Head:
O this is He! fall down, and worship him! fall down
And kiss his Feet, whose Head even Heaven thus stoops to Crown!
23. December. 1660.
The PASSION.
An Ode.
I.
TWice sixteen Years have almost over Thee past,
Twice sixteen more Thou may as fondly waste,
In expectation, Sylvius, as thou have
The swift-wing'd Years, which in their Passage escaped thee last.
The Calendar is searched, and all in vain
Would Thou have this Day return
To the same Point, as when in 'it Thou were Born,
But 'twill not be this Age, 15. Apr. 1636.
if it e're come again.
Enough it is that Thou once did see
The great Conjunction:
Wait not over long, for what may be
Too late for Thee,
And is sufficient of it self alone,
Without that Circumstance to fill Thy Song.
For grant it now what could to Thee be' applied
But that thy Birth fell out the Night thy Savior Di'd.
II.
Rise then my Muse, but from a nobler Ground,
And sing in Numbers mournful as the Day,
Of Natures fright, and disarray,
Which did Philosophy confound,
And scattered dismal Horrors all around.
When Heaven, and Earth, and Hell partook
In the Darkness, and the Night,
Which like a Sea o'reflow'd the plains of Light,
And all Spectators with amazement struck!
Unlike to that, which once in Egypt reigned,
When solid Night did Rhamases invest,
But Goshen, of the Sun possessed,
Over the Gleam a Prospect gained,
And uninvellopt saw how far the Heavens were stained.
Nor was it to the Antipodes
The Day had hastened his access;
For they unsensible of Light,
Lay buried all the while in Night,
And without Miracle could not behold it bright.
Unless Thou add'st the Prodigy to raise,
(And which none else but Thou, O Muse, dares say)
The Antipodes at Midnight rose to gaze,
And Night Jerusalem less admired, than they the Day.
III.
A thought too wild this, and extravagant,
And which does all but its own airy basis want:
Say rather that the Pangs and Agonies
Of a new, and better World,
Which was thence to take its rise,
Were thus concealed from Mortal Eyes,
And Darkness, as at first, over all the Expansion hurld.
God's sacred Kingdom was that Birth,
The same New Heaven, and new Earth,
Which the beloved Disciple saw,
In all its Beauties, as it did appear,
And to provoke Adventurers there,
A Chart thereof by Vision did exactly draw.
For on the Cross as our great Savior hung,
And just Expiring, bowed his Conquering Head,
From the black Skies bright beams like Lightning sprung
But as the Day, continued long,
Chasing winged Darkness, which before them fled.
And as the first Creations Work begun
By the commanding Word, which He
To Nothing, and to Chaos sed,
Making when He spoke only, "Let there be,
By a no less Word this too was done,
Created by that Voice, which cried, "It Is Finished.
IV.
It Is Finished the Mighty Victor cried,
All reaking in Triumphal Gore,
Which his own Wounds, not Enemies Necks supplied;
For tho with them He Skirmisht had before,
And often rebated had their Power,
He could not thoroughly for us Conquer, till He Di'd.
Alone He did the Wine Press tread,
Of his Just Father's Wrath, alone,
Isreal's to raise, stoopt his own Head,
And to assist Him was there none.
So far from that, that in the pursuit
Of Satan, Sin, and Death, when He cried out,
With fainting Groans, I Thirst,
His Patience some, and some his Conquest Cursed,
And Gall and Vinacre of the bitter Tree, was all the Fruit.
Till having tasted of the Brook in the way,
Anew He followed, till He gained the Day;
And to complete his Victory,
Got thence more Aids, and strength enough to Die.
V.
Blessed Savior, who but Thou could Live so long,
And in one Soul so many Deaths endure,
And different all, and all their Pains so strong,
That their rehearsal does fresh Griefs ensure,
And again pierce those Hearts Thou bledst to Cure?
When in the Garden Thou did first begin,
Gethsemane, for ease designed,
And safe retirements of a troubled Mind,
Purging thence all the effects of Sin,
Which still, tho hid, remaind behind,
The dregs of what on Man in Paradise brake in.
Fatal, but happy Place that, where did grow
Midst whole Woods, no less beauteous, but one Tree,
That even, by Wilfulness alone, could be
The occasion of our Misery;
But in all else, more secret Snares than we
Till by them Caught, shall ever know!
From this to clear it, and restore
To the Garden, what it had before,
And perfect Innocence add, one Beauty more;
As there fallen Man his Life first forfeited,
There, to Redeem him, first the Blood of God was shed.
VI.
How grievous were his Pains there, and how great?
Burning, tho in the frosty shades of Night:
Shivering with Cold, but in a Bloody Sweat;
And all dissolved, at his approaching Passions Sight?
Thrice did He his Disciples leave,
And thrice to his Great Father prayed,
Thrice to himself He answer made,
And by an Angel did support receive;
But, (O!) the Assaults that were within,
Compared with which his Bodies Flame,
Was temperate heat, and scarce deserved the Name,
When in his Soul the Burning did begin,
And Hell to 'encrease the Fire, did Mines of Brimstone bring!
A thousand Fiends about him flew,
And Coals, and bailful Firebrand; threw,
That seized at length the noblest Part,
Beyond the weak defense of Nature, or of Art,
And unconsum'd, did only leave the Heart.
VII.
The Heart did unconsum'd remain,
By the Arch-Fiend
With its own Grief to burst designed,
When in the Judgment Hall again,
He should the Charge renew, but all in vain.
Thither betrayed by 'a Kiss the Traitors bring,
With Fetters bound, Heavens Sacred King;
Where being Cited, and Blasphemed,
Flouted, Scourged, Spat upon,
Derided, and Contemned,
By them Reviled, denied by 'His own,
A Reed in 'his Hand, his Head with Thorns they Crown,
And lead to Golgotha their God, whom they 'had Condemned*
VIII.
Follow, Muse, if thou have the heart, and see
What other Torments they prepare;
I know the utmost of their Cruelty,
And from thy Mouth had rather hear,
The sad Report, than a Spectator be.
Yet, that thou may not stand thy self surpised,
Stripped off his Clothes, in Nakedness disguised,
To the Cross they 'll nail his Hands, it is said,
And bore with Nalls his tender Feet;
Then, all his Sufferings to upbraid,
"Cry, If Thou art the Son of God, let us see it,
"Now from the Tree triumphantly come down,
"Or reign thence, like Thy self alone,
"Or any other Wonder show,
"Whereby Thy De'ity may be known,
"And to its Scepter we will bow.
As if there greater Miracle could be,
Than all that Patience, which they do, but will not see.
IX.
Nor is this all, but when He Is Dead,
His Side they 'll open with a Spear;
Approach the Wound, and look what Blood is shed,
For it Mysterious will appear,
And be another Argument for thee next Year!
A better Spring will thence arise,
Than Helicon, so Famed of old,
There bath thy self, if thou art wise,
Nor fear in those chaste Streams to be too bold.
But see, be sure too long thou do not stay,
For all the while Thou art away,
Tears only from these Eyes will flow,
And in my Fancy I shall double over
All that I have told thee now before,
And all that thou returned will'st tell again, and more,
Beside my Verse will fettered be, and slow,
And want both Wings to fly, and Feet to go.
10. Martii 166^.
EPIGRAM. Out of Latin.

WHen my God Di'd, I first began to Live,
And Life which he refused Heaven me did give
Unlike that Day, O how unlike we were!
Him dead the Cross, me 'alive the Knees did bear.
But may not I die too? This life of mine
I can as well as Thou despise, if not like Thine.
Ah dearest Lord, this Legacy bestow,
A double Life, then to Thy Death I Will owe:
And sanctified thus in my Birth by Thee,
A living Death, my dying Life shall be.
5. December. 1668.
An Ecstasy of Divine Love.
Out of Spanish. S. Teresa.
Aquesta Divina Union, etc.
I.
THat sacred Bond of Charity,
Wherein I uncorrupted Live,
Makes God the Captive Chain receive,
But my pinioned Heart sets free,
Tho causing still such love in me,
To see Heavens King my Prisoner lie,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
II.
How teadious now this Life is grown,
The way to Death how hard and long;
How dark the Dunge'on the Ir'ns how strong,
With which my' unwilling Soul's kept down,
And has no trust but hope alone!
These thoughts my Troubles raise so high,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
III.
Bitter Life, shall thou be to me,
Where I my God can never enjoy;
But if my Love has no alloy,
My hope as tried and pure may be;
Ah! come my Lord, and set me free!
Take off this weight, which makes me cry,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
IV.
By hope alone it is I Live,
Hope that I bear the seeds of Death,
And dying once, a second Birth
Secures that Hope, and Life does give;
O Death, I Will never thy coming grieve,
When Life succeeds, through hope so nigh,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
V.
Who can the Charms of Love refuse?
Ah Life, no more my Heart betray,
It Is only thou stand'st in my way,
Which rather than my Love, I Will loose,
And Death for my great Champion choose;
So much alate thy Enemy,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
VI.
The Life alone, that is hid above,
Can of true Life the Title claim;
That Toy, which here usurps the Name,
Its pleasure hides, and deads our Love,
And a worse Foe than Death does prove;
Death, for whose sake I Life so fly.
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
VII.
What can I give, frail Life, but thee
To the God, who in me deigns to live?
Yet how can I the nothing give
Till he first grants me Liberty?
O let me die his Face to see!
But that is so distant from my Eye,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
VIII.
Beside, my God, from thee away,
Who would not of a Life complain,
That terrible, and full of pain,
Suffers a thousand Deaths each Day,
A Mortal, but a slow decay?
And this so swells my Misery,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
IX.
All Creatures love their Element,
And pleasure there enjoy and rest;
And if by Death they are disseas'd
To their first nothing they are sent:
But I Am beyond kind Death's extent,
And yet so many hardships try,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
X.
When in the Eucharist my dull Soul,
Eating thy Flesh it self would ease,
A thousand thoughts for entrance press,
And there not to enjoy Thee whole,
Whole, and alone, I a 'new condole;
For it is the Voice of every Sigh,
That I die, cause I cannot die.
XI.
I please my self in the Hopes, it is true,
E're long, my God, of seeing Thee;
But fearing lest they false should be,
My Torments with my Fears renew,
And both so close my Soul pursue,
Hoping mid both so heartily,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
XII.
Lord, from this Death deliver me,
And Life thus begged at length bestow!
Why should I still be kept below?
Look how I die for love of Thee!
And since enjoyed Thou can not be
In this Life's death, regard my cry,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
XII.
My dying Life I Will then lament,
And living Death in Tears bewail;
For my Sins sake those Foes prevail,
And all my Age in Mourning's spent:
To my release at length consent,
Nor let me grieve eternally,
That I die, 'cause I cannot die.
L'Envoy.
Blessed Soul! that hither could arrive,
How do I love yet envy Thee,
Wishing my self this Ecstasy,
And that the Example Thou do give,
Would make me less afraid to live;
And to each close of Thine reply!
That I die, cause I cannot die!
20. May, 1668.
The FLIGHT.
I.
NO wonder, Soul, thou so admir'st a Verse,
And countst thy self in its Possession brave;
For it is, what e're thou can desire to have,
On this side Heaven, but more to make, than to rehearse.
II.
It Is the end of Preaching, Loves best Exercise,
The Quintessence of Prayer, Praises refined;
A Change exstatic into the Heavenly Mind,
And on whose soaring Wings above the World I rise.
III.
O, could I always stay where 't first sets me!
How naked looking down would the World appear!
Its Joys how empty, and how vain its Fear;
Another flight would make me leave Mortality.
IV.
For as the sealed Dove so high does tower;
That in the pure Air at last it flying dies;
So should I mount too, and above the Skies,
Rapt to the Etern aboads unfeel my dying Hour.
V.
But I must live still, and my flight to bound,
Till truly sealed, there something, Lord, will be;
Some Work of Thine be' it, be it but a Tree,
Eve'n there I nearer Heaven shall rest, than on the Ground.
22. Jan. 1671/2. noctu. Exsurrexi and adhuc sum tecum
An Hymn and Prayer, To the Holy JESUS my Lord. The hint and manner of stanza
taken from the last Canzoue of Fr. Petrarc. Lib. 11. Virgin ^ella.

Parode.
I.
JESU, the Eternal Sun of Righteousness,
Unlike our Mortal Suns, which Rise, and Set,
Subliming this, and the other World with Light,
Love bids me of Thy wondrous Power to Treat,
But how Thy Power, or Wonders to express,
I know not, till Thou make my Darkness bright,
And with Thy Beams dispel the shades of Night:
Therefore I beg Thy aid,
JESU, to whom IN have prayed,
And still pray, that I worthy Thee may write;
Illustriously over all the Expansion shine,
And if I 'm weak to endure
A Light so pure, dart through my Verse a ray Divine.
II.
JESU, the Wisdom of the Deity,
In whom the Mistic Treasures are concealed,
Be'yond Reasons search, of the Eternal Mind,
And with whose Stripes the Afflicted World is healed;
Proof against Death, the Vanguishts Victory,
Under whose Standart to its God rejoined,
Love for the Noblest Service is designed,
Love that is a Rebel now,
JESU, so Great that Thou
From Heaven Thy self must come his Slaves to 'unbind:
That I some Trophies of Thy Power may boast,
When Thou do Conquering ride,
I Will Crowns provide, and sing Thy Triumphs through his Coast.
III.
JESU, the Virgins, and the Martyrs Wrath,
Who without Spot, or Wrinkle did adorn
The fairest Soul, which in a Body all,
Of Charms was wondrously Conceived, and Born;
Fought'st, and wast fought, resign'dst thy laboring Breath,
The Lost to Save, and from the Dungeon call
Hopes weary Prisoners, and Death's Captive thrall,
To Thrones, at Thy Right Hand,
JESU, as Thou do stand
At Thy Great Father's, in Heavens Judgment Hall;
Grant me the Grace, and Thou the Grace can grant,
That when Thou shall come next,
Tho now perplexed, to attend, Thee then I nothing want.
IV.
JESU, in whom the Godhead does repose,
Infinites Comprehension, and the Bound
Of boundless Majesty, fathomless Deep,
With Thorns first pierced, and e're with Glory Crowned,
Submitted to the Triumphs of Thy Foes,
A Man of Sorrows, and inured to weep;
Substantial God, and Man, who both do keep
Unmixt, and Unconfus'd,
JESU, the Untoucht, and Bruised,
Quickening the Dead, yet who in the Grave did sleep;
It Is Thou have broke our Bands, the Uusurper hurld
Headlong into the Pit,
In whose sides sit the great Disturbers of the World.
V.
JESU, the Way, the Truth, whose Life does give
The' exactest Method how we may direct
Our wandering Course to Thy Divine Abode,
And whence, seduced to stray, is not to Live;
A Way Thy self, while here Unerring trod,
And now Exalted do with Blessings load;
A Way, which rough at first,
JESU, appears and Cursed,
But entered once proves worthy Thee and God;
Show me Thy Way, nor take it in ill part,
Since I am blind and weak,
If I bespeak Thee' in that, whose Guide and End Thou art.
VI.
JESU, Whose Cross the surest Anchor makes,
Both strong and sure, entering within the Vail,
Where Passions waves, how fierce and uncontrolled
So e're, rage not, (and yet they dare assail
The holiest Place, and Heaven in the Tempest shakes)
Thou see how there I have fixed all my hold,
And am in the midst of Storms and Floods grown bold:
Yet still there is a Shelf,
JESU, I mean my Self,
Against which I am in danger to be rolled.
I sink, O now thy saving Hand forth stretch!
Now e're my Head with the Weeds,
Which this Sea breeds, is wrapped, and I Am below Thy reach!
VII.
JESU, how many Tears have I in vain,
How many Sighs, and Prayers in vain poured out,
Tho by the expense my Flame alone's encreast!
My Life from its first Stage, traced all about,
Unchanged by change of Habit, 's naught but Pain,
Anguish and Torment, void of Peace and Rest;
Nay even my Soul Heaven-born, has been oppressed,
And humbled to the Grave:
JESU, make have to save,
Nor tarry, tho of Men I 'have loved Thee least!
Now help, to Morrow may not be so well,
For Misery and Sin.
Have me 'on the Wing, and where they will pitch me who can tell?
VIII.
JESU, one half of me's already gone,
So gone, that tho I have piec'd up the Rent,
Methinks I Am not the perfect thing I was,
Tho happy still in that I am content,
And who shall be more perfect when the World's done,
And One made Three into' One again shall pass:
Unhappy Man, unhappy were my case,
Such doubts gave'st Thou not skill,
JESU, to Reconcile,
Thou who see Past and Future in one Glass!
Dear Lord, for whom too hard there nothing is,
Give all my Griefs such end,
As may intend Thy Honor first, and then my Bliss.
IX.
JESU, my Confidence, my Hope, my Fear,
Able to help in time of Need, and Free,
O leave me not, when my last Hour shall come,
Not for my sake, but His, who Ransomed me!
Merit cries, No. But the Image which I bear
Pleads Lord, and would for Ancient Love make room;
Tho I have been betrayed by Errors doom,
To a Fairy Wood, and Brook,
JESU, my Shepherd, look,
Find Thy lost Sheep, and bring the Stragler home!
Of Streams so troubled, may I drink no more,
Or having found my Way,
Any more stray, or wander as I did before!
X.
JESU, My God, who far off see the Proud,
And hate him, let Thy Blood some pity move,
An Heart all bruised, and contrite to regard,
That does at length bewail its sottish love;
And which, if once it can get free, has vowed,
'Twill that and all things else for Thee discard,
'Twill more than ever eye the adored Reward,
And purged with sacred Flame,
JESU, to Thy great Name,
No cost of Wit, or Verse being basely spared,
Its Life, and Hymns, and Service dedicate,
To Thee own its changed desires,
Kiss the soft Fires, and blame it self that 'twas so late.
XI.
JESU, to whom that Name, as the Prize is given,
Of all Thou did and sufferedst without measure,
The mighty Dowry, which Thou broughtst Thy Wife,
Deaths plague, Hells spoil, but whose exstatic Pleasure
Is for the Marriage Feast reserved, in Heaven,
Whither I haste, to be than hope more rife,
And plentiful; (Lo!) the short Day of Life
Posts swiftly on, and flies;
JESU, in my Agonies,
Tho now with Death and Hell I am at strife,
Let me experience this Names healthful ease,
And after a long War,
With Truces rare, in 'it Conquer, and depart in Peace.
Comiato.
Thus, tho with different heat of Spirit, and Verse,
What Petrarch sang to another,
JESU, Thy Virgin Mother,
The stops in part changed, I to Thee rehearse:
I changed the stops, (for under Thy Broad Seal,
Thou give us no Commission,
Her to Petition) and from her to Thee appeal.
Altro Comiato.
And now be pleased to accept this humble Praise,
Other, and better Fruit,
JESU, of all my Suit,
I dare not beg; eve'n Pardon here is Bays.
And for the Prayers, dropped more from my Heart than Pen,
Since their least part's the Rhyme,
And words, which chime, say, as I to them say, AMEN.
I Will Terzo.
When I sent it to Sir Kingsmil Lucy.
At London, Song, Thy Russet, and Thy Freeze,
Will seem, I fear, but course;
Jesu, they will cry, or worse,
See! the dull fleghm of Solitude and Trees.
Thus will the Fops treat Thee, those Wits by Rote;
Yet one Thou 'lt know, e're long,
Who will own Thee, Song, and see Thy Beauties through Thy Coat.
An HYMN for Vespers.
(...) Out of Greek.

JESU CHRIST, blessed Light of Light,
The Immortal Fathers cheerful shine,
Ray of Glory, all Divine,
Equally with Him fair, and bright;
When we see the Day decline,
Calling the sluggish Evening on,
We praise the Father and the Son,
And in our Lauds the Spirit join,
"Worthy art Thou, O God, we say,
"Worthy, O Son of God, art Thou,
"And Thou, of Life the Lord and Giver,
"Worthy to be Praised for ever,
"Great THREE and ONE to Thee we bow,
"And with the whole World thus Crown each Day!
LENT.
Sestina al' Italiana Convertimento a Dio.
I.
WELCOME, great Queen of Fasts, thrice welcome Lent,
With solemn Penance, and Devotion crowned,
Sweet Abstinence, clean thoughts, and chaste desires,
The Wings, whereby the unpinion'd Soul does fly
Above this lower Circle, and exchange
Substantial Cares, for Joys unmixt and pure.
II.
I well remember, when with thoughts less pure,
Nor more to Piety a Friend, than Lent,
Pleased I could well have been never to exchange
My course of Living, no, tho to be Crowned
With Bays Immortal, and Exalted rise,
In hopes as large, as are my changed desires,
III.
But that time's gone, and with it those desires,
Which held me down, and in their stead a pure
Ethereal Flame, which upward still does rise;
Kind thoughts of what is esteemed severe in Lent,
My Soul (the Victim) has for the Alter Crowned
And even burnt up, nor would I 'again exchange.
IV.
This was Thy Work, Lord, Thou 'twas did exchange
What was beyond my power to curb, stubborn desires,
Making them subject to the Head Thou 'hadst Crowned;
And now I Am Victor, with Robes clean, and pure,
(Pure to what once I wore) and this new Lent,
To 'attend the Triumph, rich in Spoils does rise.
V.
And as that rises, such hope I to rise,
When on the Fasts great close I shall exchange
For Easters Sun, the dewy Pearls of Lent;
Bathing, like Oar, with frequent Tears, desires,
Which only such a Flame can render pure,
Fined from all Dross, and worthy to be Crowned.
VI.
Have Thee, blessed Day, wherein with Glory crowned,
The Worlds great Savior from the Grave did rise;
And credence gives His Spouse, that like Him pure,
She shall rise too, and all her Spots exchange,
For Glories, larger than her vast desires,
And the' Pious Resveries of an holy Lent.
Were Lent, and Primitive Institutes thus Crowned
With purgd Desires, and Lives, their Fame would rise,
And none the Church exchange, to be more Pure.
Sent to my Excellent Patron, Sir Nicholas Stuart, Baronet, 1672/3.
The Song of the ANGELS, At the Fall of Lucifer.
I.
SON of the Morning, Firstborn of the Light,
The once bright Phospher of the Day Divine,
How art Thou hurld into eternal Night,
And hid in Flames, who did with Glory shine!
The bold Usurper of the Almighty's Crown,
Proud Lucifer, to Hell is thrown,
And singed the Heavens, as he from thence fell headlong down.
II.
"Above the Heavens, he said, I will ascend
"And there above the Stars exalt my Throne,
"My Conquests in the North I will extend,
"And God a parted Rule shall have, or none:
"Above the heights never reached before I Will fly,
"And equal made with the Most High,
"Or gain an honorable Fall, or Victory.
III.
Scarce said, a pointed Thunderbolt was sent
From the Hand Divine, which pierced him to the heart.
In vain he sought the Vengeance to prevent,
And to Almighty Strength opposed his Art.
But down he sank, and down the Monster fell,
The stroke, nor bore, nor could repel,
And exiled once the Heavens, the next descent was Hell.
IV.
Hell from beneath to meet him raised its Head,
"And now no longer shall we empty be,
"Since Thou art come, the pale Abyssus said,
Look! How we dress our Flames to welcome Thee!
And all with Flames they Circled him around,
With Flames their Princes Temples bound,
An Heaven of Flames they made him, strewed with Flames the Ground.
V.
Rejoice, O Heavens, for your Oppressor's ceast,
And tho e're long there shall from Earth ascend,
An unknown Race, which shall disturb your rest;
Rejoice, and their Triumphant Arms attend,
For when by these you shall invaded be,
With Tears, and Importunity,
Thus to be Conquered is to gain the Victory.
VI.
Son of the Morning, Firstborn of the Light,
The once bright Phospher of the Day Divine,
How art Thou hurld into eternal Night,
And hid in Flames, who did with Glory shine:
The bold Usurper of the Almighty's Crown,
Proud Lucifer to Hell is thrown,
And singed the Heavens, as he from thence fell headlong down.
L'Envoy.
SONG, that long since were finished,
And for another Place designed,
Than what Thou here do find,
In a long Work, and long since promised;
Say that (the Piles Foundations laid)
The 'unwary Builder all his Charge has lost;
For till begun
As a Wise Man should first have done,
He did not, as was fit, sit down;
Thoroughly the Ground had not surveyed
His Friends, or Strength had tried, or reckned up the cost.
Convertimento a Dio.
LORD, my First Fruits should have been brought to thee,
Whose due I am, and all that is mine,
By Birthright Thou a Title have to me,
And by Command those Fruits are Thine;
But Thine, or mine, neither have bin
Offered before to Sin.
Sin came, and first concealed my right in Thee,
And then usurpt what e're was mine,
But let Redemption clear Thy Right to me,
And then both shall again be Thine,
Both Thine and mine, tho they have bin,
Offered before to Sin,
Instead of First Fruits, Lord, I bring to Thee,
The whole Harvest, which yet is not mine,
Thou in return shall give Thy self to me
And make it so, because it is Thine:
Both Thine and mine, as if 't had bin,
Neve'r offered up to Sin.
Deo Opt. Max. S.
Qui huc usque auxiliatus est and in futurum mihi Providebit.
THIS Altar to Thy Name, Great God, I raise,
The Pious Labor of my too late Praise:
With Stones from Thine own sacred Quarry brought,
Tho by my Artless Hand but rudely wrought.
Artless and rude, tho its Traces be,
Methinks I by it clearly see,
My Past Supports, and Future care,
And what of both 'is my present share,
The Guidance of the Love Divine,
Making me call his Pleasure mine.
O let it in Thy Presence stand,
Inviolable from any Hand.
And when on it thou deignst to look,
Write down the Votary in Thy Book.
Who at his Foot, has his Dependence set,
And in the Inscription thus proclaims Thee Great;
To God, who hitherto has helped; my only Trust,
And for the Future will provide, for He is Just.
Ad Psalmos, 1667.
The following Compositions and Translations are therefore here Printed by the
Authors consent and allowance (suitable enough to the general Title of RIMES,
and he hopes not unbecoming his Habit, tho done the most of them long before
his Admission into Holy Orders) because they have (as to such of them at least,
which he could much rather have wished lost and forgotten) by some too curious
Collector of such Trifles against his will and knowledge, been already from
very false Copies very falsely Published, and he is not wholly out of danger for
the rest, as neither was he till now for a good number of those above, whose
